FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Aureoboletus citriniporus  (Halling) Klofac^  Ost. Z. Pilzk. 19: 141. 2010;  Boletus citriniporus  Halling 1977 
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a raw umber to dark brown cap that is dry to moist, and velvety to woolly, 2) whitish unchanging flesh, 3) unchanging lemon yellow pores and tubes, 4) a club-shaped to equal stem that is whitish with orange yellow at the top, the stem surface dry, sometimes with a slight reticulum at the top, 5) growth under oak, and 6) microscopic characters.^ |The description is derived from Halling(7). RANGE ^The holotype was found in CA (originally described in Halling(7)). He examined collections from Amador Co., Nevada Co., and Santa Cruz Co. in CA. It also occurs in the Pacific Northwest (the accompanying M. Beug photo from WA of a sequenced collection. N. Siegel, pers. comm., has collections from Corvallis OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^ammonia on cap cuticle dark vinaceous
CAP  4-7cm across, convex to flat-convex, "becoming irregular in outline"; raw umber to dark brown when young, unchanging or becoming blackish when old; dry, velutinous (velvety) to finely subtomentose, becoming moist and minutely rimose (cracked) when old
FLESH  in cap 0.5-1.0cm thick, solid, firm, whitish to pallid, unchanging when exposed; in stem firm, white, unchanging when exposed
PORES  small, 1-2 per mm, intense lemon yellow, unchanging; tubes 0.5-0.8cm long, "adnate when young, barely subdepressed with age", intense lemon yellow, unchanging when bruised
STEM  5-6cm x 1-1.5cm "subclavate to equal, pinched at the base"; "whitish to pallid, orange yellow at the apex"; dry, bald nearly overall, "but occasionally with a very slight reticulum at the apex"
ODOR  mild
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single to gregarious in soil under Quercus chrysolepis (Canyon Live Oak) and Q. agrifolia (California Live Oak)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (10.5)12-13.5 x 3.75-4.5 um, "subfusiform and inequilateral in profile, often with a conspicuous suprahilar depression", elliptic to fusiform-elliptic in face view, very pale yellowish to colorless in KOH, pale ochraceous in Melzer's reagent^; basidia 2-spored or 4-spored, 22.5-37.5(45) x 9-10.5 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia 37.5-67.5 x 9-12.75 um, "clavate to ventricose rostrate to cylindrical", colorless in KOH, (brightly colored cystidia as indicated by Watling for the genus Aureoboletus were not found); cap cuticle "differentiated as a suberect tangled trichodermium when young, hyphae sometimes appearing subgelatinous with age, apical cells of hyphae not greatly differentiated, but occasionally tapering, rarely incrusted", colorless to ochraceous in KOH, reddish brown in Melzer's reagent, thin-walled, 3-4.5 um wide; stem cuticle "at apex composed of a hymenium of fertile clavate basidia and clavate to obpyriform hyphal tips, below the apex consisting of a turf of clavate to obpyriform hyphal cells, becoming repent and interwoven with age"; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  refers to the intense lemon yellow color of the pores
SIMILAR  Aureoboletus flaviporus is distinguished from B. citriniporus by the presence of a viscid to glutinous cap cuticle and stem, a different anatomy of the cap cuticle, a differently colored cap, and larger spores. The cap of B. citriniporus is "an umber brown that usually becomes blackish" and the stem is "whitish to pallid without any reddish to brown coloration". (Halling(7)). Previous authors have indicated that the stem and cap of Aureoboletus auriporus [described from New York] are cinnamon to light reddish brown, (Halling(7)).
SOURCES  Halling(7) (as Boletus citriniporus)
2
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Aureoboletus flaviporus  (Earle) Klofac^  Ost. Z. Pilzk. 19: 138. 2010;  Boletus flaviporus  Earle  Bull. N.Y. Bot. Gard. 3: 297. 1905
ENGLISH NAME(S)  viscid Boletus
NOTES  Features include viscid, cinnamon-colored to reddish brown cap, intensely yellow pores, pallid stem that is often punctate-roughened, especially when young, and an association with hardwoods.^ | The description is derived from Thiers except where noted. RANGE ^Aureoboletus flaviporus is found CA and OR (Bessette). It has been reported from British Columbia (C. Roberts pers. comm.) but there appear to be no vouchered or sequenced from BC, WA, or OR. The holotype is from NY.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH applied on cap surface slowly orange, slowly pale greenish yellow with FeSO4, flesh pale lavender or negative with application of NH4OH and pale grayish olive with FeSO4, (Bessette), flesh pale gray with FeSO4 (Thiers)
CAP  7-15cm, convex becoming flat-convex to flat to somewhat depressed on disc and flared to highly irregular on margin; reddish brown, when old darkening to dark reddish brown to sometimes dark brown on the disc and fading to brown on the margin (elsewhere in the description cap referred to as cinnamon-colored); viscid to subviscid during all stages, "tomentose or apparently appressed-fibrillose, sometimes appearing glabrous", often reticulate [netted] to irregularly wrinkled, rarely fibrillose-scaly on margin when old^, (Thiers), pale cinnamon to dark reddish brown, sometimes darker brown on disc when old; viscid, subtomentose to bald, (Bessette)
FLESH  1-2cm thick, white to pale pink, unchanging when exposed or slowly becoming pinkish vinaceous^, (Thiers), white to pinkish buff (Bessette)
PORES  1mm or more broad, angular; intense yellow, unchanging or darkening slightly when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, broadly and shallowly depressed, forming a distinct raised yellow network at top of stem, yellow and unchanging when exposed^, (Thiers), about 1mm wide, angular; brilliant yellow at first, becoming greenish yellow when old, (Bessette)
STEM  6-15cm x 1-3(4)cm, equal to club-shaped to narrowing toward the base, solid; yellow at top, becoming white to pallid to nearly colored as cap, base typically white; viscid in wet weather, dry to subviscid otherwise, bald to "punctate-roughened to occasionally fibrillose or fibrillose-scaly, sometimes ridges or obscurely reticulate", conspicuous white mycelium at base^, (Thiers), 7-15 x 1-3cm, nearly equal or widening or narrowing downward; yellowish near top, pale reddish brown on whitish ground color in lower part; viscid when fresh, soon dry, bald or sometimes with shallow longitudinal ribs, lacking reticulation or reticulate only at top, white basal mycelium, (Bessette)
ODOR  mild
TASTE  acid, somewhat unpleasant
EDIBILITY  edible (Bessette), edible but mediocre (Arora)
HABITAT  single to scattered to occasionally cespitose [in tufts] in soil in mixed or hardwood forests, especially with madrone^, (Thiers), under hardwoods, especially oak, madrone, manzanita, or in mixed woods, September to April, (Bessette), fall, winter, spring
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive brown^ (Thiers), olive brown (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-15 x 4-6 um, cylindric to obscurely fusoid [spindle-shaped], smooth, pale ochraceous in KOH, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 23-29 x 8-12 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, 48-70 x 9-15 um, clavate to obscurely fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled, often appearing incrusted at apex; cap cuticle of interwoven gelatinous hyphae 3-4 um wide; stem cuticle a layer of interwoven gelatinous hyphae with scattered areas in which the terminal cells are differentiated as enlarged clavate cells, 40-50 x 15-20 um; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 11-17 x 4-6 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with yellow pores'
SIMILAR  The viscid cap and punctate stem may cause confusion with Suillus species but Aureoboletus flaviporus is not associated with conifers, and has neither glandular dots nor a veil (Arora). Oregon State University has one collection from OR labeled Boletus auriporus Peck, a similar species found in the east, and west at least to Texas. Its pore surface usually stains dull brick red when bruised. See also the SIMILAR section of Boletus citriniporus.
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus), Arora(1)* (as Boletus), Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*
3
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Aureoboletus mirabilis  (Murrill) Halling^  in Halling, Fechner, Nuhn, Osmundson, Soytong, Arora, Binder & Hibbett,  Aust. Syst. Bot. 28: 19. 2015;  Boletus mirabilis  (Murrill) Murrill  Mycologia 4: 217. 1912;  Boletellus mirabilis (Murrill) Singer  Farlowia 2: 129. 1945;  Xerocomus mirabilis  (Murrill) Singer
ENGLISH NAME(S)  admirable bolete, bragger's bolete
NOTES  Features of this common bolete include 1) plush maroon brown cap, 2) stem of similar color, often with roughened longitudinal ridges reminiscent of bark, sometimes netted near the top, 3) yellow pores that do not stain blue, and 4) growth on or near rotting conifers.^ |A whitish then yellow stage of the pyrenomycete Hypomyces chrysospermum frequently grows on it. |The Aureoboletus name was proposed in Halling(6) in 2015 and supported in 2020 by the molecular study of Kuo(12).|"Aureoboletus mirabilis has a sister relationship to Aureoboletus, so one could either consider it an Aureoboletus or give it a different genus name. Since Aureoboletus species are viscid-capped boletes, and the suede-like velvety cap of this species could not be more different, A. mirabilis may be placed in its own genus", (D. Miller, pers. comm.). RANGE ^Aureoboletus mirabilis is common in WA and OR and also found in northern CA, (Thiers), ID (Drew Parker, pers. comm.), MT (Larry Evans, pers. comm.), and MI (Arora). It is also common in BC and there are collections at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  5-15(20)cm, convex to flat, margin often hung with fragments of tissue; dark reddish brown to maroon-brown, bay-brown, or chocolate-brown; moist to dry, granular to plush-like (with small often erect fibrillose scales)^, (Arora), 7-15cm, convex to bluntly convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex, margin often with hanging remnants; dark reddish brown; "moist to lubricous when young, dry when older, somewhat appressed fibrillose-scaly to rough-tomentose when young, becoming conspicuously fibrillose-scaly and often squarrose-scaly with age", sometimes granular-scaly to somewhat areolate [cracked like dried mud] or cracked-scaly, (Thiers)
FLESH  thick; "white to dingy pinkish or yellow, rarely blueing when bruised"^, (Arora), 1-2cm thick, white to yellow, frequently with numerous areas staining flesh-colored when older, rarely changing to blue in irregular areas, in stem pale vinaceous, sometimes appearing yellow and typically becoming yellow when dry, (Thiers)
PORES  1-2 mm wide, pale yellow becoming mustard yellow and finally greenish yellow, sometimes staining darker yellow when bruised; tubes yellow^, (Arora), 1-2 mm wide, round to angular; yellow becoming olive yellow when old, unchanging or becoming deeper yellow when bruised; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick, depressed around stem, colored as pores, unchanging when bruised, (Thiers)
STEM  7-20cm x 1-3.5cm, usually club-shaped, firm; dark brown to maroon-brown or reddish brown, "sometimes with yellow, buff, or beige streaks"; "often roughened, pitted, or longitudinally ridged", the top often coarsely reticulate [netted], the base often with yellow mycelium^, (Arora), 8-15cm x 1-3cm, club-shaped, up to 5cm wide at base, solid; dark brown with occasional streaks of ivory or beige or brown; moist to dry, bald, but typically shaggy reticulate at top, "becoming smooth to uneven or ridged or shallowly pitted toward base, sometimes roughened and split with age", with yellow mycelium at base, (Thiers), partial veil and annulus absent (Bessette)
ODOR  acid or mild^ (Thiers), pleasant (Phillips)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Thiers), pleasant (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  choice (Thiers), delicious, some have a distinct lemony flavor, avoid specimens attacked by Hypomyces, (Arora)
HABITAT  single or in small groups "on or near rotting conifers, (especially hemlock), but sometimes appearing terrestrial"^, (Arora), single to scattered in soil in coastal forests, typically with hemlock logs and stumps, (Thiers), summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Arora, Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 18-24 x 7-9 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 18-22 x 7-9 um, subfusoid to subelliptic, smooth, dark brown in Melzer's, ochraceous in KOH, thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 31-36 x 7-11 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, 60-90 x 10-18 um, fusoid-ventricose with obtuse to elongated apices, colorless, thin-walled; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'wonderful'
SIMILAR  Boletus fibrillosus has a fibrillose brown to dark brown cap, grows on the ground, and has smaller spores, (Bessette).
SOURCES  Arora(1)* (as Boletus), Thiers(1) (as Boletus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus), Lincoff(2)* (as Boletus), Miller(14)* (as Boletus), McKnight(1)* (as Boletus), Ammirati(1)* (as Boletus), Sept(1)* (as Boletus), AroraPocket(1)* (as Boletus), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus), Halling(6), Siegel(2)* (as Aureoboletus mirabilis), Marrone(1)* (as 'Boletus' mirabilis), McBride(1)* (as Boletus mirabilis), Kuo(12), McAdoo(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*
4
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus barrowsii  Thiers and A.H. Sm.^  Mycotaxon 3: 262-264. 1976
ENGLISH NAME(S)  white king bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) whitish to grayish buff smooth cap with suede-like texture, 2) thick unchanging whitish flesh, 3) pores that are whitish when young and do not become blue when bruised, 4) whitish to buff stem that is reticulate at least on the upper part, and 5) mild taste.^ | Trudell(4) comment, "Generally considered to occur only in the Southwest, there have been sporadic reports of it occurring in places such as Idaho and southern California, under trees other than ponderosa pine. In Seattle, a very similar mushroom is fairly common in late spring under oaks and species of Tilia [italicized], such as lindens and basswood. Although it was felt that this had to be a different species, preliminary DNA analysis suggests it is very close to B. barrowsii.". RANGE ^If both close taxa are included, B. barrowsii found at least in ID, CA, AZ, NM, CO, and Mexico, (Bessette). The University of Washington has collections from WA and NM. It was reported from BC (Redhead(5)), and there are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia and the Pacific Forestry Center.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains gray with application of FeSO4
CAP  6-30cm, obtuse to convex becoming broadly convex; margin incurved at first; white, whitish, or grayish buff; dry, smooth^, (Bessette), (6)9-20(30)cm, white to whitish or buff during all stages, unchanging or becoming brown when bruised; dry, dull, smooth, sometimes obscurely rimose [cracked], when young often covered with easily removable pruinose coating, or appearing bald, (Thiers), 6-25cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat; dull white to grayish or buff; dry, smooth or very slightly velvety, (Arora), whitish, creamy with a pinkish tinge, to buff; smooth or suede-like, (Phillips), whitish to pale tan; suede-like surface texture, (Trudell)
FLESH  white, unchanging when cut^, (Bessette), 2-3cm thick, white except sometimes pale vinaceous near cap surface, unchanging, (Thiers), thick; "white, not blueing when exposed (or blueing only very slightly near the tubes)", (Arora)
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular, "white when young, becoming yellowish and finally olive or brownish at maturity, not blueing when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick^, (Bessette), 1-2mm, angular, white becoming yellowish when old, unchanging or darkening slightly when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, colored as pores, (Thiers), white or pallid when young, becoming yellow to olive-yellow when old, not turning blue when bruised, (Arora)
STEM  8-15.5cm x 2-5cm, nearly equal or widening downward, sometimes bulbous at base, stem solid; whitish to pinkish buff or tinged brownish; dry, finely reticulate [netted], at least on upper part^, (Bessette), 8-15cm x 2-5cm, bulb up to 9cm wide, clavate or bulbous, sometimes base pointed; solid, whitish to pinkish buff or pinkish cinnamon, white at base, unchanging when bruised, reticulations typically fine and delicate, (Thiers), 6-20 x 2-6cm, equal or widening downwards, solid, firm; whitish to buff or cap-colored, sometimes with brownish stains; finely reticulate over at least upper part, (Arora)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), pleasant (Phillips), mild or sometimes strong and unpleasant when dried, (Thiers), strong odor when drying (Trudell)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), pleasant (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  yes, choice, (Bessette, Arora) 
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under ponderosa pine, fir, spruce, and live oak^, (Bessette), single or scattered to gregarious under hardwoods and conifers
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Bessette), dark olive brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-15 x 4-5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped] to subelliptic [somewhat elliptic], smooth, ochraceous^, (Bessette), spores 13-15 x 4-5 um, subfusoid to subelliptic, smooth, pale ochraceous in Melzer's reagent and in KOH, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 25-34 x 8-11 um, clavate; hymenial cystidia crowded on pores, scattered to numerous on sides of tubes, 37-50 x 5-9 um, "cylindric to subclavate to obscurely fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle poorly differentiated as a cutis of appressed to slightly interwoven hyphae; no clamp connections, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  in honor of its discoverer, Charles Barrows (1904-1989), a New Mexico amateur mycologist
SIMILAR  Boletus edulis can be similar when whitish, but B. edulis has a clearly differentiated, often viscid cap cuticle (skin), (Arora). Caloboletus marshii has a bitter taste, has pale yellow pores when young, and lacks reticulation on the stem, (Arora). C. marshii has a bitter taste and bluing flesh, and lacks the reticulate stem (Frank). See also SIMILAR section of Boletus subalpinus.
SOURCES  Thiers(8), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Trudell(4), Redhead(5), AroraPocket(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Frank(5) (discussing Caloboletus marshii), Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*
5
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus edulis  Bull.^  Syst. Mycol. I: 392. 1821
ENGLISH NAME(S)  king bolete, cep, steinpilz, porcini, penny bun
NOTES  Features include 1) light brown to warm brown, yellowish brown or reddish brown cap, 2) small white pores that become yellowish or olive yellow or brownish but do not turn blue, 3) massive stem with whitish reticulation (network), and 4) mild taste. It is a popular edible mushroom.^ | Arora(2) described the often massive Boletus edulis var. grandedulis from CA, differing "in its brown to slightly reddish pore surface when mature". Var. edulis prefers Sitka spruce, whereas var. grandedulis occurs in a variety of coniferous forests, especially under pines, and occasionally live oak or madrone, (Siegel(2)). RANGE ^Boletus edulis is widely distributed in North America, including at least BC (collections at the University of British Columbia), WA and CA (collections at the University of Washington), and OR (collections at Oregon State University). It has been reported from MT (Larry Evans, pers. comm.), and ID (K. Chadwick, pers. comm.). It occurs in Europe, Asia, North Africa, and Australia, (Breitenbach). Daniel Winkler has reported var. grandedulis from WA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap stains orange with application of KOH, and slowly pale grayish green with FeSO4, flesh negative with KOH and pale grayish green with application of FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  4.5-38cm, convex to nearly flat; "brown to reddish brown, pale cinnamon brown, rusty red, or yellowish tan"; dry, viscid when moist, smooth to slightly wrinkled^, (Bessette), 9-18cm, convex to obtusely convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex, typically massive and heavy, margin incurved becoming downcurved; pale brown to buff, when old darkening to brown to reddish brown, often paler toward margin; moist to lubricous [slippery], usually becoming viscid to subviscid when wet or old, uneven or often irregularly pitted or wrinkled when old, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris) 
FLESH  white, not turning blue when bruised^, (Bessette), 2-4cm thick, firm, white, unchanging or darkening slightly when exposed, in stem white, unchanging except occasional brown stain at base, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "white when young, becoming yellow to olive-yellow, then brownish yellow to brown in age, staining yellowish olive to dull orange-cinnamon or pale yellowish brown when bruised", tube layer 1-3cm thick^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm, angular, white becoming yellow when old, unchanging or staining brown when bruised, tube layer 1-3cm thick, adnexed becoming depressed when old, unchanging when exposed, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), pores in the Boletus edulis group are ' "stuffed" with a white material which disappears as the pores expand and the yellow pore surface is exposed', (R. Bishop, pers. comm.)
STEM  5-31cm x 2-7.5cm, widening downward or nearly equal, sometimes bulbous, stem solid; white or pale brown with distinct whitish reticulum [network] on upper one-third or more^, (Bessette), 8-16cm x 1.5-2.5cm, massive, usually club-shaped, up to 6cm thick at bulbous base, solid; white to occasionally pale brown; dry to moist, bald but reticulate [netted] at least at top, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), reticulum first white then slightly darker than background (Lincoff(1))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), pleasant (Phillips)
TASTE  not distinctive (Bessette), mild or nutty (Arora(1) who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), pleasant (Phillips), sweetish, like hazelnuts, (Lincoff(1))
EDIBILITY  choice (Arora(1))
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on the ground under conifers^, (Bessette), generally associated with pines in California, but there are some varieties that are associated with hardwoods, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), favors conifers (pine, spruce, hemlock, fir) but also grows with hardwoods such as oak and birch, (Arora(1) who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), under conifers (pine, hemlock), and hardwoods (birch, aspen), (Lincoff(2)), under conifers, summer, "if there has been enough rain, and throughout the fall season; in spring in the Cascade Mountains, at elevations above 1000 feet", (Ammirati), spring, summer, and fall, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-19 x 4-6.5 um, "elliptic, smooth, pale yellowish brown"^, (Bessette), spores 13-18 x 4-7 um, fusiform [spindle-shaped] to somewhat elliptic, smooth, dark ochraceous in Melzer's, colorless to pale ochraceous in KOH, walls slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 30-40 x 10-12 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia often inconspicuous, often deeply embedded, 48-67 x 5-10 um, "fusoid-ventricose, often narrowed and with tapered, elongated apices", colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium that collapses when old, occasional free hyphal tips; stem cuticle differentiated as a layer of fertile basidia with scattered to numerous fusoid-ventricose caulocystidia; clamp connections absent, (Thiers who includes B. pinophilus / B. rex-veris), spores (13)14-17(19) x 4-6.5 um, subfusiform, smooth, as revived in Melzer's reagent "ochraceous shaded greenish gray but gradually becoming ochraceous to weakly dextrinoid", in KOH pale tawny, wall only slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 32-40 x 10-12 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; pleurocystidia "scattered, 42-48(65) X 7-10 um, narrowly fusoid-ventricose", colorless in KOH, pale yellowish in Melzer's reagent, smooth, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium of intertangled (and finally collapsing) hyphae 4-10 um wide, colorless and gelatinous in KOH, lacking incrusting pigments; clamp connections absent, (Smith for var. edulis)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'edible'
SIMILAR  Boletus pinophilus [the concept probably falling now under Boletus rex-veris] has a stem that is pale brown to brown in its lower part, darkening when handled or bruised, and the reticulation is whitish near top but brownish in lower part and darkening when handled, (Bessette). Boletus rex-veris [as B. pinophilus] has a dark red to red-brown cap whereas B. edulis is often lighter (Arora(1)). Boletus fibrillosus has a dry, much darker fibrillose cap, yellow tubes when young, and a much darker stem, (Thiers). Boletus regineus has a very dark brown to blackish cap that is moist to dry but not viscid, shorter spores, and a different cap cuticle, (Thiers). Boletus mottiae has a dark brown cap with a wrinkled-netted surface. Boletus barrowsii is told from very pale forms of B. edulis by the lack of a clearly differentiated cap cuticle in B. barrowsii. See also SIMILAR section of Boletus 'pinophilus' and Boletus subalpinus.
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Smith(35), Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Ammirati(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Breitenbach(3)*, Sept(1)*, AroraPocket(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Arora(2)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*, McBride(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*
6
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus edulis  var. grandedulis  D. Arora & Simonini^  Econ. Bot. 62(3): 374. 2008
ENGLISH NAME(S)  California king bolete
NOTES  Var. grandedulis is similar to typical (European) Boletus edulis but larger and with a brown to cinnamon pore surface when mature, (Arora(2)).^ | This variety is abundant "in the fall and winter (and occasionally spring) from San Luis Obispo County in central California north along the coast to Mendocino County and beyond, and fruiting during the summer and fall in the Sierra Nevada and other mountain ranges", (Arora(2)). The description is derived from Arora(2) except where noted. RANGE ^Boletus edulis var. grandedulis was described from CA and has been reported from the Pacific Northwest (e.g. Siegel(2) - see HABITAT.). Daniel Winkler has reported var. grandedulis from WA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  10-50cm across or more, "convex or bun-shaped in most stages"; "whitish soon becoming pale brown or brown or often deep reddish-brown as it matures, or yellow-brown in dry weather (often yellowest at margin)"; "viscid when moist", "smooth or wrinkled"
FLESH  in both cap and stem "thick, firm, white, not staining when cut (or rarely blueing slightly just above the tube layer)"
PORES  pore surface "whitish when young, becoming tan or slightly yellower as it matures and then becoming distinctly brown to cinnamon or even reddish at maturity", not blueing when bruised "but may stain slightly brownish"; "tube layer olive-yellow or olive when mature"
STEM  up to 40cm or more long and up to 15cm wide, "often massive", "often bulbous when young", clavate (club-shaped) to more or less equal when old; "typically white at first, remaining white or becoming pale brown to brown" when old; upper part "covered with a fine white reticulum when young, the reticulum sometimes obscure in age and at other times conspicuous and extending nearly to the base"
ODOR  mild or pleasant
TASTE  mild or pleasant
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  in California, single to gregarious in coastal pine forests and at their edges, less commonly associated with live oaks, "and found at mid-to-high elevations in the Sierra Nevada" with pine and true fir, (Arora(2)), Siegel(2) say in the California context that it occurs in various coniferous forests, especially under Monterey Pine and Bishop Pine, also Shore Pine on the Far North Coast, into the Pacific Northwest. They also mention that it occasionally associates with live oak and madrone, more rarely with Valley Oak. (Siegel(2))
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores (12)13-15.5(17) x (3.8)4-5.5(6) um, "fusiform to subellipsoid" (spindle-shaped to somewhat elliptic), smooth^; basidia 2-spored, 3-spored, or 4-spored, clavate; hymenial cystidia "inconspicuous, fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an often gelatinized trichodermium of interwoven hyphae with irregular, often diverticulate end cells" measuring 34-52 x 6-10 um
NAME ORIGIN  "grand" means "large" and "edulis" refers to Boletus edulis
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Arora(2)*, Siegel(2)*
7
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus fibrillosus  Thiers^  California Mushrooms pp.45-46. 1975
ENGLISH NAME(S)  fib king
NOTES  Features include a dark brown to chestnut brown, dry, fibrillose cap, unchanging flesh, yellow pores, a brown reticulate stem, and a red to pink KOH reaction on the cap cuticle.^ |According to D. Miller, pers. comm. May 10, 2021, reports of Boletus pinophilus could be Boletus fibrillosus. |The description is derived from Thiers except where indicated. RANGE ^Boletus fibrillosus is "recorded from the Pacific Coast of California and the Cascades, distribution limits yet to be established" (Bessette). The type is from CA. There are collections from WA and OR at the University of Washington. Paul Kroeger says Boletus fibrillosus appeared in quantity in BC about 2004, and a collection from 2005 in BC is at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  cap cuticle red to pink with application of KOH
CAP  6-17cm, convex becoming flat-convex to broadly convex to flat with flared margin, margin incurved at first; brown to dark brown, occasionally with paler buff spots; dry, even or somewhat wrinkled, typically tomentose to velvety, becoming fibrillose, sometimes fibrillose-scaly on disc^, (Thiers), "brown, cinnamon-brown to chestnut-brown, or dark brown, sometimes with buff-colored spots", (Bessette), "some shade of dark brown, often with lighter areas"; "typically wrinkled and velvety to distinctly fibrillose", (Trudell)
FLESH  1.5-3cm thick, soft, floccose [cottony]; white to pale buff, unchanging when exposed, in stem white, unchanging when exposed
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular; light yellow when young, dark yellow when old, unchanging; tube layer 1.5-2.5cm thick, "narrowly and shallowly depressed" around stem, colored as pores, unchanging or darkening slightly when bruised^, (Thiers), light yellow or somewhat darker, tubes similar in color, (Trudell), pale yellow to yellow or dingy olive-yellow (occasionally pallid when very young), (Arora)
STEM  10-16cm x 2-3cm, equal to club-shaped, rarely narrowing downward, often pinched at base, solid; pale yellow at top, otherwise light brown to brown; dry to moist, reticulate [netted] over most parts, white basal mycelium^, (Thiers), long relative to cap, often pinched off at base; pale at top, dark brown in lower part; strongly reticulate, covered by mycelium at base, (Trudell), 8-16cm x 2-4cm, usually lined or fibrillose, reticulate at least at top, (Arora)
ODOR  mild
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers), edible but inferior to B. edulis (Trudell)
HABITAT  single to scattered in soil in dense mixed coastal forests^, (Thiers), in dense mixed coastal forests or in conifer woods (Bessette), in old growth forests of Abies (fir) and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), earlier succession forests of T. heterophylla and Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) and other mixed forest stands, (Trudell)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive-brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-17.5 x 3.5-5.5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped] to ventricose [wider in middle], smooth, pale ochraceous in Melzer's, pale yellow to colorless in KOH, moderately thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 35-40 x 8-12 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered, inconspicuous, 38-46 x 6-8 um, subcylindric to fusoid-ventricose with elongated apices, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle differentiated as a trichodermium of hyphae have +/- equal cells; stem cuticle differentiated as a layer of fertile basidia and caulocystidia, which are colorless, subcylindric to clavate, abundant on edges of reticulum
NAME ORIGIN  means 'fibrillose'
SIMILAR  Boletus edulis has a smooth to wrinkled or pitted (not fibrillose) cap that is lighter in color and slippery to viscid when wet, white tubes when young, and a stem that is lighter in color, (Thiers). See also SIMILAR section of 'Boletus' mirabilis and Boletus mottiae.
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4)*, Arora(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*, McAdoo(1)*
8
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus mottiae  Thiers^  California Mushrooms p.36. 1975
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  It is possible that at least the holotype of the species is a variation of Boletus regineus. The outstanding character is the strongly reticulated or ridged cap surface, other features include a moist to subviscid brown cap, white unchanging flesh, angular white pores that become yellow to greenish yellow but do not change when bruised, buff to pinkish buff stem that is finely reticulate over entire surface, and microscopic characters.^ |According to D. Miller (pers. comm. December 5, 2021) this may be Boletus regineus and not a separate species, since an unpublished sequence of the holotype of B. mottiae matches Boletus regineus - the name B. regineus could still take priority if B. mottiae is considered an aberrant form. |Boletus mottiae was described by Thiers as Boletus mottii, but since the person after whom it was named was a woman, "mottiae" is correct. RANGE ^The species was described from CA. It has been reported from WA (J. Lindgren, pers. comm., M. Beug, pers. comm.) and there is a collection by J. Ammirati at the University of Washington. There is a collection from BC at the Pacific Forestry Centre. It has also been reported from OR (H. Thiers 1986 pers. comm. to L. Norvell), and appears on an Idaho foray list.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap surface and flesh do not stain with the application of KOH or FeSO4
CAP  6-12cm, convex, expanding, sometimes as far as shallowly depressed; brown ("cinnamon" to "cinnamon brown" Ridgway colors) on the disc, fading to buff toward the margin; moist to subviscid, "conspicuously wrinkled-reticulate over entire surface during all stages of development, the reticulum noticeable raised on the disc, but sometimes becoming shallower and may eventually appear as appressed fibrillose scales on the very margin", the reticulations remain very pronounced on dried caps and are usually darker than intervening areas^, (Thiers), pitted and/or ridged cap (Arora)
FLESH  1-2cm thick, soft; white, unchanging when exposed, the same in the stem^ (Thiers)
PORES  up to 1mm broad, angular, white when young, becoming yellow to greenish yellow when mature, unchanging when bruised; tube layer 0.5-1cm thick, depressed, colored as pores and unchanging when bruised^, (Thiers)
STEM  5-7.5cm x 1.5-3cm at top, equal to subclavate [somewhat club-shaped], solid; buff to pinkish buff during all stages of development; "dry, finely reticulate over entire surface", reticulations colored as surface, "reticulations suggestive of those of B. edulis but more delicate"^, (Thiers) 
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Thiers)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers)
HABITAT  type was found under conifers^ (Thiers), fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  unknown
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14.3-17.1 x 3.8-5.2 um, cylindric to subelliptic [somewhat elliptic] in face view, ventricose and inequilateral in side view, smooth^, pale ochraceous in KOH, slightly darker in Melzer's reagent, thin-walled; basidia 2-spored and 4-spored, 23-30 x 7-10 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia not seen when specimen revived in KOH; cap cuticle "subgelatinous, up to 500 um broad, well differentiated as a somewhat tangled to suberect trichodermium, hyaline in KOH, not staining in Melzer's, terminal hyphae septate with terminal cells ovoid to clavate, 20-40 x 10-20 um, subterminal cells more elongate to ellipsoid, walls sometimes incrusted, cross walls inamyloid, hyphae 3-7 um wide"; stem cuticle "a layer of fertile basidia with scattered, fusoid to clavate to fusoid-ventricose caulocystidia"; clamp connections not seen, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  after Lillian Mott
SIMILAR  Boletus mottiae is distinguished from members of Boletus edulis complex by the strongly reticulated or ridged surface of the cap of B. mottiae. When compared with B. edulis, cap color is different, and reticulum on stem is of B. mottiae is much more delicate, (Thiers). Boletus fibrillosus (cap surface remotely suggestive of that of B. mottiae) has yellow tubes when young, a rather elongated stem, and a much darker colored cap, (Thiers).
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus mottii), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)
9
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus regineus  D. Arora and Simonini^  Economic Botany 62: 358. 2008
ENGLISH NAME(S)  queen bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a nearly black cap with a whitish bloom and a smooth or somewhat pitted surface, 2) white to vinaceous-tinged, unchanging flesh, 3) small angular pores that are white becoming yellowish and do not turn blue, 4) club-shaped to equal, reticulate stem that is white and may become vinaceous brown, 5) growth under hardwoods or conifers, and 6) microscopic characters.^ | Boletus regineus was known for many years on the west coast of North America as Boletus aereus Bull. The taxon of CA, OR, and WA was redescribed in 2008 as Boletus regineus D. Arora and Simonini. The authors noted that the gelatinized pileipellis clearly distinguished it from European B. aereus, which has a dry, velvety cap cuticle with a non-gelatinized trichoderm of subparallel hyphae, and a browner stem, (Arora(2)). Boletus mottiae Thiers, California Mushrooms p.36. 1975 is said to be a "mutated" collection of Boletus regineus. RANGE ^Collections of Boletus regineus were examined from CA where it is fairly common, but distribution in OR and WA is also mentioned (Arora(2)). It has been reported as Boletus aereus from OR (H. Thiers 1986, pers. comm. to L. Norvell, Dick Bishop pers. comm.), WA (J. Lindgren pers. comm., M. Beug pers. comm., collection at the University of Washington as B. aereus), and BC (O. Ceska pers. comm.). A collection from OR was used in Arora(3).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh does not stain with application of KOH or FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  7-30cm across, convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; "very dark brown when fresh but often entirely or partially overlaid with a fine white bloom that gives it a paler, frosted appearance, in age the hoary patches tending to disappear and the surface often becoming paler brown or cinnamon blotched with even paler (white to pale tan) areas"; bald except for the hoary patches, moist but typically viscid when old or in wet weather, "sometimes uneven or with broad depressions"^, (Arora(2)), 5-15(20)cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat; "dark brown to nearly black when young and covered at least partially with a fine whitish bloom", but when old becoming smooth and cinnamon or red-brown or blotched with paler, whitish to tan areas; dry or moist (or viscid only when old), smooth or somewhat pitted, (Arora(1)), 10-15cm, convex to nearly spherical when young, becoming broadly convex to flat-convex to flat when old; dark brown to almost black, when old sometimes fading irregularly on disc to buff or reddish brown with irregular areas almost white to tan; moist, never viscid, often pitted or with broad depressions, especially toward margin, bald but with scattered white pruinose areas or patches, (Thiers)
FLESH  thick and white in cap and stem, "but sometimes vinaceous-tinged when young", "not staining appreciably when cut or sometimes exhibiting a very slight blueing just above the tube layer"^, (Arora(2)), thick; white or tinged reddish, not turning blue when exposed, or turning blue only slightly near tubes, (Arora(1)), 2-4cm thick, vinaceous when young, white when old except pale yellow above tubes and pale vinaceous below cap surface; in stem white and unchanging when exposed, (Thiers)
PORES  pores and tubes white when young becoming yellow then greenish yellow as fruiting body matures, not turning blue when bruised^, (Arora(2)), at first stuffed with pith; white when young, becoming yellow to greenish yellow when old, not turning blue when exposed, (Arora(1)), up to 1mm, angular; white when young, becoming yellow when mature, not changing where bruised; tube layer 1.5-2cm thick, adnate to adnexed, becoming depressed, white when young, becoming yellow when mature, (Thiers), 1-3 per mm, angular; white becoming dingy yellow; tube layer 1-2cm thick, becoming depressed near stem when old, (Bessette)
STEM  7-20cm x 2.5-8cm, equal to clavate [club-shaped] or slightly bulbous (especially when young); white or nearly so, sometimes brownish when old but more often remaining white; bald except for reticulation over at least the upper part, the reticulation white at first, white to brown when old^, (Arora(2)), 5-15cm x 2-5cm at top, usually widening downward when young but often equal when old, firm, solid; white or often brown when old; "finely reticulate at least over upper part", (Arora(1)), 8-11.5cm x 2.5-4cm at top, clavate [club-shaped] to subbulbous [somewhat bulbous] when young, unchanging or becoming more or less equal when old, solid; when young pallid to almost white, unchanging or becoming vinaceous or vinaceous brown when old; "dry, reticulate at least over upper half, sometimes strongly and conspicuously so", (Thiers)
ODOR  mild^ (Arora(2)), mild (Thiers)
TASTE  mild^ (Arora(2)), mild or pleasant (Arora(1)), mild (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  delicious (Arora(1), Arora(2), Thiers)
HABITAT  single or in groups, in California north to Oregon commonly associated with hardwoods (tanoak especially, madrone, golden chinquapin, manzanita, and oak), usually in fall (October to December) but occasionally in spring, less commonly in the Cascades of Oregon and Washington associated with conifers^, (Arora(2)), single, scattered, or gregarious in mixed woods and under hardwoods (especially oaks), (Arora(1)), scattered to gregarious under oaks and madrones (Thiers for California)
SPORE DEPOSIT  "olive-brown in mass, but yellower than closely related species under the microscope", (Arora(2))^, dark olive-brown (Arora(1), Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (11.5)12-15(17) x (3.9)4-4.7(5.4) um, "variable in shape and dimensions, long ellipsoid-fusiform"^; basidia clavate, colorless, mostly 4-spored, 23-40 x 8-11.5 um; hymenial cystidia thin-walled, inconspicuous; cap cuticle "a completely gelatinized cutis when mature (and sometimes even in youth)", typically 200-250 um thick, cuticular cells 7-10 um wide, "variable in shape, sometimes branched or with diverticula", (Arora(2)), spores 12-14 x 4-5 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth, (Arora(1)), spores 12-13.5 x 4-5 um, subelliptic to subfusoid, smooth, pale ochraceous in KOH and Melzer's, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 23-30 x 8-10 um, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia inconspicuous, embedded in hymenium, 28-34 x 7-11 um, colorless in KOH and Melzer's, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a broad (175 um) trichodermium of erect, septate hyphal tips, terminal cells not enlarged, not incrusted, hyaline in KOH, pale ochraceous in Melzer's, hyphae 4-5 um wide"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  Latin 'regina' means 'queen'
SIMILAR  Boletus edulis has a lighter brown cap that may become viscid, longer spores and a different cap cuticle, (Thiers). B. edulis has paler cap often with reddish tones, and grows under conifers, (Bessette).
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus aereus), Arora(1)* (as Boletus aereus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus aereus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus aereus), Lincoff(1)* (as Boletus aereus), Both(1), Arora(2), AroraPocket(1)* (as Boletus aereus), Arora(3), Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus rex-veris  D. Arora & Simonini^  Economic Botany 62(3): 373. 2008;  Boletus pinophilus (misapplied name);  Boletus pinicola (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  spring king bolete, spring king
NOTES  Features include a dry reddish brown cap, when young with a fine white bloom, white unstaining flesh, pores that are whitish at first becoming yellow or yellowish buff and finally greenish, the pores sometimes becoming cinnamon brown near cap margin, dry white-reticulate club-shaped stem that is white when young, often developing brownish tinges when old, and spring growth in the mountains.^ |This species has been referred to as Boletus pinophilus Pilat & Dermek and as its synonym Boletus pinicola (Vittad.) A. Venturi, but that European species is considered by some authors to be somewhat different. Bessette(3) describes as "widely distributed throughout North America" a taxon under the name Boletus pinophilus Pilat & Dermek (with spores given as 15-20 x 4-6 um). Arora(2) say that the spores of B. rex-veris are slightly narrower than those of the European Boletus pinophilus, but Snell(6) found that spores of dried collections of Boletus edulis subsp. pinicola sent to them from California, Montana, Wyoming and Colorado were somewhat wider and longer (otherwise fitting the European descriptions). Whereas the European descriptions gave the spores as 14-17 x 4.5-5.5 um, 13-17.5 x 4.5-6 um etc., they found the spores of the specimens on the whole slightly larger: 14-21 x 5-6 um, with a few 7 um wide, mostly 16-18 x 5.5-6 um, with a tendency for the majority to vary in length directly with the size of the specimens. (Snell(6)). Nevertheless, sequencing of a Washington "spring king" indicated that it was Boletus rex-veris (M. Beug, pers. comm.). |According to D. Miller, pers. comm. May 10, 2021, reports of Boletus pinophilus could be Boletus fibrillosus. |The description is derived from Arora(2). RANGE ^Collections were examined from CA and OR by Arora(2). In CA it is locally abundant in the mountains, fruiting in April-June at mid-elevations (3000-4000 feet) and into July at higher elevations (up to 7000 feet), occurring at somewhat lower elevations in Cascades of OR and WA, extending east through the Blue Mountains to ID and perhaps beyond, and north to BC, outside of California also fruiting principally in spring or early summer, (Arora(2)). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  10-30cm across or occasionally larger, broadly convex or bun-shaped, becoming nearly flat or uneven; usually reddish brown but varying to tan or paler where hidden by soil or humus and often developing ocher tones when old or where exposed to the sun, and at other times dark dull brown; typically not viscid except when wet or when old, bald "except for a very fine white bloom that covers often irregular areas of the young cap"
FLESH  firm; white in both cap and stem (or reddish-tinged just above tube layer), unchanging when cut
PORES  pores and tube layer "white when young, gradually becoming yellow or yellowish-buff and finally olive-yellow or greenish", pores near the cap margin may become cinnamon brown, but pore surface not becoming uniformly brown or cinnamon
STEM  5-20cm or more long, 2.5-10cm wide, typically club-shaped with a pointed and often a curved base when young, becoming more or less equal when old "but usually retaining a tapered or pointed base"; white when young, often developing tan or more often reddish brown tinges when old, especially in the upper part but occasionally throughout; typically reticulate over upper part, reticulum fine and white when young (sometimes barely discernable), often brown when old "as the meshes become wider and coarser" 
ODOR  mild
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  yes (Arora)
HABITAT  scattered to gregarious or clustered under mountain conifers, especially Pinus ponderosa (Ponderosa Pine), Pinus contorta (Lodgepole Pine), and Abies spp., often buried in needle duff beneath understory shrubs
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores (14)15-17(19) x (4)4.5-5(5.2) um, elliptic-subfusiform with prominent suprahilar depression, yellowish^; basidia 4-spored, (40)45-60(65) x 9-11.5 um, clavate; pleurocystidia (45)50-65 x 6.5-7.5 um, cylindric-fusiform, colorless, cheilocystidia (30)45-70 x 11-17 um, "clavate, sometimes narrowing or with capitulum or wide-fusiform"; cap cuticle an interwoven trichoderm, often weakly gelatinized when old but typically not gelatinized when young, made up of chains of cells with cylindric end cells having sharpened or rounded tips, (20)30-80(128) x (6.5)8-14(20) um
NAME ORIGIN  "rex" Latin for "king", "veris" Latin for "of spring" (Arora)
SIMILAR  Boletus pinophilus of Europe has a more highly gelatinized cap cuticle, a browner stem, and slightly broader spores, (Arora(2) but see also comments in NOTES). Spores of Boletus pinicola Vitt. of Europe (with synonym B. pinophilus Pilat and Dermek) are 14-17 x 4.5-5.5 um, (Moser(1)). See also SIMILAR section of Boletus edulis and Boletus 'pinophilus'.
SOURCES  Arora(2), Bessette(3) discussing B. pinophilus, Moser(1) (discussing B. pinicola Vitt. and B. pinophilus Pilat & Dermek), Snell(6) (re Boletus edulis subsp. pinicola), Desjardin(6)*, Marrone(1)*, McAdoo(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  'Boletus' smithii  Thiers^  Mycologia 57: 530-532. 1965
ENGLISH NAME(S)  Smith's Boletus
NOTES  It is likely that this species will require combination into a new genus. Features include 1) velvety, tomentose, or fibrillose cap that is olive buff to olive yellow with pinkish or reddish overtones and decidedly red when old, 2) flesh that is pale yellow and turns blue erratically when cut, 3) yellow pores that become olive yellow when old, and turn blue, and 4) stem that is yellow or red or usually a combination, and often pruinose but not reticulate.^ |'Boletus' smithii will probably be moved to a different genus.. RANGE ^Boletus smithii is found in WA, OR, ID, and CA, (Thiers), and also in BC, (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle black with KOH, (Thiers), cap cuticle dark reddish brown to blackish with application of KOH, flesh blue-gray with application of FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  5-16cm, convex to flat-convex; background olive yellow to buff, but typically with pinkish or reddish overtones or blotches, when older frequently red over entire surface or with olive pigment confined to narrow marginal zone, sometimes becoming yellowish near margin; dry, dull, often with irregular depressions when older, velvety to tomentose when young, becoming fibrillose to matted fibrillose when old, sometimes appearing bald when very old and often becoming rimose-areolate [cracked like dried mud] with drying or when old^, (Thiers), "tawny-olive to olive-yellow or pale olive-brown when young, developing reddish tones and sometimes pinkish red to read nearly overall in age", (Bessette), olive to olive-yellow, flushed in part or entirely rose red, especially when old, (Phillips), olive buff with pinkish or reddish overtones, more decidedly red when old, (Both), often has gray-brown, buff, and tan colors, especially at first, becoming infused with pinkish to reddish overtones, some caps very reddish when old, (Trudell)
FLESH  1-3cm thick, pale yellow except sometimes reddish near cap surface, "unchanging or slowly and erratically changing to blue when exposed, occasionally rapidly bluing", in stem yellow to pallid, turning blue when exposed^, (Thiers), firm, pale yellow, slightly blue when bruised, (Phillips)
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular; yellow becoming dark yellow, sometimes reddish near margin when old; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, adnate to arcuate-decurrent when young, typically deeply narrowly depressed when old, yellow becoming dark yellow, turning blue immediately on exposure^, (Thiers), yellow at first, becoming olive-yellow when old, turning blue when bruised, (Bessette)
STEM  6-16cm x 1-3.5cm at top, equal or widening downward or sometimes club-shaped, solid; red toward top, changing to yellow toward the base, "sometimes the color pattern reversed or entirely red or, rarely, entirely yellow, usually with a distinct red band at the apex with age"; "dry, smooth to granulose or pruinose, occasionally appearing appressed-fibrillose to tomentose, not reticulate"^, (Thiers)
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers)
TASTE  mild^ (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette, Phillips)
HABITAT  single to gregarious on soil in dense mixed coastal forests, fall and winter^, (Thiers), single, scattered, or in groups on ground in conifer or mixed woods, July to December, (Bessette), summer, fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14.5-19.2 x 4-6 um, fusoid to subcylindric in face view, ventricose in side view, smooth, inamyloid, pale ochraceous in KOH, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 30-36 x 7-10 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia typically deeply embedded in hymenium (and sometimes obscure), 30-60 x 7-12 um, clavate to fusoid to fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a layer of loosely interwoven tangled hyphae forming poorly differentiated trichodermium, often with free hyphal tips, occasionally with numerous laticifers; stem cuticle has fertile basidia in upper part, changing to a layer of interwoven hyphae toward base; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  after Alexander H. Smith (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Caloboletus calopus and Caloboletus rubripes have bitter flesh and do not develop red tones on the cap, (Bessette).
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Ammirati(1)*, Both(1), Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)* (as "Boletus" smithii), McAdoo(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Boletus subalpinus  (Trappe & Thiers) M. Nuhn, Manfr. Binder, A.F.S. Taylor, Halling, Hibbett^  Fungal Biology 117 (7-8): 479-511. 2013;  Gastroboletus subalpinus  Trappe and Thiers  Brittonia 21: 245. 1969
ENGLISH NAME(S)  gastroid king-bolete
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. Features include 1) a whitish to buff cap, 2) white flesh that does not turn blue, 3) tubes oriented at various angles and often covered by a membrane extending down from cap, 4) whitish pores that finally darken to olive brown, 5) a short stem colored as the cap, and 6) development under the ground under mountain conifers, often with partial exposure at maturity.^ | It is common among false truffles in the Pacific Northwest (Trappe(13)). |According to D. Miller, pers. comm., "This is a sister species of the western queen, Boletus regineus." RANGE ^Boletus subalpinus is fairly common a higher elevations in CA, OR, and Washington, (Bessette), There is a collection from Washington at the University of Washington by P. Matheny in 1996, but Castellano(1)(1999) gives sites only in CA and OR and says "not known from Washington". The type is from Cloud Cap Oregon, not far from Washington.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  5-12cm, convex becoming flat to depressed or somewhat irregular; "whitish to buff or pale tan", sometimes darkening somewhat when old; "smooth to slightly velvety, often pitted and/or wrinkled, not viscid but often covered with dirt and debris"; margin extending down over tubes as thin white membrane that often disappears or breaks up when old^, (Arora), 6-10cm, convex becoming flat to deeply depressed to flat-convex when mature, bolete-like in general appearance, often highly pitted and wrinkled and irregular in outline; "warm buff" to "light buff" to "pale olive buff" to "pinkish cinnamon", unchanging when old or darkening to near "clay color" to "buckthorn brown" (colors from Ridgway); dry to moist, bald to velvety to subtomentose, typically covered with soil and debris; peridium on lateral and undersides of spore-producing area "white, thin, velvety, persistent except for scattered, circular ruptures" over mouths of occasional tubes when old, (Thiers)
FLESH  thick, soft; "white or with yellow to olive discolorations", not turning blue when bruised but sometimes staining pinkish^, (Arora), 0.3-1.5cm thick at disc, tapering to margin, soft; white or on some fruitbodies "with sordid yellowish or olivaceous zones", in some fruitbodies "slowly staining pinkish to grayish lavender when cut", in stem white, unchanging, (Thiers)
PORES  pallid or whitish becoming grayish yellow to buff and finally darkening to olive-brown, not turning blue when bruised; tubes occasionally vertical especially near stem but mostly at various angles, colored more or less like pores^, (Arora), less than 1mm across, colored like tubes, unchanging when bruised; tube layer 1-3cm thick, but markedly decreased in thickness in both directions from thickest area, depressed to seceded from stem, strongly projecting beyond upper cap surface but then covered by the thin, pallid, lateral outer covering; occasionally tubes vertical near stem but mostly curved and oriented 20 degrees from vertical, or angled upward near edges of fruitbody, "dark olive buff" to "pale olive buff", when old becoming "buffy citrine" to "citrine drab", (Ridgway colors), (Thiers), pores round to angular, (Bessette)
STEM  2-6cm x 2-5cm, narrowing downward or sometimes equal or even swollen in lower part, solid; white to buff, sometimes darkening to tan when old, base sometimes orange-yellow; dry, often dirty^, (Arora), 2-5cm x 2-4.5cm at top, base up to 3-4cm wide; subventricose [more or less wide in middle] or narrowing downward, occasionally "equal to bulbous at the base"; dirty white to "warm buff" to "light buff" in some, the base "apricot yellow" (Ridgway colors); dry, but typically covered with adhering debris, bald to velvety to subtomentose with the top reticulate when gleba has seceded, (Thiers), no annulus (Bessette)
ODOR  mild to farinaceous^ (Thiers)
TASTE  mild, pleasant^, (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single to gregarious "in soil or duff under mountain conifers in late spring and summer", developing underground but often partially exposed when mature^, (Arora), single to gregarious, underground or partly emerging, under Pinus albicaulis or Abies magnifica and possibly other conifers of montane and subalpine forests, "Commonly found late spring and summer", (Thiers), developing under soil surface associated with the roots of various Pinaceae above 1550m elevation, particularly Abies magnifica (Red Fir), Pinus albicaulis (Whitebark Pine), and Tsuga mertensiana (Mountain Hemlock), September and October, (Castellano), with Pinus albicaulis, Pinus contorta, Abies, Tsuga mertensiana, at relatively high elevations to the tree line; July to October, (Trappe, M.(3)), spring, summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  unobtainable^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-16(18) x 4.5-6(8) um, elliptic to spindle-shaped, smooth, yellowish to brown^, (Arora), spores 10-16(18) x 4.5-6(8) um, elliptic, oblong to subovate [somewhat oval], "occasionally irregularly constricted near middle", smooth, light yellow to ochraceous in Melzer's reagent and KOH, moderately thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 40-48 x 8-13 um, clavate, colorless and guttulate in KOH, thin-walled, brachybasidioles 25-40 x 7-12 um, similar in shape and appearance to basidia; cystidia "often abundant when young, especially on the pores, apparently collapsing and appearing infrequently with age, single or sometimes clustered", 24-75 x 7-10 um, subcylindric to clavate to tapered, colorless in KOH and Melzer's reagent, thin-walled; epicutis of peridium on top of fruitbody of densely interwoven, much branched hyphae 4-15 um wide, with erect, tapered, blunt-tipped terminal cells 25-45 x 7-12 um, sparsely scattered over surface; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "subalpine, growing below the timber line" (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Boletus barrowsii and Boletus edulis are somewhat similar, but those species have vertical tubes.
SOURCES  Thiers(7) (as Gastroboletus), Arora(1) (as Gastroboletus), Bessette(3)* (as Gastroboletus), Castellano(1)* (as Gastroboletus), Trappe, M.(1)* (as Gastroboletus), Trappe, M.(3)* (as Gastroboletus), Both(1) (as Gastroboletus), Desjardin(6)* (as Gastroboletus), Trappe(13) (as Gastroboletus)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Buchwaldoboletus lignicola  (Kallenb.) Pilat^  Friesia 9: 217. 1969;  Boletus lignicola  Kallenb.;  Xerocomus lignicola  (Kallenb.) Singer;  Gyrodon lignicola  (Kallenb.) Heinem.;  Pulveroboletus lignicola  (Kallenb.) E.A. Dick & Snell
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) reddish brown to yellow brown, tomentose cap, 2) yellow flesh that usually turns blue when cut, 3) decurrent, angular pores that are yellow becoming brownish yellow when old, and staining bluish green when bruised, 4) eccentric, finely powdery stem that is colored like the cap, and 5) growth on or near conifer stumps or sawdust.^ | It often fruits with the polypore Phaeolus schweinitzii (Bessette(3)). RANGE ^Buchwaldoboletus lignicola, also known as Boletus lignicola, has been found in OR (The accompanying photograph of an OR collection was identified based on 99% ITS sequence match to multiple European collections.) It is rare in North America, with distribution in eastern Canada south to PA, west to MI, the limits yet to be established (Bessette(3)). Distribution is NS and ON, south to PA, west to MI, the limits yet to be established, (Both). Distribution specifically includes PQ (Ortiz-Santana). It is also found in Europe from the subarctic south to Switzerland (Ortiz-Santana).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  2.5-10cm across, convex becoming broadly convex, margin inrolled when young; "rusty brown to yellow-brown or rusty golden yellow"; dry, subtomentose, often finely rimose-areolate [cracked] when old, "margin with a narrow band of sterile tissue"^, (Bessette), 5-20cm across, "convex with inrolled margin; reddish brown to yellow-brown or rust; subtomentose, floccose at first, then smooth", (Phillips), tomentose-squamose (Both), soft appressed tomentum is easily removed, "attached to the flesh by a thin gelatinous layer, so that the tomentum can be moved back and forth", (Ortiz-Santana)
FLESH  yellow, usually turning blue when cut, "but sometimes slowly or not at all"^, (Bessette), firm; pale lemon yellow (Phillips)
PORES  1-3 per mm, angular, subdecurrent, "yellow to golden yellow at first", becoming brownish yellow when old, "staining bluish green when bruised"; tubes 0.3-1.2cm deep^, (Bessette), decurrent; "bright yellow, bruising blue-green"; tubes bright yellow, (Phillips), greenish yellow, bruising blue (Both), yellow to golden, bruising greenish blue, (Ortiz-Santana)
STEM  3-8cm x 0.6-2.5cm, "nearly equal or tapered in either direction, usually narrowed at the base, frequently eccentric", solid; "rusty brown to yellow-brown"; dry, finely tomentose, yellow mycelium at base; "partial veil and annulus absent"^, (Bessette), 3.0-8.0 x 0.5-2.5cm, "often eccentric, tapered below"; rust yellow to brown; dry, pulverulent, (Phillips), frequently eccentric; similar in color to cap; finely tomentose, (Both), "basal area sulphur-yellow, ending in a golden-yellow mycelium", (Ortiz-Santana)
ODOR  faintly aromatic^ (Bessette), faint, aromatic, (Phillips), 2 records of unpleasant odor (Ortiz-Santana)
TASTE  weakly acidic or not distinctive^ (Bessette), pleasant (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette), edible (Phillips)
HABITAT  single or scattered "on or around conifer stumps or sawdust", July to October^, (Bessette), "always on stumps or trunks of conifers, exceptionally on sawdust", (Phillips), "with white pine and other pines, on and around conifers stumps", (Both), associated with pines, also with Larix (larch), rarely with hardwoods (Prunus avium), (Ortiz-Santana)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette), olive (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-10 x 3-4 um, elliptic, smooth, yellowish^ (Bessette), spores 6.5-9 x 2.8-3.8 um (Phillips), spores 6-9(12) x 3-4 um, cystidia 29-80 x 4-9 um, (Ortiz-Santana)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'inhabiting wood'
SIMILAR  Both(1) wrote that there was considerable confusion between this species and other similar lignicolous boletes (here considered as Buchwaldoboletus sphaerocephalus and Buchwaldoboletus hemichrysus). Buchwaldoboletus hemichrysus Berkeley and Curtis is similar but has not been documented from the Pacific Northwest. It has a bright golden yellow to orange-yellow cap, "a red-brown to reddish brown pore surface", and a stem tinged with red, (Bessette(3) as Boletus hemichrysus). Buchwaldoboletus sphaerocephalus has a yellow cap and stem.
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus lignicola), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus lignicola), Both(1) (as Boletus lignicola), Ortiz-Santana(1)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Buchwaldoboletus sphaerocephalus  (Barla) Watling & T.H. Li, in Watling^  Edinb. J. Bot. 61(1): 46. 2004;  Boletus sphaerocephalus  Barla
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include a yellow, dry to slightly viscid cap that is somewhat tomentose, yellow flesh that turns blue, yellow pores that turn blue then brown, yellow stem that is more or less equal and somewhat matted-fibrillose, and growth on wood or soil near stumps.^ RANGE ^Buchwaldoboletus sphaerocephalus has been recorded from ID, FL west along the Gulf Coast, and MI: the distribution limits remain to be established, (Bessette). Distribution is throughout Europe, from Scandinavia to Italy, in North America from Canada south to FL, along the Gulf Coast to TX, west to MI and ID, (Ortiz-Santana).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh no reaction with FeSO4 (Smith)
CAP  4-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; sulphur yellow when young, becoming pale yellow to whitish when old; dry to slightly viscid, subtomentose [somewhat tomentose], often areolate [cracked like dried mud] when old^, (Bessette), 5-10cm, convex with incurved irregular margin, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; sulphur-yellow but on aging slowly fading to pallid; subviscid (but no gelatinous layer present), unpolished to subtomentose, when old at times areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith)
FLESH  "sulphur-yellow to pale yellow", typically turning blue when exposed, but sometimes slowly or not at all^, (Bessette), thick; sulphur-yellow under the cap surface, paler inward, turning blue when cut; in stem yellow, (Smith)
PORES  1-3 per mm, angular; "yellow at first, becoming dull yellow to brownish yellow", turning blue, then slowly staining brownish when bruised; tube layer 0.8-1.5cm thick, becoming depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm when young, broader when mature, staining blue and then slowly brownish where injured; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, pale sulphur yellow when young, staining blue when injured, (Smith)
STEM  5-10cm x 1-3cm, nearly equal down to tapered base, solid; yellow, with yellow basal mycelium; dry, appressed-fibrillose, lacking reticulation; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 6-10cm x 1-2.5cm, (or larger in giant fruitbodies), central to off-center, narrowed to a point at base, solid; yellow; unpolished, appearing somewhat matted-fibrillose, yellow tomentum and mycelium with debris around base, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), slight (Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive or somewhat bitter^ (Bessette), mild to bitterish (Smith)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  "scattered, in groups or clusters in sawdust, or on stumps or the surrounding soil"^, (Bessette)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Bessette), "olive brownish or tinged ochraceous in the olive-brown range", (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-9 x 3-4 um, oblong to elliptic, smooth, yellow^, (Bessette), spores 7-9 x 3-3.5 um, oblong to elliptic, smooth, dull ochraceous in Melzer's reagent, bright yellow in KOH, lacking apical pore, walls slightly thickened; pleurocystidia 33-50 x 9-15 um, "mostly fusoid-ventricose, thin-walled and with ochraceous to rusty ochraceous content as revived in KOH, none seen with incrustation"; cap cuticle of interwoven hyphae, yellow fading to colorless in KOH, incrustation not seen, epicuticular zone compactly interwoven, with hyphal end-cells that have blunt to narrowed apex and some inflated to 8-12 um wide in the midportion; clamp connections absent, (Smith) 
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with spherical head [cap]'
SIMILAR  Both(1) wrote that there was considerable confusion between this species and other similar lignicolous boletes (here considered as Buchwaldoboletus lignicola and Buchwaldoboletus hemichrysus). Buchwaldoboletus hemichrysus Berkeley and Curtis is similar but has not been documented from the Pacific Northwest. It has a bright golden yellow to orange-yellow cap, "a red-brown to reddish brown pore surface", and a stem tinged with red, (Bessette(3) as Boletus hemichrysus). Buchwaldoboletus lignicola has a rusty brown to yellow-brown or rusty golden yellow cap and a stem colored similarly to the cap.
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus sphaerocephalus), Smith(35) (as Boletus sphaerocephalus), Both(1) (as Boletus sphaerocephalus), Ortiz-Santana(1), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Butyriboletus abieticola  (Thiers) D. Arora & J.L Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 466. 2014;  Boletus abieticola  Thiers  California Mushrooms pp.39-40. 1975
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a cap that is light rose with tan-colored spots interspersed and appearing yellow under shingled fibrillose scales, 2) white to pale yellow flesh that occasionally changes to blue, 3) yellow tubes and pores that change to blue, 4) yellow stem with reddish base and yellow reticulation on upper part, and 5) microscopic characters including spirally incrusted hyphae of the cap cuticle.^ | According to Bessette(3), it is fairly common. RANGE ^Butyriboletus abieticola is found from the Rocky Mountains to the Pacific Northwest and CA, (Bessette). There are collections from WA at the University of Washington and from OR at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH on flesh pale violaceous, (Thiers)
CAP  9-13cm, convex becoming flat-convex at maturity, margin incurved, entire; "light rose with tan-colored spots interspersed and appearing yellow under the scales"; "dry, appressed fibrillose, forming more or less imbricated fibrillose scales that remain apparent even when dried"^, (Thiers), 5-13cm wide, convex, becoming broadly convex to flat when old, margin incurved and even; "predominantly pinkish to tan on a yellowish ground color"; dry, appressed-fibrillose to fibrillose-scaly, (Bessette)
FLESH  up to 2cm thick; "white to pale yellow, usually unchanging, occasionally slowly changing to blue in irregular areas when exposed"; in stem white except reddish at base, unchanging when exposed^, (Thiers), white to yellowish, unchanging or slowly turning blue erratically, in stem white except for reddish base, unchanging when cut, (Bessette)
PORES  up to 1mm, angular; yellow ("lemon yellow") when young, darkening and with greenish tints when mature, changing to blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, "adnate to adnexed, shallowly depressed with age", colored as pores, changing to blue when bruised or exposed^, (Thiers), pores up to 0.1cm wide, pore surface "yellow at first, becoming olive-yellow at maturity, staining blue when bruised", tubes 1-2cm deep, often depressed near stem when old, (Bessette)
STEM  9-12cm x 4-5cm, widening to 5-6cm at widest point, club-shaped to somewhat bulbous, solid; yellow, reddish at base; strongly yellow-reticulate over all but basal part^, (Thiers), 4-12cm x 2.5-5cm, widening downwards, solid; yellow with reddish base, dry, strongly yellow reticulate at least on upper part, often nearly over the whole surface; partial veil and ring absent blueing, (Bessette)
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers), not distinctive (Bessette)
TASTE  mild, sweet^, (Thiers), not distinctive (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers), yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  "scattered or in groups on the ground in conifer forests, especially firs, at higher elevations, August-January"^, (Bessette), rare at lower elevations but not uncommon in the zone characterized by Abies lasiocarpa (Subalpine Fir) / Tsuga mertensiana (Mountain Hemlock), (R. Bishop, pers. comm.), summer, fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-17.5 x 4.5-5.5 um, elliptic to somewhat spindle-shaped to somewhat cylindric in face view, inequilateral and wider in middle in side view, smooth, thin-walled^, pale ochraceous in KOH, ochraceous in Melzer's; basidia 1-, 2-, and 4-spored, 22-27 x 8-10 um, clavate, colorless, guttulate in KOH, hymenium colorless with scattered yellow areas in KOH, bright rust-red color in Melzer's; hymenial cystidia scattered to rare, inconspicuous, 40-50 x 4-7 um, cylindric to pyriform (pear-shaped) to subclavate to subfusoid, colorless in KOH and Melzer's, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a tangled trichodermium of hyphae with occasional erect hyphal tips, hyaline spiral incrustations on most hyphae, contents staining bright red to tawny in Melzer's, hyphae 8-10 um wide"; stem cuticle composed largely of fertile basidia; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 14-18 x 4.5-6 um, elliptic to subcylindric, smooth, pale ochraceous, (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  means "dwelling with Abies [fir]"
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus persolidus (established for California) differs in cap color, does not show the imbricated fibrillose scales on cap, is not noted to be associated with fir, and has slightly smaller spores and differently shaped cystidia, (Thiers). See also SIMILAR section of Butyriboletus autumniregius, Butyriboletus primiregius, and Butyriboletus querciregius.
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus abieticola), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus abieticola), Arora(3), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus abieticola), Siegel(2)*, McAdoo(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Butyriboletus autumniregius  D. Arora & J.L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 475. 2014;  Boletus regius  Krombh.  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) large size, 2) cap that is rose red or vinaceous red or at times mottled with tan or yellow, dry, and bald or breaking up into small scales, 3) flesh that is yellow but vinaceous tinged in the stem base, 4) small yellow pores that become greenish, and turn blue when bruised, 5) bulbous to equal stem that is yellow with dark red stains toward base, and yellow-reticulate in upper part, often staining blue when bruised or rubbed, 6) fruiting under conifers and possibly hardwoods, generally in the fall, in mountains or lowlands, and 7) spores with length to width ratio 3.5.^ | The descriptions is derived from Arora(3). RANGE ^Butyriboletus autumniregius collections were examined from CA and OR, (Arora(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  8-30cm across, convex becoming broadly convex when mature or sometimes flat; "wholly or mostly rose red to vinaceous red or at times mottled with tan or yellow"; dry or slightly tacky but not viscid, bald or "breaking up to form many small scales" (the scales typically smaller than in Butyriboletus abieticola)
FLESH  firm, dense, thick; "pale yellow, typically unchanging in the button stage but in age usually turning blue at least somewhat when cut (often slowly and erratically)"; in stem, "firm, pale yellow except for the base, which usually has a distinct vinaceous tinge, turning blue slightly or not at all when cut"
PORES  typically less than 1 millimeter wide, pore surface usually the same color as the tubes which are "pale yellow becoming bright yellow and eventually olive yellow or greenish", pore surface "typically turning blue quickly when bruised except in the button stage"; tubes at first short in relation to the cap flesh but 1-2.5cm or longer when mature
STEM  5-15(25)cm x 3-7cm at top, usually clavate [club-shaped] "and sometimes markedly bulbous when young, but often less so (at times equal) with age"; "butter yellow to bright yellow throughout or commonly with dark red (rhubarb red) stains toward the base, often bruising blue when touched or rubbed"; finely reticulate over at least upper part, "the reticulation yellow or occasionally reddish but often becoming brown after spores are shed"
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or in groups on the ground in coastal and mid-elevation forests, "associated with conifers and possibly also with hardwoods", has been observed in pure stands of Douglas-fir; fruiting generally in the fall (September to December)
SPORE DEPOSIT  spores dark olive brown in mass, at least under microscope
MICROSCOPIC  spores (12)13-16.5(18) x 4-5 um (length to width ratio 3.5), fusoid, smooth, thin-walled, "pale brown or yellowish in KOH"^; basidia 4-spored, clavate; "hymenial cystidia fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an interwoven trichodermium with few erect hyphal tips"
NAME ORIGIN  "autumni" refers to autumn fruiting, and "regius" means "kingly" and also refers to the European Butyriboletus regius
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus primiregius is best distinguished by fruiting in the spring. The cap has a pronounced tendency, lacking in B. autumniregius, to fade to dingy olive brown or paler when it is exposed to sunlight, but this could be environmental. (Arora(3)). |Butyriboletus querciregius 1) grows with oak, 2) has shorter spores and a smaller length to width ratio (3.0), and 3) has a less vivid cap color (pinkish red, pinkish brown, or yellowish when fresh as opposed to tending toward deep rose red or vinaceous red when fresh). The cap color of the cap of B. querciregius is also referred to as "red or dull red or even orange-red, sometimes blotched with yellow before fading to pinkish brown, tan or yellowish". (Arora(3)). |Butyriboletus abieticola has fibrillose scales when mature. (Arora(3)). |Butyriboletus regius, known from Europe turns blue only rarely when cut. (Arora(3)).
SOURCES  Arora(3)*
