CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH: "dingy olive-green flash, quickly becoming golden orange on pileus, dingy orange-brown on tubes, yellowish on stipe, no reaction on context"
CAP  2-6(8)cm across, "rounded-convex to broadly convex when young", to nearly flat or irregular or wavy when old; "rosy red, red, pinkish red to pinkish"; "dry to moist, pubescent when young, becoming smooth"^, (Frank), 2-4cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat to "irregularly lobed, incised or convoluted at maturity", margin downcurved; purple red, sometimes slightly paler, unchanging or becoming slightly paler when old; dry to moist, typically finely velvety to red-pubescent when young, usually bald when old, plush-like in appearance, sometimes finely areolate [finely cracked like dried mud] near margin, (Thiers)
FLESH  "firm, moderately thick to thin" in cap, pale yellow or yellowish brown in stem base, "not staining when cut"^, (Frank), 0.5-1cm thick, yellow, "usually unchanging, but sometimes slowly and erratically changing to blue", in stem yellow, unchanging with exposure, (Thiers)
PORES  "small and irregular at first", expanding when old; "dull yellow at first, to yellow or greenish yellow", becoming olive yellow when old, "not staining blue on younger specimens, occasionally bruising bluish green" when old; tube layer sunken around stem "and rather short at margin"^, (Frank), 1-3 per mm, angular, adnate to depressed, seceding; bright yellow, unchanging when bruised; tube layer 0.2-0.5cm thick, dull yellow, unchanging when exposed, (Thiers)
STEM  1.5-7cm x 0.5-2(3)cm, "equal, peg-like", narrowing toward base to irregular; "pinkish red to pale reddish brown over yellow"; "often streaked with longitudinal striations to appressed-fibrillose, finely punctate at apex, to smooth"^, (Frank), 1.5-4.5 x 0.5-1cm, equal or narrowing downward, stuffed to solid; red, yellowish at base; dry, appressed-fibrillose becoming bald, smooth or with longitudinal striations, not reticulate [not netted], light yellow mycelium at base, (Thiers)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Frank), not distinctive (Thiers)
TASTE  mild^, (Frank, Thiers)
EDIBILITY  undetermined (Thiers)
HABITAT  single or in small clusters, in the Pacific Northwest often in stands of Populus trichocarpa (Black Cottonwood), on the California coast under Notholithocarpus densiflorus (Tanoak), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), Alnus rubra (Red Alder), and Quercus agrifolia (California Live Oak), in the Sierra Nevada in mixed forest with Abies concolor (White Fir), A. magnifica (California Red Fir), Pinus contorta (Lodgepole Pine), and Populus tremuloides (Quaking Aspen); late summer and fall^, (Frank), gregarious to subcespitose [almost tufted] under conifers, (Thiers)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Frank, Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-17.5 x 5-7 um, cylindric, oval to elliptic in face view, "often somewhat variable in shape and size", smooth, moderately thick-walled, "ocher in KOH"^; basidia 1-spored to 4-spored, 23-28 x 7-10 um, "narrowly clavate", colorless; hymenial cystidia absent; cap cuticle a tangled trichoderm of hyphae 5-7 um wide, "staining dark yellow in KOH"; clamp connections absent, (Frank), spores 11.2-17.6 x 5.3-7 um, cylindric, to oval to elliptic, very variable in size and shape, smooth, often dextrinoid, ochraceous in KOH, moderately thick-walled; basidia apparently 1-spored, 2-spored, 3-spored and 4-spored, 23-28 x 7-10 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia "not apparent, but occasionally basidioid cells staining dark brown in Melzer's scattered in hymenium"; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium of more or less repent hyphae 5-7 um wide, walls asperulate to faintly incrusted; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "becoming rose red (when dried)"^, (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus species lack the rosy red to pink cap, may have cracking in the cap (not typical for H. coccyginus), and have flesh that may turn blue, (Frank).
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus), Both(1) (as Boletus), Siegel(2)* (as "Boletus" coccyginus), Frank(9)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Hortiboletus 'rubellus'  (Krombh.) Simonini, Vizzini & Gelardi^  in Vizzini, Index Fungorum 244: 1 2015;  Boletus rubellus  Krombh.  Naturgetr. Abbild. Beschr. Schwamme (Prague) 5: 4. 1836;  Xerocomus rubellus  (Krombh.) Quel.;  Xerocomellus rubellus  (Krombh.) Sutara
ENGLISH NAME(S)  ruby bolete
NOTES  Features include a red, dry, velvety cap that often becomes finely cracked, yellow flesh that turns slowly and slightly blue when cut, small, yellow pores then turn somewhat blue when bruised, more or less cylindric stem that is yellow at the top and reddish in the lower part, and growth near hardwoods.^ |According to D. Miller, pers. comm., the Pacific Northwest material is not the same as European material and will need a new name. RANGE ^Hortiboletus rubellus is found in North America, Europe, North Africa, and Asia, (Breitenbach), and the United Kingdom (Bon). The range in North America is given by Bessette(3) as "eastern North America west to Minnesota", but Trudell(4) say that several Pacific Northwest collections fit H. rubellus well. Trudell(4) say, "There is a good chance that boletes in this group have been introduced into our area. They often occur during summer in grassy areas, mossy lawns, or along the edges of trails, always near trees such as oaks, cottonwood, willow, and basswood or linden." Hortiboletus rubellus [or something close] has been collected from Seattle, WA (S. Trudell, pers. comm.). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains dull orange with KOH, and olive-green with FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  2-8cm across, convex, becoming nearly flat when old, margin even; dark red when young, fading to brick-red when old; dry, velvety-subtomentose, often becoming finely rimose [cracked]^ when mature, (Bessette(3)), 3-5cm across, hemispheric - bell-shaped when young, later convex to flat, somewhat undulating when old; "strikingly blood-red when young, later fading and brown-red"; dry, finely tomentose, (Breitenbach), 3-8cm across, broadly convex; brick-red; dry, velvety, finally areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith), scarlet red when young, becoming dull olivaceous red when old, margin often yellowish; dry and velvety, finally bald, and often areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Phillips), 5-7cm across, scarlet to vinaceous red; smooth, (Bon)
FLESH  "bright yellow, slowly staining bluish green on exposure"; in stem "yellow, slowly staining bluish green, reddish orange in the base"^, (Bessette(3)), thick, soft; yellowish, blueing slightly when cut, (Breitenbach), pale, only slowly tinged blue (Bon)
PORES  1 to 2 per mm, angular, thick-walled, "yellow, staining bluish green when bruised"; tube layer 0.6-1cm deep, typically depressed when mature^, (Bessette(3)), lemon yellow to gold-yellow when young, "later with an olive tint, bluing somewhat when touched"; tubes 0.5-1cm deep, greenish yellow, the layer "somewhat notched and sometimes decurrent as thin ridges", (Breitenbach), small; pale yellow, (Bon)
STEM  3-8cm x 0.5-1.3cm, narrowing downward or nearly equal, often with a narrowed base; yellow at top, "punctate with dull reddish orange dots and points" from the base to the top, "darkening to brown where handled"^, (Bessette(3)), 4-10cm x 0.7-1.5(2)cm, cylindric to fusiform-clavate; light yellow at top, increasingly red to brown-red downward, base and mycelium chrome yellow, blueing slightly when bruised; punctate at top, increasingly longitudinally fibrillose downward, (Breitenbach), equal; bright yellow at top, shading to bright rose red or scarlet in lower part, with yellow basal mycelium, (Phillips), 6cm x 1cm, yellow, spotted red in lower part; smooth, (Bon)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3), Breitenbach), pleasant (Phillips)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3)), mild, sourish, (Breitenbach), slightly soapy (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette(3))
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on the ground in hardwood forests and mixed woods, especially under oak and beech; July to October^, (Bessette(3)), single to gregarious, in mixed hardwood forests, parks, gardens, and floodplain forests, primarily on calcareous soils; summer to fall, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown (Bessette, Phillips), yellow-brown, (Breitenbach)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-13 x 4-5 um, "elliptic, smooth, pale brown"^; cap cuticle a trichodermium of long, tubular, tapered, colorless hyphae, 5-12 um wide and not conspicuously inflated, with dingy ochraceous content in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette(3)), spores 9.5-12.5 x 4.2-6 um, elliptic, smooth, thick-walled, yellowish; basidia 4-spored, 35-45 x 9-13 um, clavate, without basal clamp connection; pleurocystidia and cheilocystidia 35-62 x 8.5-11 um, cylindric-lageniform, some with faintly yellow contents; cap cuticle a trichoderm of mostly erect hyphae 6-12 um wide, "some with reddish incrustation", septa without clamp connections, (Breitenbach), cap cuticle a trichodermium with hyphae 5-11 um wide, walls smooth to minutely roughened, end cells cylindric to subcapitate, 8-25 um broad where enlarged, (Smith), spores up to 12-13 um long, thick-walled; "cap cuticle composed of erect septate hyphae with tapered ends and some encrusted walls", (Trudell)
NAME ORIGIN  'rubellus' is diminutive of 'ruber', Latin for 'red'
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus diffractus and Boletus truncatus are less brightly colored, (Trudell(4)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus rubellus), Breitenbach(3)* (as Xerocomus rubellus), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bon(2) (as Xerocomus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Smith(4) (as Boletus rubellus), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bessette(2)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bacon(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Buczacki(1)* (as Xerocomus)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum atrostipitatum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 155-157. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dull orange to pinkish tan or brownish cap (pale and rather dull colored) that is often patchy when old, 2) flesh that stains pink then purple-gray to blackish, and with FeSO4 turns bluish gray, 3) pores that are pale when young when young, becoming dingier when old, unchanging or staining olive to brownish when bruised, 4) a stem with dense black scabers when young and when mature, and 5) growth with birch.^ | The online Species Fungorum, accessed February 12, 2018, considered this taxon a synonym of Leccinum versipelle (Fr. & Hok) Snell, but MycoBank, accessed the same day, maintained them separately. RANGE ^The distribution is eastern Canada to MN, CO to WA, southern range unknown, (Bessette), AK (Dick(1)), and NS (Grund(11)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh bluish gray with FeSO4; flesh bluish with NH4OH, (Smith)
CAP  6-16cm, convex, margin at first with thin flaps of tissue, at least when young; "dull orange to tan or brownish"; dry to slightly viscid, "fibrillose, breaking up to form downy patches or small scales" when old^, (Bessette), 6-13(18)cm, hemispheric to broadly convex; pale dull orange-buff, "slowly becoming pale orange brownish and finally a dingy pinkish tan", dry, appressed-fibrillose, "becoming obscurely to distinctly appressed-squamulose by maturity, the outer fibrils often grayish", the squamules finally merely brownish; margin exceeding tubes by 0.5cm and this band soon breaking up into segments, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, staining pinkish, then purple-gray to blackish when exposed", especially where cap joins stem^, (Bessette), "white, when cut soon vinaceous-buff and this progressing to violaceous-fuscous", in stem white, color change the same but sometimes staining blue-green in addition toward the base, (Smith)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round, "whitish to buff or pale gray when young, becoming dingier in age, unchanging or staining olive to brownish when bruised"; tube layer 1-1.8cm thick^, (Bessette), minute, adnate-seceding; "staining olive to olive-brown and if severely bruised avellaneous or darker"; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, "olivaceous-pallid when young, then dingy honey-brown and finally wood-brown", (Smith)
STEM  8-15cm x 1-2.5cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; dense layer of scabers that are black when fruitbody young and when mature, on whitish to dingy tan ground color, blue or blue green stains occasionally on lower part; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette), 8-15cm x 1-2.5cm at top, up to 4cm wide at base, clavate [club-shaped] becoming equal when old, solid, hard, fibrous; "coarse black ornamentation in the form of points and squamules from button stages to maturity and from base to apex, with a cottony-fibrillose layer beneath this", base may stain bluish green when injured, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  single to scattered under hardwoods, usually birch, July to September^, (Bessette), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), dull yellow-brown (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-17 x 4-5 um, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^; end cells on cap cuticle tubular, yellowish, lacking orange brown granules in Melzer's; caulocystidia clavate to fusoid-ventricose (Bessette), spores 13-17 x 4-5 um, smooth, subfusiform, pale yellow brown in KOH and not changing much in Melzer's reagent, wall slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 8-12 um wide, colorless in KOH; pleurocystidia "scattered, 30-40 X 8-12 um, fusoid-ventricose, with dull yellow-brown content when revived in KOH or in Melzer's, smooth, thin-walled", cheilocystidia "26-35 X 6-10 um, fusoid-ventricose to clavate, ochraceous in KOH"; cap cuticle of tangled filaments, in KOH the hyphae with yellow to colorless content, when revived in Melzer's yellowish without pigment globules but content granular to homogeneous, hyphae "with cells tubular to somewhat inflated, mostly 6-11 um wide, some rather short, finally disarticulating, the end cells tubular to either narrowly clavate or tapered to apex, walls smooth to minutely asperulate"; caulocystidia "40-120 X 10-25 um, clavate to fusoid-ventricose, content bister in KOH"; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with black stem'
SIMILAR  Leccinum versipelle has a brighter orange cap that is less scaly when old, a darker pore surface when young, flesh that stains bright green with FeSO4, and different caulocystidia, (Bessette). L. versipelle is less associated with birch, has a less patchy cap at maturity, and has different caulocystidia, (Smith).
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Dick(1), Grund(11)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum aurantiacum  (Bull.) Gray  group^  Nat. Arr. Brit. Pl. I: 646. 1821
ENGLISH NAME(S)  red-capped scaber-stalk, red-capped scaberstalk, orange-capped scaber-stalk, orange-capped scaberstalk, aspen scaber-stalk, aspen scaberstalk, orange-capped bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a brick-red cap, 2) flesh that turns reddish before becoming bluish black, and 3) the development of pigment globules in cells of the cap cuticle hyphae in Melzer's reagent. The name Leccinum aurantiacum was formerly applied to several similar species, and its presence in North America is disputed.^ | See Kuo(5) (accessed July 10, 2013) under "Leccinum: Uncertain Taxa" for a more detailed discussion. Trudell(4) comment in relation to the Pacific Northwest (Latin names italicized) "A large, often abundant species that has been called Leccinum aurantiacum occurs in our conifer forests. That name, however, is incorrect since L. aurantiacum has been shown to be associated with hardwoods. Our species may be closer to L. vulpinum described from Europe.". RANGE ^Leccinum aurantiacum is said to be "widely distributed across the northern United States and Canada, southern limits unknown", (Bessette). Various observers give a range that includes CA (rare), (Thiers(1)), AK (Dick(1)), CO (Lincoff(2)), WA (reported by numerous observers), OR (R. Bishop, pers. comm.), ID (E. Butler, pers. comm.), MT (L. Evans, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains very pale blue with application of FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  5-20.5cm, convex to broadly convex, to nearly flat when old; "orange to brick-red, rusty red, or reddish brown"; dry, appressed-fibrillose when young, often bald when old; "margin with thin flaps of sterile tissue when young"^, (Bessette), 8-15cm, convex to broadly convex or flat-convex; bright rust brown to brick red; "dry, dull, uneven, or roughened, finely to matted-fibrillose when young", unchanging or appearing bald when older, "sometimes finely and obscurely areolate"; margin with conspicuous sterile band, (Thiers(1)), bright to dull ferruginous-red but often with a pallid area where covered by debris; dry and uneven, roughened or appressed-fibrillose, when old more or less bald and subviscid, (Smith(35)), red to reddish orange-brown or dark reddish brown; fibrillose, (Trudell)
FLESH  "white, slowly staining pinkish to wine-red then gradually darkening to purple-gray or blackish when exposed", especially at junction between cap and stem^, (Bessette), 2-3cm thick, white when first exposed, changing to vinaceous to near flesh color, then gray to bluish gray or black, (Thiers(1)), white, when cut slowly staining vinaceous then grayish and then slowly fuscous, in the stem when cut "pallid at first but staining to fuscous in the upper part, sometimes staining caerulean blue in the base and in places strongly reddish to red-brown", (Smith(35)), whitish, "changes slightly or more distinctly to grayish or blackish, sometimes after going through a vinaceous gray phase", (Trudell)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "whitish when young, becoming dingier and grayish to olive-buff to pale olive-brown in age, unchanging or staining olive, brownish or burgundy-brown when cut or bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick^, (Bessette), 1-2 per mm, angular; "staining olive to olive brown to vinaceous brown when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, adnate to depressed when young, becoming deeply and narrowly depressed when old, when young pale olive, darkening to olive drab or becoming brown at maturity, (Thiers(1)), "when lightly bruised pores staining olive to olive-brown but where severely bruised stained avellaneous or darker", (Smith(35))
STEM  10-16cm x 2-3cm, equal or widening downward, solid; "whitish, with whitish to buff scabers that darken to orange-brown to reddish brown and finally blackish brown, at least over the lower portion, occasionally with blue-green or yellowish stains on the lower portion"; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 10-17cm x 2-3cm at top, clavate [club-shaped] to ventricose [wider in middle], becoming equal, often narrowed at base and top, solid; whitish to white, squamules pallid at first, black or dark brown when old, occasionally staining blue in base; dry, (Thiers(1)), with black scabers, base blueing, (Trudell)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under aspen or pine, July to September^, (Bessette), summer and fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette, Thiers), dark yellow-brown to dull cinnamon (Smith(35))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-18 x 3.5-5 um, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^; end cells of cap cuticle "narrowly clavate to tubular or slightly tapered, content orange-brown and forming globules of various sizes in Melzer's solution"; caulocystidia clavate to cylindric, (Bessette), spores 12-13.5 x 3.5-4.5 um, (up to 28 um long from 1-spored basidia), fusoid to subelliptic, smooth, pale ochraceous in KOH, yellow brown in Melzer's reagent, thin-walled; basidia 1-spored to 4-spored, 30-55 x 9-11 um, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered, deeply embedded, 30-40 x 9-11 um, clavate to fusoid, colorless in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a tangled trichoderm, pale ochraceous in KOH, hyphae 7-11 um wide, considerable disarticulation of cells noted, walls often asperulate, pigment globules formed when mounted in Melzer's reagent, terminal cells not strongly differentiated, no short intermediate cells present"; caulocystidia "38-48 X 8-13 um, dark brown in KOH, clavate, with large fusoid-ventricose cells interspersed"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 13-16(18) x 3.5-4.5(5) um, subfusiform, smooth; basidia 4-spored, 8-9 um broad, clavate; pleurocystidia rare to scattered, 35-60 x 8-12 um, apices obtuse, cheilocystidia 18-26 x 5-8(10) um, clavate to subfusoid; cap cuticle "of appressed interwoven hyphae 5-12(15) um wide, cells elongate (mostly more than 5 times as long as broad), walls thin, smooth, some minutely roughened, the cells disarticulating to some extent; the end-cells narrowly clavate to tubular or near apex slightly tapered", in Melzer's the content orange-brown and forming globules often filling the cells; caulocystidia mostly clavate, 9-30 um wide, a few cylindric, smooth, (Smith(35))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "golden orange"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum discolor has duller cap colors (dull orange brown to dull cinnamon), and lacks orange-brown pigment globules in the cap cuticle hyphae when revived in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette). Leccinum insigne has flesh that stains bluish gray to blackish without an intervening reddish or pinkish phase, and grows under birch as well as aspen, (Bessette). L. insigne is ornamented by maturity with black scabers, but Leccinum aurantiacum may not reach this state till old age; hyphae of the cap cuticle of L. aurantiacum are relatively smooth in contrast to those of L. insigne, (Smith(35)). Leccinum idahoense has a dark liver-brown granulose-areolate cap, is very slow change to gray when the flesh is cut, and has a relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit, (Smith(37)).
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Thiers(13), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4), Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Dick(1), Smith(37), Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Kuo(5), MacKinnon(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum brunneum  Thiers^  Mycologia 63: 269. 1971
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dull dark brown cap with marginal flaps of tissue, 2) flesh that turns fuscous with no intermediate reddish phase, and sometimes becomes blue in base, 3) white pores that become pale olive and bruise brown, 4) stem with scabers that are white to darker, and 5) growth under aspen.^ | Note that this is not the same as Leccinum insigne var. brunneum Thiers, which was described from California in the same paper. The description derived from Thiers(1) except where specified. RANGE ^L. brunneum was described from CA but was also reported from ID by Dr. H. Thiers who described it (pers. comm. to L. Norvell, 1986).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^no distinctive reactions observed (Thiers(13))
CAP  6-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex to cushion-shaped to irregular in outline; dark brown ("bister" to "warm sepia" to "Prout's brown", colors from Ridgway), sometimes somewhat paler brown when old ("cinnamon-brown" to "Verona brown" to "snuff brown"), drying dark dull brown; dry, but may be viscid or subviscid in wet weather, dull, bald to finely subtomentose [somewhat tomentose] or obscurely appressed-fibrillose, occasionally becoming somewhat fibrillose when old; margin with conspicuous sterile flaps of tissue
FLESH  1-2.5cm thick, "white when first exposed, changing to fuscous with no reddish intermediate phase, fuscous color sometimes fading after prolonged exposure"; in stem, white when first exposed, becoming fuscous directly as in cap, sometimes becoming blue in base
PORES  up to 1mm, angular, colored as tubes; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed, white when young becoming pale olive when mature, staining brown when bruised, color fading in some after bruising
STEM  8-15cm x 1.5-3(4)cm at top, club-shaped, solid; dry, white ground color, covered with small squamules that are white at first becoming darker with maturity
ODOR  mild
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers)
HABITAT  gregarious to scattered on ground under aspen
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores (14)15.5-19.5 x 4-6 um, fusoid to subcylindric [nearly cylindric] to subelliptic [nearly elliptic], smooth, some dextrinoid, in KOH ochraceous, walls moderately thick^; basidia 4-spored, 30-35 x 7-11 um, clavate to pear-shaped, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered to abundant, often inconspicuous, 35-50 x 8-12 um, fusoid to fusoid-ventricose to clavate, some staining pale brown in KOH and dark reddish brown in Melzer's reagent; cap cuticle a trichodermium of tangled hyphal tips, pale ochraceous in KOH, tawny to russet in Melzer's reagent, hyphae 11-15 um wide, walls sometimes asperulate, pigment globules not forming when mounted in Melzer's reagent, "terminal cells relatively long and narrow, tapered somewhat at the tip"; caulocystidia "24-37 x 7-12 um, clavate to fusoid to clavate with elongated, tapering apices", typically dark brown, but sometimes colorless in KOH; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means 'brown'
SIMILAR  Leccinum fibrillosum can be confused according to Thiers, but cut flesh turns fuscous with an intermediate reddish phase, the cap is normally more red-brown and more fibrillose, and growth is under conifers. See also SIMILAR section of Leccinum scabrum.
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Thiers(13), Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum clavatum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 135. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include a whitish to tan, somewhat viscid cap, white flesh that stains directly bluish gray when cut, whitish pores that bruise brown, a club-shaped stem with scabers that slowly become dull brown, and growth under conifers.^ RANGE ^Leccinum clavatum is known only from the Pacific Northwest and the northern Rocky Mountains, (Bessette). The type from ID, and it also occurs in CA, (Both).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  8-20cm, pallid becoming crust-brown; dry becoming subviscid [somewhat viscid], appressed fibrillose when young, (Smith(4)), whitish to tan, with band of sterile tissue on the margin, (Bessette)^, 8-20cm, "convex becoming broadly convex, margin appendiculate as in L. aurantiacum", cap margin often intergrown with stem; white to pallid ("tilleul-buff"), when young, gradually becoming pale tan ("pinkish-buff" to "cinnamon-buff") on disc or over all when old; "typically dry, when old and moist somewhat viscid, appressed fibrillose-tomentose", the fibrils becoming aggregated into small appressed scales over all when old, areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith(38))
FLESH  white, staining directly bluish gray in young specimens when cut^, (Smith), white, slowly stains fuscous without a reddish phase when exposed, (Bessette), 2-3cm thick, soft and spongy; white, turning bluish gray in young specimens when cut, unchanging in old caps; in stem white "except for the yellowish to greenish-yellow base in old specimens", (Smith(38))
PORES  2 per mm, whitish at first, bruising dingy yellowish then dingy brown^, (Smith(4)), 2 per mm, round, not stuffed, with thin walls; "whitish, gradually yellowish or if bruised sordid olive-yellow, when young quickly changing to brownish where bruised", tube layer 1-2cm thick, "depressed, whitish when young, soon dingy avellaneous, changing to brownish where bruised", (Smith(38))
STEM  4-8cm x 1.5-3cm, clavate, up to 7cm thick at base, white with scabers that slowly become dull brown^, (Smith(4)), 4-8cm x 1.5-3cm, bulbous base up to 7cm wide, solid; 'white at first, densely scabrous, scabrosity slowly darkening to dull brown, in old specimens surface may appear coarsely fibrillose with the fibrils toward the base dark brown, staining blue where cut or handled ("light methyl blue")', note young buttons did not give this change but in time changed to pinkish, (Smith(38))
ODOR  faintly fragrant^ (Smith(38))
TASTE  mild to nutty^ (Smith(38))
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  type gregarious under spruce and fir^, (Smith(38))
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark rusty brown^ ("Mars brown"), (Smith(38))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-18(19) x 4-5 um^, end cells of cap cuticle tubular to narrowly clavate; caulocystidia clavate, mucronate or fusoid-ventricose, all mixed together, (Smith(4)), spores 14-18(19) x 4-5 um, narrowly subfusoid, smooth, yellowish or a few reddish tawny in Melzer's reagent, yellowish transparent to dingy ochraceous revived in KOH, wall relatively thin; basidia 4-spored, 28-34 x 8-10 um, very delicate and soon collapsing; pleurocystidia "scattered, 34-46 x 8-12 um, fusoid-ventricose with tapered curved neck and subacute apices", colorless to yellowish or darker as revived, smooth, thin-walled, cheilocystidia "similar to pleurocystidia but neck less elongated"; caulocystidia 36-57 x 10-16(20) um, clavate to fusoid-ventricose or mucronate, content pale ochraceous, walls smooth and thin, caulobasidia [basidia on the stem] and basidioles "18-33 x 7-12 um, content ochraceous, often merely in the form of unenlarged hyphal tips but mostly clavate"; cap cutis a matted down aggregation of fibrils with content yellowish to yellowish-brown when revived in KOH and yellowish transparent in Melzer's reagent, the end-cells 5-15 um wide and tubular to narrowly clavate, hyphal cells tending to disarticulate in mounts from old caps, walls smooth and thin; hyphae of context 10-15(20) um wide, interwoven, yellowish transparent to pale orange when revived in Melzer's reagent; clamp connections none, (Smith(38))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'clavate' or 'club-shaped', referring to stem
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Smith(38), Smith(4), Bessette(3), Both(1)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum cyaneobasileucum  Lannoy & Estades^  Docums Mycol. 21(no. 81): 23. 1991;  Leccinum brunneogriseolum  Lannoy & Estades
ENGLISH NAME(S)  bluing birch bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark grayish brown to tan-colored cap that is suedelike on the surface, 2) flesh that does not turn blue above the stem base, 3) pore surface that is creamy white to beige when young with small pores, the tubes depressed, 4) a club-shaped whitish stem that is densely covered with small plaques and scabers at first that gradually blacken, the stem base often with blue-green blotches and slowly bluing when scraped, and 5) growth with birches, often in urban areas.^ RANGE ^Leccinum cyaneobasileucum is found in CA (Siegel(2)) and also occurs in the Pacific Northwest at least in OR, (N. Siegel, pers. comm.). It was described from France and occurs elsewhere in Europe including (Buczacki(1)) Britain/Ireland.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH orange on cap, yellow in stem flesh
CAP  4-12cm across, "hemispheric at first, soon bun shaped, to broadly convex", sometimes uplifted with a wavy margin when old; dark gray to grayish brown or tan, "sometimes paler beige", "usually with a narrow white band around margin"; "moist to dry, suedelike to finely tomentose, often bumpy and rough at first, becoming nearly smooth"^, (Siegel), 5-16cm, hazel-brown, fulvous-brown, or walnut-brown; dry but may be tacky in wet weather, (Phillips)
FLESH  solid; whitish marbled with beige, unchanging^, (Siegel), "white, flushing pale pink, sometimes blue-green" (Phillips), "soft, unchanging or becoming slightly pinkish in cap and stem apex", usually "with bluish staining in base and where damaged by snails or insects", (Buczacki)
PORES  "round, tiny at first", staying small when old; creamy white to beige when young, "soon pale buff", dingy when old; tubes deeply depressed at stem, relatively short at first, becoming long^, (Siegel), pores small, angular, white-gray, bruising brownish; tubes white-grayish (Buczacki)
STEM  5-15cm x 1-3cm, cylindric "with a swollen, club-shaped base, usually tall and straight"; white to creamy white, "but covered in small plaques and scabers" which are "very pale and dense at first but soon become light gray to dusky gray and then nearly black and more spread out" as the stem elongates, base "often with bright teal to blue-green blotches, and slowly bluing when scraped"^, (Siegel), 7-20cm x 0.8-2.0cm (Phillips), the dense covering of white woolly scales may later turn grayish and cause the entire stem to appear woolly (Buczacki)
ODOR  indistinct
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  edible, "and with a good flavor, but often quite slimy in texture", (Siegel)
HABITAT  single or scattered in troops, "sometimes in large numbers on urban lawns under planted birches"; in California Redwood Coast frequently found with Russula versicolor, Paxillus cuprinus, Lactarius pubescens var. betulae, and Cortinarius hemitrichus, and common in late summer and early fall^, (Siegel), with birch in moss, but not normally in Sphagnum, summer to fall, (Phillips), on soil and peat on heathland, moorland and other acidic sites with birch, sometimes among mosses although often in drier habitats and not usually with Sphagnum, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dusky brown^ (Siegel), walnut brown (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-21 x 3.5-7 um, "spindle-shaped, smooth"^
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Leccinum scabrum also occurs under urban birches but "has a dark brown cap when young and darker, denser scabers, and lacks the blue-green stains on the stipe", (Siegel(2)).
SOURCES  Siegel(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Phillips(3)* (including the white variation)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum discolor  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 152-153. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry to subviscid cap that is orange brown and nearly bald, 2) white flesh that changes to pinkish then blackish, 3) white pores that become pale olive-brown and when bruised stain dark brown, 4) a white stem with conspicuous scabers that turn from whitish to dark brown or black, 5) growth under pine and aspen, and 6) microscopic characters including the absence of orange-brown pigment globules in the cap cuticle hyphae.^ | Leccinum discolor is one of the species previously identified as L. aurantiacum (Theirs(13)). RANGE ^It is found from eastern Canada west to ID, MT, WY, CO, and CA, (Bessette). It has been reported from BC (M. Beug, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  7-15cm, hemispheric to convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; "dull orange-brown to orange-cinnamon or orange-yellow", becoming dull cinnamon when old; dry to subviscid, nearly bald; margin with flaps of sterile tissue^, (Bessette), 10-15cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex to depressed on disc to occasionally irregular in outline; brown to orange-red to sometimes pale buff; "dry to subviscid, dull, often shallowly pitted or reticulate", bald "but often appearing tomentose or fibrillose", sometimes cracked-areolate [cracked like dried mud] near margin; margin with typical sterile flaps, (Thiers(1))
FLESH  "white when exposed, staining pinkish to brownish orange, then fuscous"^, (Bessette), 1-3cm thick, white when first exposed, changing to flesh color to pink then blackish, (Thiers(1))
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular; "white at first, becoming greenish yellow to pale olive-brown", "staining dark brown when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, becoming depressed near stem when mature^, (Bessette), 0.5-1mm, angular, colored as tubes; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed, white when young, becoming pale olive-brown when mature, staining dark brown when bruised, (Thiers(1))
STEM  6-12cm x 1-3cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; with conspicuous scabers that are whitish when young and dark brown to black when old over whitish ground color; dry; basal mycelium white; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 6-12cm x 1-3cm at top, equal to club-shaped, solid; white to whitish ground color, conspicuously ornamented, squamules white to pallid when young, becoming dark brown to black when old; dry; white mycelium at base, (Thiers(1))
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers(1))
TASTE  mild^ (Thiers(1))
EDIBILITY  reported by Theirs(1) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on ground under pine and aspen^, (Bessette), single to scattered in soil under aspen (Thiers(13))
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette, Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 15-20 x 4.5-6.5 um, fusoid [spindle-shaped] to subcylindric [nearly cylindric], smooth, some producing a dextrinoid reaction, ochraceous^; cap cuticle hyphae lacking orange-brown pigment globules when revived in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette), spores 15.5-20 x 4.5-6.5 um, fusoid to subcylindric, smooth, ochraceous in KOH and Melzer's reagent, some showing a dextrinoid reaction, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 30-37 x 9-12 um, clavate to pear-shaped, colorless and with granular contents in KOH; hymenial cystidia 30-46 x 8-15 um, "fusoid to clavate with elongated, tapering apices", colorless to sometimes appearing filled with dark brown amorphous material in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium of free hyphal tips, buff in KOH, ochraceous tawny in Melzer's reagent, "hyphae 8-10 um wide, walls smooth to asperulate and sometimes appearing incrusted, considerable disarticulation of cells noted, terminal cells only slightly narrowed at apex"; caulocystidia 25-45 x 10-17 um, "clavate to mucronate to subfusoid", mostly dark brown in KOH but some colorless cells present, "an occasional large cell interspersed", walls smooth, thin; clamp connections absent, (Thiers(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means "of a different color", (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum aurantiacum has an abundance of pigment globules formed within the cells of the cap cuticle, whereas in L. discolor the contents remain homogeneous or only become finely granular, (Thiers). Leccinum insigne does not have a reddish phase in the discoloration of the exposed flesh, (Thiers).
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Thiers(13), Both(1), Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum fallax  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 146. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark red to ferruginous-red cap that is dry and appears fibrillose under a hand lens, 2) white flesh that slowly turns dingy vinaceous then dark gray when cut and that stains blue-green in the stem base, 3) pallid to grayish pores that bruise slightly yellowish to green then brownish, 4) a conspicuously club-shaped - bulbous stem that is white with scabers gradually becoming brownish to dark brown but not black, and 5) microscopic characters including pigment balls that form in Melzer's reagent.^ RANGE ^The type was found in ID.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  6-20cm, convex, dark red to ferruginous-red; dry, matted-fibrillose^, (Smith(4)), appears fibrillose only when examined under a lens (Bessette), 6-20cm, obtuse to convex expanding to broadly convex, margin appendiculate from sterile parts of overgrown cap margin; evenly rufous overall; dry, matted-fibrillose under hand lens, (Smith(38))
FLESH  thick; white, slowly staining dingy vinaceous then gray when cut^, (Smith(4)), thick, soft; "white slowly staining dingy grayish vinaceous where bruised or cut", in stem white, "staining reddish slowly when cut and finally going to blackish", staining bluish-green in base when damaged, (Smith(38))
PORES  "pallid to grayish, when bruised slightly staining yellowish to green and then brownish"^, (Smith(4)), small, round; "pallid to grayish staining slowly when bruised to yellowish green and then brownish or at times merely olive-yellowish"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed around stem, "whitish, slowly becoming pale avellaneous, finally near wood-brown", (Smith(38))
STEM  5-14cm x 1-4cm near top, short-clavate with base up to 8cm wide; "white at first, ornamentation gradually becoming brownish to dark brown finally but not black"^, (Smith(4)), 6-14cm x 1-4cm, clavate [club-shaped] bulb up to 8cm wide, becoming nearly equal when old, solid; white, staining bluish-green around the base when handled or damaged; stem surface "scabrous-roughened, the scabrosity gradually staining dark brownish but not blackish even when dried", (Smith(38))
ODOR  mild^ (Smith(38))
TASTE  mild^ (Smith(38))
EDIBILITY  orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  type gregarious under spruce^, (Smith(38))
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-17 x 3.2-4.5 um^, cap cuticle of hyphae having content aggregating into globules when mounted in Melzer's reagent, end-cells subcylindric to narrowly clavate; caulocystidia up to 20 um broad, mostly clavate, content colorless to yellowish in KOH, (Smith(4)), spores 14-17 x 3.2-4.5 um, narrowly subfusoid, smooth, pale to bright tan in Melzer's reagent, yellowish in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 26-37 x 8-10 um; pleurocystidia scattered, 44-60 x 9-15 um, fusoid-ventricose, obtuse to subacute, colorless or with ochraceous content, smooth, thin-walled, cheilocystidia 18-30 x 5-8 um, clavate to subfusoid, dull ochraceous in KOH; caulocystidia mostly clavate and 9-14 um wide but some up to 20 um wide, mostly not with a projecting neck, content colorless to yellowish in KOH, walls thin and smooth; cap cutis of appressed hyphae 4-11 um wide, with dull orange-brown content in KOH, in Melzer's reagent the content orange-cinnamon to orange-red and aggregating into pigment balls, cells not or only slightly constricted at the septa, "end-cell nearly equal in width throughout or narrowly clavate to a short-tapered apex, walls thin and many hyphae with asperulate walls"; context hyphae orange to red when revived in Melzer's; all hyphae inamyloid; clamp connections absent, (Smith(38)) 
NAME ORIGIN  means 'false'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Smith(38), Smith(4), Bessette(3)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum fibrillosum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 152-153. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry cap that is dark reddish brown or liver brown, conspicuously fibrillose at first becoming finely scaly, 2) white flesh that turns pinkish then purplish, 3) pores that are dingy buff when young and bruise yellow-brown, 4) a stem with dark blackish brown scabers, and 5) growth under conifers.^ | Leccinum fibrillosum is found in the Rocky Mountains west to WA and north to western Canada, (Bessette(3)). RANGE ^Distribution includes eastern WA, ID, MT, WY, CO, and AB, (Bessette(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh greenish blue with application of FeSO4, (Bessette(3))
CAP  6-25cm, obtuse to convex to flat, margin with band of sterile tissue; dark reddish brown fibrils and fine scales on paler reddish brown ground; dry^, (Bessette(3)), dry to sticky in moist weather; margin appendiculate with 1-2mm of sterile tissue, (Bessette(1)), 8-25cm, convex; dark liver brown; conspicuously fibrillose at first, changing from matted fibrillose to finely scaly, at times with overtone of grayish fibrils, (Smith)
FLESH  white, immediately flushing pink when exposed then changing to dull bluish red to bluish purple or fuscous^, (Bessette(3)), firm; white, immediately flushing pink in cap and upper stem when cut or bruised, "then changing to muddy bluish red to bluish purple or fuscous in 3 to 4 minutes, often nearly black after several minutes", (Bessette(1)), staining vinaceous then purple-drab (Smith)
PORES  1-2 per mm when mature, round to angular, buff to pale olivaceous, when old becoming olive-brown; staining brown when bruised; tube layer 0.5-1.7cm thick^, (Bessette(3)), at first 3-4 per mm, than 1-2mm when old, (Bessette(1)), dingy buff when young, staining yellow-brown where slightly injured, (Smith)
STEM  7-11cm x 2.5-4.5cm, nearly equal or narrowing or widening downward, solid; dark blackish brown scabers on white ground; dry; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette(3)), 4-12cm x 2-5cm, "densely coated with woolly-scabrous material that forms squamules that blacken", (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  scattered or grouped under conifers, especially Engelmann Spruce and Lodgepole Pine^, (Bessette(3)), late summer and early fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette(3))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-18 x 3.5-5 um, elliptic, smooth, yellow-brown^; caulocystidia fusiform to ovate, and often pointed, (Bessette(3)), spores 14-18(20) x 3.8-5 um, cap cuticle of tubular hyphae 6-12 um wide, end cells tubular to weakly cystidioid, content of cells not forming globules in Melzer's; caulocystidia mostly ovate-pointed, (Smith), spores 14-18 x 3.8-5 um, elliptic, yellow-brown in Melzer's reagent and KOH, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 23-36 x 8-11 um, clavate, thin-walled; pleurocystidia and cheilocystidia 35-43 x 7-8.5 um, fusiform to fusoid-ventricose, colorless to dark yellow-brown in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium of erect or tangled hyphae 6-12 um wide, end cells somewhat cystidioid; caulocystidia 23-60 x 8-18 um, thin-walled, fusiform to ovate, often pointed, (Bessette(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'fibrillose'
SIMILAR  Leccinum brunneum can be confused with this species but it has a dull, dark brown cap that is not strongly fibrillose, its flesh turns fuscous with no intervening reddish phase, and it grows under aspen, (Thiers(1)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Bessette(1)*, Miller(14)*, Smith(4), Thiers(1), Marrone(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum holopus  (Rostk.) Watling^  Trans. Brit. Mycol. Soc. 43: 692. 1960
ENGLISH NAME(S)  pale rough-stem, white birch-bolete, white bog-bolete, ghost bolete
NOTES  Features include a whitish cap that is bald and may be viscid, white unchanging flesh, whitish pores that may bruise yellowish or brownish, a whitish stem with scabers that become brown, and bog habitat.^ | The description here is for var. holopus: var. americanus is nearly identical but the cap is white at first, soon becoming dull vinaceous buff, with white context that changes to reddish when cut, (Bessette). RANGE ^The distribution of var. holopus as eastern Canada south to New York, west to the northern Rocky Mountains, of var. americanus eastern Canada south to NY, west to MI, (Bessette). L. holopus has been reported from BC and NB (collections at the University of British Columbia), ID (M. Beug, pers. comm.), AK and VT (collections at University of Washington), NS (Grund), and Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle displays a pink flash lasting less than one second, then negative, with application of KOH, flesh stains slightly olive with application of FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  3-10cm, acutely convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old; "white or whitish when young, occasionally with gray, buff, tan, or pinkish tints", often darkening when old age and developing a greenish tinge; often viscid when moist or when old, bald or nearly so; margin with narrow sterile band of tissue^, (Bessette), 3-10cm, cushion-shaped to convex, becoming broadly convex or nearly flat; "dull white and remaining this color or if water-soaked olivaceous along a broad marginal area, disc often tinged buff to vinaceous-buff, rarely pea-green overall in age"; soft, subviscid, when old or wet slightly more viscid, typically bald and unpolished "but some slightly areolate at times over disc, rarely with inconspicuous streaks from appressed fibrils"; margin often exceeding tubes by 0.05cm, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, unchanging when cut or rubbed"^, (Bessette), thick, soft, white, when cut not staining or finally brownish (especially around larval tunnels); in stem not staining pink in apex when cut, but greenish to bluish in the stem base when mature, (Smith), take note of different staining reaction of var. americanus under NOTES
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "whitish to slightly grayish or pale dingy brown, unchanging or staining yellowish or brownish when cut or bruised"; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick^, (Bessette), whitish becoming brownish, "when bruised staining yellow and then brown"; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick, adnate but soon deeply depressed around stem, pallid to pale olive-buff, when old wood-brown, (Smith)
STEM  8-14cm x 1-2cm, equal or widening slightly downward, solid; whitish ground color with scabers that are whitish when young and darken to tan or darker when old, "occasionally with greenish stains on the lower portion"; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 8-14cm x 1-2cm, equal or nearly so; pallid, ornamented with squamules that become brownish (rarely blackish), top merely scurfy and remaining pallid a long time, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to scattered on ground in and around bogs, cedar swamps or wet birch woods^, (Bessette), scattered to single in cold bogs, cedar swamps, etc., (Smith), summer to fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), cinnamon-brown (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-20 x 5-6.5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 14-20 x 5-6.5 um, subfusoid, smooth, yellowish in Melzer's reagent when immature and dark reddish brown when mature, pale dingy cinnamon to ochraceous-cinnamon revived in KOH, no apical pore seen, wall slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 26-32 x 8-11 um, clavate with colorless to pale cinnamon content in KOH, yellowish in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia scattered to rare, almost embedded in hymenium, 28-36 x 9-12 um, fusoid-ventricose, content not distinctive; cap cuticle a trichodermium of interwoven subgelatinous hyphae 4-7 um wide, "end-cells tubular to somewhat cystidioid with obtuse apex"; caulocystidia "mostly the fusoid-ventricose type with elongate somewhat flexuous necks and subacute apex", clavate cells also present; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "with perfect stalk", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Leccinum rotundifoliae has a white to tan or grayish tan cap that becomes pinkish buff to cinnamon-buff or darker (as opposed to L. holopus which has a cap that is whitish to grayish when young, darkening when old and often developing a greenish or olive tinge), (Bessette).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (var. americanum), Courtecuisse(1)*, Breitenbach(3)*, Grund(11), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum idahoense  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Lloydia 31: 256-257. 1968
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark brown viscid granular cracked cap, 2) whitish flesh that becomes vinaceous gray in 15 minutes, 3) small whitish to olive-buff pores that bruise brown, 4) dingy cinnamon-buff scabers that become blackish in the lower half of the stem, and 5) relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit.^ RANGE ^Leccinum idahoense is found in ID, but "distribution limits remain to be established", (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh slightly greenish with FeSO4, (Smith(37))
CAP  8-15cm, convex, margin incurved at first, narrow band of marginal tissue breaks into segments when old; uniform dark brown; "viscid, granulose-areolate"^, (Bessette), 8-15cm, broadly convex; dark liver-brown; granulose-areolate (like 'Boletus' mirabilis), very viscid even in dry weather; "sterile margin breaking into small segments but definitely forming a sterile band at first", (Smith(37)), 8-13(20)cm, dark liver brown; viscid, granulose-roughened, (Smith(4))
FLESH  "whitish, very slowly staining vinaceous gray (taking up to 15 minutes) when exposed"^, (Bessette), pallid, when cut unchanging or in top of stem slowly slightly vinaceous gray in 15 minutes, with a watery line above tubes when mature, (Smith(37)), pallid, when cut turning faintly vinaceous-gray in about 15 minutes, (Smith(4))
PORES  2-3 per mm, round to angular, whitish becoming pale olive-buff when mature, bruising brown; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), up to 3 per mm; pale olive-buff when mature, where bruised "severely changing to vinaceous brown or finally to wood-brown, where bruised slightly staining olive"; tube layer about 2cm thick, depressed, pallid when young, slowly changing to vinaceous gray on cutting, (Smith(37)), "small and staining olive when lightly bruised, pale olive buff when young", (Smith(4))
STEM  7-12cm x 2-3.5cm, widening downward or nearly equal, with constricted base, stem solid; "whitish, with dingy cinnamon-buff scabers that become blackish, especially near the base, in age"; dry; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette), 8-12cm x 2.5-3.5cm in upper part, solid, equal, flared at top in some, more or less clavate [club-shaped] when young, appearing pinched off at base; pallid, with dingy cinnamon-buff ornamentation in upper part, finally nearly blackish in lower part, "exterior at base stained greenish", (Smith(37)), 8-12cm x 2.5-3.5cm, pinched off at base, scabers "cinnamon buff but finally blackish over lower half", (Smith(4))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Smith(37))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Smith(37))
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  single or scattered on ground under conifers^, (Bessette), under western cedar, hemlock, and western white pine mixed, (Smith(4))
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^, (Bessette), dingy cinnamon when air-dried (Smith(4)), "Sayal-brown" (Ridgway color) when moist, pale cinnamon after loss of moisture as in most species of Suillus, (Smith(37))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-17 x 4-5 um, subfusoid (somewhat spindle-shaped), smooth, dull ocher-yellow^, (Bessette), spores 13-16(17) x 3.8-5.2 um, subfusoid, smooth, nearly colorless in Melzer's reagent "but many becoming bright cinnamon overall or only in the distal portion", dull ocher-yellow in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 22-30 x 10-15 um, clavate, colorless revived in KOH and with large oil droplet; pleurocystidia scattered, 38-52 x 9-16 um, "ventricose with slightly enlarged apex above a narrowly tapered neck, or obtusely fusoid-ventricose, with smoky brown content when revived in KOH"; cap cuticle a tangled turf of hyphae 4-7(10) um wide, "with cells often short but not enlarged, in KOH or Melzer's filled with orange-brown droplets of pigment, end-cells tubular with obtuse apex to somewhat cystidioid but not conspicuously inflated, many hyphae with disarticulating cells"; caulocystidia 24-42 x 8-15 um, "fusoid-ventricose, thin-walled, the content smoky brown in KOH", caulocystidioles 20-25 x 8-12 um, "not greatly enlarged, with smoky brown content and thin smooth walls"; clamp connections not seen, (Smith(37)), 13-16(17) x 3.8-5.2 um; hyphae of cap cuticle 4-7(10) um wide, filled with orange-brown globules in Melzer's, (Smith(4))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'from Idaho'
SIMILAR  Leccinum aurantiacum differs from L. idahoense in the latter's dark liver-brown granulose-areolate cap, its very slow change to gray when the flesh is cut, and its relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit, (Smith(37)).
SOURCES  Smith(37), Bessette(3), Smith(4) (as L. idahoensis)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum insigne  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 160-161. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  aspen scaber-stalk, aspen scaberstalk, aspen rough-stem, aspen bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a reddish brown, dry cap that is minutely granular to fibrillose-scaly, 2) whitish flesh that with exposure turns purplish gray then blackish without an intervening reddish phase, 3) white pores that darken but do not become blue, 4) a stem with pallid scabers (fine scales) that become dark brown to black, the stem base sometimes turning blue, 5) growth under aspen or birch, and 6) brown spore deposit.^ |In addition to the type variety from CA, Thiers describes var. brunneum from CA with a young cap that is dark brown to reddish brown and more or less bald (as opposed to brick red to ferruginous and tomentose to fibrillose for the type variety when young), (Thiers(13)). |"The dark brown pileus when young with a tendency to fade somewhat with age and the dull, dark brown color when dried readily distinguish this variety from others that have been described." (Thiers(13)). |Smith(35) describes from MI var. luteopallidum with tubes that become yellowish before the darkening of maturity as well as several invalidly published forms. |Leccinum insigne is common in suitable habitat. RANGE ^Distribution includes CA (type variety and var. brunneum) and MI (var. luteopallidum). L. insigne is reported from WA and ID, (A. Parker, pers. comm.), BC (M. Beug pers. comm.), and MT (L. Evans, pers. comm.). The range includes eastern Canada south to NJ, west to the northern Rocky Mountains, south to the Sierra Nevada of CA and the Southwest (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains bluish with application of FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  4-15cm, convex becoming broadly convex; rusty orange to rusty cinnamon or rusty brown; dry, minutely granular to fibrillose-scaly; margin with band of sterile tissue when young^, (Bessette), 6-17cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex to highly irregular with broad shallow depressions or pits; color variable but typically some shade of reddish brown, and somewhat variegated when old; dry to moist, occasionally subviscid when wet, bald to finely velvety to subtomentose when young, not changing or sometimes becoming obscurely appressed-fibrillose or appressed-scaly when old, often finely areolate to cracked-areolate when old; margin with sterile flaps of tissue, (Thiers(1)), color variable: bright orange to rusty orange, reddish orange, orange-brown, reddish brown, or cinnamon, often paler or duller (tan, brown, or dull orange-brown) when old, (Arora)
FLESH  white, when exposed turning purplish gray then blackish without an intervening reddish phase^, (Bessette), 1.5-3cm thick, white, with exposure changing to fuscous with no intermediate reddish stage, color changes sometimes slow in developing; blue discoloration sometimes developing in stem base, (Thiers(1)), turning bluish gray to purplish gray or fuscous when bruised or cut, but sometimes very slowly or erratically, (Arora)
PORES  1-3 per mm, round, "whitish, slowly becoming dingy yellow" and finally yellow-brown to olive-brown when old, not turning blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick^, (Bessette), up to 1mm, angular; white when young, olive buff to yellowish when mature; tube layer 1-2cm thick, narrowly depressed at first, becoming broadly and deeply depressed when old (somewhat ventricose), colored as pores, (Thiers(1)), not turning blue when bruised but may stain yellowish to brown, lavender, or vinaceous, (Arora)
STEM  7-12cm x 1-2cm, widening downward, often ventricose at base, solid; whitish, with brownish scabers that darken to blackish when mature; dry; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 8-13cm x 1-2cm at top, equal to club-shaped, solid; dry, ground color whitish, scabers close and pallid when young, typically becoming dark brown to black and often more widely spaced when mature, "often very fine, and sometimes more or less collapsing" when old, (Thiers(1)), rather tough and fibrous, scabers initially pallid but become reddish orange to brown and finally blackish by maturity, stem base often staining or discoloring blue, (Arora)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  scattered or in groups on ground under aspen or birch^ (Bessette), associated primarily if not exclusively with aspen (Arora), gregarious to scattered in soil under aspens, (Thiers(13)), spring, summer, and fall, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark yellow-brown to olive-brown^ (Bessette), brown (Thiers(1))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-16 x 4-5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, yellowish^; caulocystidia clavate to ventricose-mucronate, (Bessette), spores (11)13-16(18) x 4-6 um, subfusoid to subcylindric, smooth, ochraceous in Melzer's reagent, pale ochraceous in KOH, walls moderately thickened; basidia 4-spored, 29-34 x 8-12 um, clavate to pear-shaped, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, 27-43 x 7-13 um, clavate to clavate with elongated tapering apices, colorless to dark brown in KOH; cap cuticle a trichodermium of tangled hyphal tips, hyphae 4-7 um wide, walls sometimes roughened, pigment globules not developing in Melzer's reagent, "terminal cells with obtuse apices, or with tapered apices"; caulocystidia "29-39 x 10-14 um, clavate to fusoid, dark brown in KOH, thin-walled"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers(1))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "remarkable", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Leccinum aurantiacum has cap flesh that stains in a progression from reddish or pinkish to fuscous or blackish (Bessette). In comparison to L. aurantiacum, L. insigne is distinguished by "the characteristic color changes occurring in the flesh when exposed, the rusty red color of the pileus and the absence of pigment globules in the cuticular hyphae when mounted in Melzer's reagent", (Thiers(13)). See also SIMILAR section of Leccinum discolor.
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Thiers(13), Arora(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Sept(1)*, AroraPocket(1)*, Marrone(1)*, McBride(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum manzanitae  Thiers^  Mycologia 63: 266-267. 1971
ENGLISH NAME(S)  madrone bolete, manzanita bolete, manzanita mushroom
NOTES  Features include 1) a reddish to brown viscid fibrillose cap, 2) a hard, tough stem with dark scabers, and 3) growth with manzanita and madrone.^ | This is one of the species that was formerly known under the name Leccinum aurantiacum. Note that Both(1) describes the staining of the flesh as changing to fuscous with red tones but neither Thiers nor Bessette nor Arora describe red tones. RANGE ^In CA it is "by far the most common Leccinum in the coastal forests of California where it occurs under manzanita and madrone" (Thiers(13) with Leccinum in italics). It also occurs in OR, (Bessette), and is reported from WA (M. Seidl, pers. comm.) and BC (O. Ceska, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains pale gray with FeSO4, tubes pale red with KOH then blackening, (Thiers)
CAP  (5)7-20(30)cm, rounded becoming convex to flat or somewhat irregular, margin with flaps of tissue when young; "dark red to reddish brown or at times rusty-brown, rusty-orange, or brown"; viscid when moist, smooth but usually with flattened fibrils, sometimes pitted when old^, (Arora), 6-20cm, cushion-shaped to convex, margin incurved at first, with flaps of sterile tissue; dark red to reddish brown, becoming darker reddish brown when old; "typically viscid, often somewhat pitted and uneven, appressed-fibrillose", (Bessette), often shallowly to deeply pitted or reticulate, viscid to subviscid, especially when old, strongly appressed-fibrillose during all stages or occasionally tomentose, never bald, fibrils often more conspicuous toward margin, (Thiers(13))
FLESH  thick, firm becoming soft; "white, usually bruising smoky-gray to purple-gray or bluish-gray when cut, but often very slowly or only in certain areas"^, (Arora), white, when exposed slowly turning fuscous without reddish phase, (Bessette), changing to fuscous with red tones when cut (Both), " 2-4cm thick, white when first exposed, slowly and erratically changing to fuscous with no reddish intermediate stage", in stem slowly staining fuscous in apical portion, sometimes turning blue in base, (Thiers(13))
PORES  "whitish to pale olive, olive-gray or grayish, usually deep dingy olive-buff in age"; not turning blue when bruised but often discoloring brown^, (Arora) 1-3 per mm, angular, pale olive-buff becoming darker olive-buff, bruising dark brown; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick, depressed near stem when old, (Bessette), up to 1mm in diameter, angular, colored as tubes, tubes layer 1-2.5cm thick, "adnate to shallowly or deeply depressed", pale olive when young, darkening to olive drab when mature, staining dark brown when bruised, (Thiers(13))
STEM  8-20cm x 1.5-4cm, equal or widening downwards, tough, fibrous, solid; whitish with pallid scabers that become deep brown to black by maturity, lower part "often bruising (or already stained) bright blue to greenish-blue"^, (Arora), 9-15cm x 1.6-4cm, widening downwards, sometimes with ventricose base; solid; whitish, with dense whitish scabers that darken to nearly fuscous when old; dry; partial veil and ring absent, (Bessette), 10-16cm x 1.5-3.5cm wide at top, "clavate to ventricose, solid"; ground color whitish, "scales typically pallid when young" darkening to near fuscous when old; "surface dry, conspicuously fibrillose-scaly", (Thiers(13))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Thiers(13))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Thiers(13))
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  singly to scattered or gregarious, under manzanita and madrone^, (Arora), single, scattered, or in groups under madrone or manzanita (Bessette), fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Arora, Bessette), cinnamon-brown (Thiers(13))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-19 x 3-5 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 13-17 x 4-6 um, fusoid to subcylindric [almost cylindric], smooth, ochraceous, (Bessette), spores 13-17 x 4-5.5 um, (var. angustisporum distinguished only by spores that reach only 3-4 um wide and often average 1-2 um longer), fusoid to subelliptic to subcylindric, inequilateral, smooth, moderately thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 27-32 x 6-9 um, clavate to pyriform; hymenial cystidia often obscure, scattered, 23-32 x 4-6 um, fusoid to clavate with narrow elongated apices, colorless in KOH; cap cuticle a trichodermium of free tangled hyphal tips, hyphae 8-12 um wide, some disarticulation of cells, walls smooth to sometimes obscurely rough, pigment globules forming in cap cuticle hyphae when mounted in Melzer's, terminal cells elongated and often noticeably tapered; clamp connections absent, (Thiers(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means "with manzanita"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Arora and Bessette mention several other "manzanita boletes" that occur in California. Leccinum ponderosum is similar but associated with conifers: it typically has unchanging or only slightly changing flesh, and the cap is bald and not as deeply colored. A similar but (in 1971) undescribed species occurs above timberline with Arctostaphylos uva-ursi in Alaska, but L. manzanitae is "much darker colored, consistently larger in size, viscid, has different color changes in the exposed flesh and slightly smaller spores", (Thiers(13)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Thiers(1), Thiers(13), Both(1), AroraPocket(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum ponderosum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 144-145. 1966
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) large size, 2) an orange-red cap that is bald or nearly so, 3) white unchanging cap flesh, 4) whitish to pale olive brown pores that bruise yellow-brown, 5) a stem with brown to blackish scabers that stains blue especially toward base, 6) growth under conifers, and 7) microscopic characters including orange-brown pigment globules in the cap cuticle hyphae in Melzer's reagent.^ RANGE ^Leccinum ponderosum is found in WA, OR, and ID, (Bessette). It appears on foray lists from BC.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  9-30cm, convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old; orange-red to dull brick-red; dry, bald or nearly so; margin "with thin flaps of sterile tissue at least when young"^, (Bessette), 10-30cm, ferruginous-red; soon viscid, (Smith(4)), 10-30cm, obtuse to convex when young, with appendiculate margin, expanding to broadly convex to nearly flat; ferruginous red ("Kaiser-brown" to "vinaceous-rufous" or "rufous") and when dried dull brick red; subviscid when young, more viscid when old, bald to appressed-fibrillose, (Smith(38))
FLESH  "white, not staining when exposed or bruised"^, (Bessette), white, when cut not changing appreciably, (Smith(4)), "white when cut and not changing appreciably, but as dried mostly merely buff-color throughout pileus and stipe but with a few gray streaks", (Smith(38))
PORES  2-3 per mm, round to angular; whitish at first, becoming dingy whitish to pale olive brown when old, "slowly staining yellowish to orange-brown when bruised"; tube layer 0.8-2cm thick, typically depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), "young pores pallid to avellaneous", staining ochraceous where lightly bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, pallid, (Smith(4)), minute; "pallid to avellaneous when young, staining ochraceous where lightly bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick (in young caps), "adnate-subdecurrent, dingy clay color as dried", (Smith(38))
STEM  7-15cm x 2-3cm, widening downward, solid; "whitish, with brown to fuscous scabers, staining blue when bruised, especially toward the base"; dry; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 8-16cm x 2-3cm at top, enlarging to 5-9cm at base, solid, hard; apex pale buff, when fresh often flushed reddish like the cap, stem "where handled staining blue (entire base finally) and in drying becoming yellow to olive-yellow"; "with avellaneous to fuscous points and squamules arising from a pallid cottony rather well-developed under-layer", 'the ornamentation drying near "snuff brown" and appearing as irregular patches of appressed material', (Smith(38))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on or near decaying conifers, especially pines^, (Bessette), scattered under Ponderosa or Lodgepole Pine, (Smith(4)), type under Pinus ponderosa and P. lambertiana (Smith(38)), fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-17 x 4.5-6 um, fusoid [spindle-shaped], smooth, ochraceous^, (Bessette), spores 14-17 x 4.5-6 um, hyphae of cap cuticle 4-12 um wide, in Melzer's reagent "the content rounding into distinct globules in some"; caulocystidia mostly clavate, (Smith(4)), spores 14-17 x 4.5-5.5(6) um, fusoid [spindle-shaped], smooth, tan in Melzer's reagent, ochraceous in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 18-24 x 9-12 um, often with a large colorless globule; pleurocystidia "36-45 x 8-13 um, fusoid-ventricose with neck often irregular, apices acute to subacute", content colorless or brownish, cheilocystidia 20-32 x 7-12 um, clavate to clavate-mucronate, ochraceous in KOH; caulocystidia (30)40-70 x (8)10-18 um, mostly clavate, content smoky ochraceous in KOH; cap cutis with hyphae of epicutis 4-12 um wide, "the end-cells not distinctively differentiated (at most merely somewhat cystidioid), walls smooth or very rarely minutely asperulate, cells readily disarticulating, cells mostly more than 5 times longer than wide, content brownish in KOH and pigment separating in rather indistinct globules, in Melzer's with large medium and small pigment globules red to orange-red in color"; hyphae of context near subcutis with yellow to orange content when revived in Melzer's reagent; clamp connections none, (Smith(38))
NAME ORIGIN  means "heavy, weighty"^, (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum manzanitae has flesh that is deeper in color and slowly and erratically changes to fuscous when exposed, and the cap is strongly appressed-fibrillose during all stages, (Thiers).

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum rotundifoliae  (Singer) A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 6: 128-129. 1967
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a whitish to tan cap that darkens when old, 2) white flesh which when cut is unchanging or turns slightly pinkish in cap, turns brownish in top of stem, and turns greenish blue in base, 3) whitish to dingy pale brown unchanging pores, 4) a whitish to pale tan stem with scabers whitish to brownish or grayish brown, 5) growth under birch, and 6) a blue reaction of cap flesh to FeSO4.^ | The online Species Fungorum, accessed February 12, 2018, considered this taxon a synonym of Leccinum scabrum (Bull.) Gray, but MycoBank, accessed the same day, maintained them separately. RANGE ^L. rotundifoliae is found in eastern Canada south to NY, west to ID and AK, (Bessette), and including MI (Smith).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh instantly stains dark blue with the application of FeSO4, (Bessette), cap flesh blue with FeSO4, (Smith)
CAP  2-8cm, obtuse to convex, becoming broadly convex; "whitish to tan or grayish tan", darkening when old; "dry to slightly viscid, typically wrinkled and irregularly depressed"^, (Bessette), 2-6cm, obtuse to convex becoming broadly convex; "white to dull white overall at first, slowly becoming pale buff to pinkish buff then cinnamon-buff to dingy clay color"; "dry at first and unpolished, becoming somewhat viscid by maturity", rimulose [finely cracked] when old at times or on drying; margin not appendiculate, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, unchanging or slightly pinkish when exposed"; in stem white, soon greenish blue in base and brownish near the top^, (Bessette), white, showing no appreciable color change when cut; in stem white when cut but soon greenish blue in base and brownish in apex [top], (Smith)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; whitish when young, becoming dingy pale brown when old, unchanging when bruised; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm, whitish becoming avellaneous; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, white to olive-buff, slowly becoming pale wood-brown by maturity, unchanging when cut or bruised, (Smith)
STEM  4-10cm x 0.5-1.2cm, solid; "whitish to pale tan beneath a layer of scabers that are whitish at first" and brownish to grayish brown when old; dry; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 4-10cm x 0.5-1.2cm at top, equal or nearly so, solid; white with scabers that are white and slowly become brown like cap but not blackish, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  scattered or in small groups on the ground under birch^, (Bessette), scattered under dwarf birch in and along the edges of cold bogs, (Smith)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 16-23 x 5.5-8 um, narrowly fusoid [spindle-shaped] to elongate-subovate, smooth, ochraceous^; caulocystidia lemon yellow in KOH, (Bessette), spores 16-20(25) x 5.5-7.5(8) um, narrowly subfusoid to elongate-subovate, many soon becoming dextrinoid when revived in Melzer's, pale ochraceous to brownish ochraceous in KOH, wall thickened slightly, no distinct germ pore; basidia 4-spored, 23-28 x 9-13 um, colorless to yellow in KOH; pleurocystidia 28-36 x 8-14 um, more or less fusoid-ventricose, colorless or nearly so, cheilocystidia 32-60 x 8-12 um, with various shapes but mostly fusoid-ventricose, "the neck tapered and flexuous or irregular in outline, ending in an acute to subacute apex, with lemon yellow content in KOH"; cap cuticle a trichodermium "of tangled mostly thin-walled flexuous narrow hyphae with hyphal ends 3-5 um wide, subgelatinous in KOH, with yellow-brown content, often flexuous, the tips of the cells obtuse to subcapitate and merely dull yellowish to orange-buff in Melzer's"; caulocystidia lemon-yellow in KOH; clamp connections absent, (Smith) 
NAME ORIGIN  means "occurring with Betula rotundifolia (round-leaved birch)"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum holopus var. holopus has a cap that is whitish to grayish when young, darkening when old and often developing a greenish or olive tinge, and cap flesh that stains slightly olive with FeSO4, as opposed to L. rotundifoliae which has white to tan or grayish tan cap that becomes pinkish buff to cinnamon-buff or darker, and cap flesh that stains instantly dark blue with FeSO4, (Bessette). Leccinum holopus var. americanum is of uncertain distribution in the Pacific Northwest and unfortunately the reaction of cap flesh to FeSO4 is not recorded; however, the caulocystidia have content that is dark yellow brown in KOH, (Smith). Leccinum scabrum is less pale with scabers on stem that are less pale, (Smith).
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Both(1)
