FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum scabrum  (Bull.) Gray^  Nat. Arr. Brit. Pl. I: 647. 1821
ENGLISH NAME(S)  common scaber-stalk, common scaberstalk, birch bolete, birch scaber-stalk, birch scaberstalk, brown birch-bolete 
NOTES  Features include 1) dingy brownish bald cap, 2) white flesh that may turn brownish, or in lower stem blue, 3) whitish pores that darken but do not turn blue, 4) whitish stem with dark brown to black scabers, and 5) growth under birch.^ | Leccinum scabrum was probably introduced with planted birches but does occur with natural birches, for example in northern ID and WA, (Trudell). |Another dingy brown birch associate is Leccinum schistophyllum Bon, described from France in 1981. We have not been able to access the original description. An internet photo by Andy Overall shows some blue staining halfway up the inside of the cut stem. According to D. Miller, pers. comm. May 10, 2021, Leccinum schistophyllum was found under Vancouver British Columbia urban birch and sequenced. RANGE ^L. scabrum has been found in eastern Canada south to GA, west to AK and CA, (Bessette), BC (Bandoni), WA and OR, (numerous observers), ID (A. Parker, pers. comm.), MI (Smith), NS (Grund), and also Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle stains reddish brown with application of KOH, bluish slate with FeSO4; flesh reddish-brown with application of KOH, bluish slate with FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  4-10cm, obtuse to broadly convex; "grayish brown to yellow-brown when young", often developing olive tones when old; moist or dry, bald^, (Bessette), 4-10cm, cushion-shaped to broadly convex; "grayish brown to dull yellow-brown" and often flushed olivaceous when old; "moist to dry or when wet fairly viscid", "glabrous and often with depressions in age"; margin scarcely projecting beyond tubes, (Smith), "dull brown to grayish brown to dingy yellow-brown or tan" (but often developing olive tints when old), lacking marginal flaps of tissue, (Arora), dry to viscid, may be somewhat tomentose, may crack when old, (Trudell)
FLESH  "white, not changing or sometimes slowly brownish when exposed", in stem white, unchanging or slowly staining pinkish near stem surface or turning blue near base^, (Bessette), white, 'when cut not staining or slowly becoming slightly brownish (near "pinkish buff")', in stem white, slowly staining pinkish buff in cortex when cut, "also developing both blue and red stains in restricted areas lower down if wet", (Smith: it is not totally clear from the description whether the blue and red stains are in the stem flesh or on the surface), dull whitish or pallid becoming dingy brownish when old, not turning blue when bruised but sometimes staining slightly yellowish or ocher, (Arora)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round to irregular; whitish at first, becoming grayish to brownish when old, "not staining when bruised or slowly staining yellowish"; tube layer 0.7-1.6cm thick^, (Bessette), small; pallid then colored as tubes and either not staining when bruised or staining yellow; tubes 0.8-1.5cm thick, deeply depressed around stem, "pallid, slowly becoming wood-brown as spores mature", (Smith), "cream to pale tan or olive-buff" (Trudell)
STEM  7-14cm x 0.7-1.6cm, widening downward, solid; whitish, with dark brown to blackish scabers, sometimes with bluish green stains near base; dry^, (Bessette), 7-12(15)cm x 0.7-1.2(1.6)cm, evenly widening downward, solid; pallid ground color with scabers dark brown to blackish ("dark brown to blackish punctate to nearly reticulate or squamulose decorations"), (Smith), club-shaped, often long in relation to the diameter of the cap, (Trudell)
ODOR  mild^ (Bessette, Smith)
TASTE  mild^ (Bessette, Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on ground under hardwoods, especially birch^, (Bessette), under birch (Smith), summer and fall (Miller), common in urban and suburban settings and less so in natural habitats, (Trudell)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), olive-buff (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 15-19 x 5-7 um, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale tawny^; cap cuticle end cells "typically uniform and parallel, not inflated and clavate to subglobose"; caulocystidia "mostly clavate with dull ochraceous content in KOH", (Bessette), spores 15-19 x 5-7 um, subfusiform, smooth, pale tawny in Melzer's reagent, when revived in KOH pale tawny singly and darker in groups, wall thickened to about 0.5 um revived in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 11-13 um wide, colorless in KOH and yellowish in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia none; cap cuticle "a tangled layer of floccose hyphae 6-12(15) um wide, above a subgelatinous subcutis, their content smoky ochraceous in KOH and with a grayer shadow in Melzer's but content remaining granular to homogeneous", wall at first enveloped in outer gelatinous matrix that dissolves slowly in KOH, at times a few short slightly inflated cells seen; caulocystidia mostly clavate, 32-46 x 9-17 um, with smoky ochraceous content in KOH, some clavate-mucronate, and a few varying to fusoid-ventricose; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'rough'
SIMILAR  Leccinum brunneum of California and probably Idaho grows with aspen, and has a dark brown cap that fades to paler brown when old and does not stain blue, whitish stem with whitish scabers that darken when old, and white flesh that stains fuscous without an intervening reddish stage, (Bessette). Leccinum alaskanum in Alaska grows with birch but has a dark brown mottled cap (Bessette). See also SIMILAR section of Leccinum rotundifoliae.
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Ammirati(1)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Bandoni(1), Breitenbach(3)*, Grund(11), AroraPocket(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Marrone(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Leccinum versipelle  (Fr.) Snell^  Lloydia 7(1): 58. 1944;  Leccinum testaceoscabrum  (Secr.) Singer
ENGLISH NAME(S)  orange birch bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a bright orange cap that fades to dull orange then pinkish tan, 2) flesh that stains reddish then purple-gray to blackish and with FeSO4 turns bright green, 3) a stem with dense black scabers when young and when old, and 4) growth under hardwoods.^ | Bessette(3) uses the name L. testaceoscabrum but notes that according to Breitenbach(3) the correct name is Leccinum versipelle (Fr.) Snell. The online Species Fungorum, accessed January 9, 2014, also uses the name Leccinum versipelle. RANGE ^The distribution is northeastern N. America west to the Pacific Northwest and to AK where it is abundant, (Bessette). There is a collection from BC determined by W. Snell at Pacific Forestry Centre (as Boletus versipellis).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh bright green with application of FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  4-10cm, convex, margin with tissue, at least when young; bright reddish orange then dull orange with rose tinge, fading to pinkish tan when old; dry, nearly bald^, (Bessette), 4-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex; dull orange with a tint of rose and when dried a dull "orange-cinnamon" (Ridgway color); dry and obscurely fibrillose when young, practically bald as well as subviscid when mature; margin with hanging remnants at first, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, staining reddish, then purple-gray to blackish when exposed", especially where stem meets cap^, (Bessette), firm when young; white, "when cut staining reddish and then changing to avellaneous and finally fuscous", (Smith)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "whitish to olive-buff or pale grayish when young", becoming dingy brown when old, bruising olive to olive-brown or darker; tube layer 0.8-1.6cm thick, depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), olive-pallid before dingy brown, "when lightly bruised staining olive, if severely bruised changing to fuscous"; tube layer 1.5cm thick, depressed around stem, whitish then olivaceous-pallid, (Smith)
STEM  5-12cm x 1-1.6cm, nearly equal, solid; with dense layer of black scabers when young and when mature, on a whitish ground, sometimes with bluish stains near base; dry; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette), 5-10cm x 1-1.5cm, equal or nearly so; pallid to white ground color, scabers typically blackish in buttons, with slight flush of reddish around the dark squamules, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Smith)
EDIBILITY  suspected to cause gastric distress in some people, (Bessette)
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under beech, oak, or birch, July to September^, (Bessette), summer, fall, (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-18 x 3.5-5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped] to elongate subelliptic, smooth, ochraceous^; caulocystidia "clavate, clavate-mucronate or sometimes fusoid-ventricose with pale fuscous content in KOH", (Bessette), spores (12)13-16(18) x 3.5-4.5 um, subfusoid to elongate-subelliptic, smooth, ochraceous in KOH or Melzer's, wall -0.5 um thick; basidia 4-spored 22-30 x 9-11 um, clavate, colorless; pleurocystidia 33-45 x 9-14 um, fusoid-ventricose with subacute apex, walls thin and colorless, content in KOH colorless to brownish, in Melzer's dark brown, cheilocystidia similar but smaller and many basidiole-like cells also present; cap cuticle of appressed hyphae 6-15(18) um wide, walls thin and often minutely roughened with colorless plates, cells readily disarticulating, mostly more than 5 times as long as broad, some short cells more or less oval in outline also present, end-cells ventricose to bullet-shaped, content of cells "slowly forming irregular pigment masses and finally globules 3-10 um in diameter in Melzer's"; caulocystidia 40-80 x10-18 um, clavate, clavate-mucronate or fusoid-ventricose, content pale fuscous in KOH or some pigment also in wall; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  "versipelle" means "with a different skin or pellicle", (Both(1)); "testaceoscabrum" means "brick-red and scabrous"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum atrostipitatum has a generally duller cap that is more scaly when old, paler pores when young, flesh that stains bluish gray with FeSO4, and different caulocystidia, (Bessette). L. atrostipitatum is more definitely associated with birch, the cap has a more patchy appearance at maturity, and "caulocystidia more frequently are fusoid-ventricose with elongated necks"; in one collection at least, the intermediate staining phase of the flesh was redder in L. versipelle, (Smith, discussing L. testaceoscabrum).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as L. testaceoscabrum), Smith(35) (as L. testaceoscabrum), Sept(1)* (as L. testaceoscabrum), Breitenbach(3)*, Both(1), Bacon(1)*, AroraPocket(1)* (as L. testaceoscabrum), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Neoboletus 'luridiformis'  (Rostk.) Gelardi, Simonini & Vizzini^  Index Fungorum 192: 1, 2014;  Boletus luridiformis  Rostk.  Consp. Fung. Esc. p.24. 1821 (misapplied name);  Sutorius luridiformis (Rostk.) G. Wu & Zhu L. Yang  in Wu, Li, Zhu, Zhao, Han, Cui, Li, Xu & Yang, Fungal Diversity 81: 145. 2016  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  slender red-pored bolete, red-stalked bolete, scarletina bolete
NOTES  {See also Red-pored Boletus Table.} This name probably represents two species in the Pacific Northwest, neither of which are described yet (N. Siegel, pers. comm.). Features include dark brown cap, bright red to orange pores, and granular rather than reticulate [netted] stem that is not bulbous.^ | The current name of the species in Europe in the Index Fungorum and MycoBank, each accessed February 9, 2019, is given as Sutorius luridiformis (Rostk.) G. Wu & Zhu L. Yang, but the need to use that genus name is disputed. RANGE ^Neoboletus 'luridiformis' is found in northeastern North America west to CA, north to AK, (Bessette), OR (vouchered at University of Washington), and WA (Buck McAdoo, pers. comm. from H. Thiers). It has been reported from BC (C. Roberts, pers. comm.), and from NS (Grund). The accompanying photographs are from the Pacific Northwest and are not the true Neoboletus luridiformis.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap surface red with KOH but quickly darkening, flesh yellow to pale orange with KOH, (Thiers), cap surface dark amber with application of KOH and dark olive-green with FeSO4, flesh slowly olivaceous with application of FeSO4, blued surface bleached yellow then white with KOH, (Bessette)
CAP  5-12cm, convex becoming broadly convex when old; "dark brown to nearly blackish brown when young, becoming reddish brown to olive-brown" when mature; dry, somewhat tomentose to nearly smooth^, (Bessette), 8-15cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat to occasionally umbonate or shallowly depressed when old, margin incurved to downcurved; brown when young, when old red on disc and reddish brown toward margin; dry, smooth to occasionally wrinkled to reticulate on disc, obscurely tomentose to velvety to occasionally appressed-fibrillose when young, frequently bald when old, "usually darkening and appearing water-soaked when bruised", (Thiers), 5-15(20)cm, may be pitted when old, (Arora)
FLESH  greenish yellow to yellow, quickly staining blue when cut, in stem yellow except reddish at base, rapidly turning blue when exposed^, (Bessette), 1-3cm thick, firm, compact; yellow, turning blue when bruised, in stem yellow except reddish to reddish vinaceous at base, turning blue when exposed, (Thiers)
PORES  1-3 per mm, round to irregular; "orange-red to dull orange, sometimes yellow when very young, staining blue when bruised"; tubes 0.8-1.4cm long^, (Bessette), 1-2 per mm, angular; typically red, sometimes near reddish orange, turning blue when bruised; tubes 1-2cm long, depressed around stem, yellow, turning blue when bruised, (Thiers), "red to brick-red to orange-red, orange, rusty-orange, or burnt sienna (but sometimes yellowish in old age), blueing quickly when bruised", (Arora)
STEM  6-10cm x 1.3-5cm, nearly equal or widening or narrowing downward, solid; "with reddish to orange-cinnamon pruina on a yellow ground color, often reddish at the base, blueing when bruised"; dry, not reticulate, lacking reddish hairs at base^, (Bessette), 8-12cm x 1.5-3.5(6)cm, club-shaped to somewhat club-shaped to occasionally equal, solid; typically yellow at top, usually with yellow background for entire length but marked with red granules, basal part often appearing reddish brown; dry, bald to more commonly punctate to obscurely velvety or fibrillose, not reticulate, white rhizomorphs attached to base, (Thiers), 4-15cm x (1.5)2-4(5)cm, often curved, (Arora)
ODOR  not distinctive (Bessette), often pungent, unpleasant, (Thiers)
TASTE  not distinctive (Bessette), often pungent, unpleasant, (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  poisonous, causing gastrointestinal distress (Bessette)^, rated edible by some, but red pores serve as a warning of poisonous species, (Thiers), poisonous to some people (Arora)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups under conifers or hardwoods^, (Bessette), single in soil in mixed forests (Thiers), late summer to fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown (Bessette), ochraceous brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-16 x 4.8-5.5 um, subcylindric to subfusoid [somewhat cylindric to somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, dark ochraceous in Melzer's, ochraceous in KOH, moderately thick-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 18-24 x 7-10 um, colorless, contents granular in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, sometimes embedded, 39-45 x 9-11 um, "subclavate with tapering apices to basidioid, often heavily incrusted and staining bright ochraceous in KOH", occasionally colorless and apparently not incrusted; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 12-16 x 4.5-6 um, subfusoid, smooth, ochraceous, (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  probably 'in the form of [Boletus] luridus'
SIMILAR  Rubroboletus pulcherrimus and Rubroboletus eastwoodiae have a reticulate [netted] stem, (Thiers). Suillellus amygdalinus is much paler, with a more pallid stem and broader spores, (Thiers). S. amygdalinus has a dark rose-red to ocher-red cap that becomes brownish red to pale brown with reddish tints when old, a yellow stem with cinnamon-red to brick-red granules, and wider spores with thick walls, (Bessette). S. amygdalinus has oranger pores and grows under hardwoods, (Arora).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus luridiformis), Thiers(1) (as B. erythropus), Arora(1)* (as B. erythropus), Grund(11) (as B. erythropus), Bacon(1)* (as Boletus luridiformis), AroraPocket(1)* (as B. erythropus), Buczacki(1)* (as Boletus luridiformis), Marrone(1)* (as Boletus 'luridiformis')

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Phylloporus arenicola  A.H. Sm. and Trappe^  Mycologia 64: 1150-1151. 1972
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Also listed in Gilled category. Features include olive to olive-fuscous cap, pallid to yellowish flesh without blue-staining, adnexed-seceding bright yellow gills, stem with dull yellow ground color (bright yellow near base), overlaid with brown to reddish pruina, growth on sand dunes, and elongated spores.^ | A.H. Smith and J.M. Trappe lists the outstanding features in the original description as "the immediate NH4OH reaction to violet-fuscous of the cuticle of the pileus when fresh, the olive to olive-fuscous color of the pileus, the lack of any blue stain when the hymenophore or context are injured, the adnexed to emarginate-seceding lamellae, and the bright yellow base of the stipe". They go on to say that the common Phylloporus rhodoxanthus "is readily distinguished by a number of characters such as the gills being decurrent, by the ammonia reaction, and the dull red to bay colored pileus". (Smith(10)). Nevertheless, there are observers who say that many collections otherwise fitting Phylloporus arenicola do stain blue, and DNA sequences thus far in California and the Pacific Northwest match Phylloporus arenicola. RANGE ^Phylloporus arenicola is found at least in OR, (Smith), also CA (Bessette), and WA (vouchered at University of Washington).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^NH4OH applied to cuticle immediately produces a violaceous-fuscous stain that soon becomes mahogany red; FeSO4 slowly weakly olive (Smith)
CAP  1.5-4.5cm across, flat becoming convex-depressed; moderate olive on the margin to olive-fuscous on the disc, when old more olive-brown, in fading becoming olive-yellow and possessing a sheen; unpolished to velvety^, (Smith), 1.5-4.5cm across, convex becoming nearly flat to somewhat depressed, "margin even incurved at first"; "dull olive with a darker olive-fuscous disc when young, becoming olive-brown" then fading to olive-ocher or dull pale brown when old; dry, velvety subtomentose, sometimes with a sheen, (Bessette)
FLESH  pallid to yellowish, not staining blue when cut^, (Smith), whitish to yellowish, unchanging when exposed; in stem bright yellow to cinnabar toward base and pale yellow in upper part, (Bessette)
PORES  adnexed-seceding, subdistant, broad; bright yellow ("mustard yellow"), not staining, edges colored as faces; edges even^, (Smith), adnexed-seceding to subdecurrent, strongly lamellate [in the form of gills], 0.3-0.7cm broad; bright yellow when young, becoming somewhat duller yellow when old, not staining when bruised, (Bessette)
STEM  4-6cm x 0.6-0.8cm, narrowing downward; dull yellow ground color overlaid with brown to reddish pruina, bright yellow in and around the base or in some becoming cinnabar toned^, (Smith), 4-6cm x 0.5-0.8cm, narrowing downward, solid; "with a dull yellow ground color that is partially covered with brown to reddish pruina and fibrils"; dry, (Bessette)
ODOR  none^ (Smith)
TASTE  mild^ (Smith)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  type single under Pinus contorta on sand dunes^, (Smith), single, scattered, or in groups on sand dunes under pine, September to November, (Bessette), fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-12 x 4-5 um, subelliptic to subfusoid, smooth, weakly dextrinoid, apex lacking a distinct germ pore, olive-hyaline in KOH^; basidia 4-spored, 38-60 x 9-13 um, clavate, with oil droplets, pale yellow in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia subfusoid to fusoid-ventricose, 52-86 x 9-16 um, thin-walled, smooth, colorless, not distinctive, cheilocystidia similar to pleurocystidia but shorter; no clamp connections, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'living in sand'
SIMILAR  Phylloporus rhodoxanthus has a dull red to bay colored cap, decurrent gills, and different ammonia reaction, (Smith(10)). Phylloporus boletinoides A.H. Sm. & Thiers, found in eastern North America, "has a strongly decurrent boletinoid to sublamellate pore surface that sometimes stains dark blue to bluish green when bruised", (Bessette(3)). Xerocomus subtomentosus group has somewhat similar cap but has pores not gills.
SOURCES  Smith(10), Bessette(3)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Phylloporus rhodoxanthus  (Schwein.) Bres.^  Fung. trident. 2(14): 95. 1900
ENGLISH NAME(S)  gilled bolete
NOTES  Also listed in Gilled category. Most or all of the observations of this species on the west coast of North America are Phylloporus arenicola. Features are dry minutely velvety brown to reddish cap that looks like a Boletus, and thick yellow gills.^ | The subspecies is not specified for the Arora description below (note that he says the gills typically bruise green or blue but sometimes not at all), but Bessette's description is for ssp. americanus Singer (without that color change). Ssp. foliiporus (Murrill) Singer has a bright yellow gill-like surface that stains bluish to greenish when bruised and a cinnamon to dark pinkish brown cap and according to Both(1) is recorded from Florida north to New Jersey. (Incidentally Both(1) says that the photo in Phillips(1) p. 127 of P. foliiporus is P. boletinoides). P. rhodoxanthus ssp. albomycelinus Snell and Dick from eastern North America (with white basal mycelium) has been renamed P. leucomycelinus Singer. RANGE ^P. rhodoxanthus is reported from WA by O'Dell, and is not uncommon on foray lists in BC (often blue-staining), with collections at the Pacific Forestry Centre and the University of British Columbia, and there are collections from WA at the University of Washington. Bessette gives RANGE of ssp. americanus as eastern Canada south to FL, west to CA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle stains blue with NH4OH (Bessette)
CAP  2-5(12)cm, broadly convex to flat or with uplifted margin when old; "dull brown to olive-brown or yellow-brown in one form, red to reddish-brown in another", "sometimes developing pallid to yellowish cracks or fissures"; surface "dry, minutely velvety to nearly smooth"^, (Arora), 2.5-10cm, obtuse to convex at first, becoming nearly flat and sometimes shallowly depressed; "color variable, dark red, dull red, reddish yellow to reddish brown or olive brown"; dry, somewhat velvety, often cracked to cracked-areolate [cracked like dried mud] when old; margin typically with narrow band of sterile tissue, (Bessette)
FLESH  thick; pallid to yellowish^, (Arora), whitish to pale yellow, tinged reddish under cap surface, (Bessette)
PORES  gill-like structures adnate to decurrent but sometimes seceding, widely spaced, broad, fairly thick, sometimes forked or with cross veins; "bright yellow to ocher, typically bruising green or blue (but often slowly, sometimes not at all), sometimes also staining brownish"^, (Arora), with strong gill-like structures, sometimes pore-like near stem, decurrent, subdistant to distant, sometimes forked, strongly interveined, separating cleanly from cap; bright yellow at first, becoming yellow to golden yellow and finally ocher when old, not turning blue when bruised, (Bessette)
STEM  3-10cm x 0.4-1(1.5)cm, equal or narrowing downward, solid, "yellow to dingy yellowish-buff or reddish", often stained dingy brown or reddish-brown in lower part; smooth, dry^, (Arora), 3-9cm x 0.5-1.3cm, nearly equal or ventricose at base; yellow with reddish tinges; often with distinct ribs near top, "scurfy or punctate with small reddish brown dots and points"; with yellow basal mycelium, (Bessette)
ODOR  not distinctive ^(Arora), not distinctive (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^, (Arora), not distinctive (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  yes (Arora)^, yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to widely scattered or in small groups (usually 2s or 3s) on ground in woods^, (Arora), scattered or in groups on the ground in hardwood forests under beech and oak, (Bessette), spring, summer, and fall, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown to yellowish-tinged or olive-tinged^ (Arora), yellowish ochraceous (Bessette), orange-brown to yellowish brown (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-15 x 3-6 um, narrowly elliptic to spindle-shaped, smooth^ (Arora), spores 8-14 x 3-5 um, elliptic to fusoid, smooth, pale yellowish (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'red-yellow'
SIMILAR  Phylloporus arenicola has an olive-brown to olive-ocher cap and adnexed to subdecurrent gills that do not stain blue when bruised, and immediate ammonia reaction to violaceous-fuscous then quickly mahogany red, (Bessette(3), but see NOTES under that species with regard to blue-staining). Phylloporus boletinoides A.H. Sm. & Thiers, found in eastern North America, "has a strongly decurrent boletinoid to sublamellate pore surface that sometimes stains dark blue to bluish green when bruised", (Bessette(3)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Kibby(1)*, Barron(1)*, O'Dell(1), Both(1)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Porphyrellus porphyrosporus  (Fr.) E.-J. Gilbert^  Skrifter udgivet af Videskabsselskabet i Christiania: 99. 1931;  Porphyrellus pseudoscaber  (Secr.) Singer;  Tylopilus pseudoscaber  (Secr.) A.H. Sm. and Thiers;  Boletus pseudoscaber  Secr.;  Tylopilus pacificus Wolfe, Biblthca Mycol. 69: 44 (1980) [1979] ?;  Porphyrellus pacificus (Wolfe) Singer, J. Garcia & L.D. Gomez  ?
ENGLISH NAME(S)  dark bolete, dusky bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry, velvety cap that is dark brown to olive-brown or dark vinaceous brown, 2) white flesh that turns blue then reddish brown when exposed, 3) pores that are brown when young, 4) tubes that become blue when bruised or exposed, and 5) cap-colored stem that may be reticulate.^ | Not all authors accept the synonymy used here. Differing species concepts may be involved as Singer (according to Bessette et al.) described Tylopilus porphyrosporus as a medium to large bolete growing at lower elevations in hardwood forests and mixed woods and having flesh that stains blue when exposed, and Tylopilus pseudoscaber as a small to medium bolete that grows at higher elevations in conifer woods and with flesh that does not stain blue when exposed (but in another source states slight blueing has occasionally been observed in T. pseudoscaber), (Bessette). Smith(35) on the other hand distinguishes T. porphyrosporus with pores pallid to grayish when young and flesh slowly becoming fuscous when cut from T. pseudoscaber with pores coffee brown when young and flesh staining blue then reddish when cut (while noting that the Fries species concept Boletus porphyrosporus that they are using does have flesh that turns blue). Tylopilus pacificus was originally described from Washington and is often regarded as a synonym too. Tylopilus olivaceobrunneus was originally described from Oregon (see under SIMILAR for different ideas about its status). By 2018 an attempt was being made to study these similar taxa and determine the status of entities on the west coast of North America. RANGE ^Porphyrellus porphyrosporus is found from eastern North America to the Pacific Northwest (Bessette), including MI (Smith(35), who is describing Pacific Northwest material), CA (Thiers, Phillips), and NS (Grund). It is found in WA (Trudell(1)). There are Paul Kroeger collections from BC labeled Tylopilus pseudoscaber at the University of British Columbia. According to D. Miller, pers. comm., more than a dozen ITS sequences in GenBank match each other, and these match a sequence from an OR collection.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle stains mahogany-red with application of KOH and negative with FeSO4, flesh stains dull orange with application of KOH, green with FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  5-15cm, convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; "dark brown to olive-brown, or dark vinaceous brown"; dry subtomentose [somewhat tomentose], sometimes finely areolate [cracked like dried mud] to conspicuously cracked when old^, (Bessette), 5.5-15cm, convex becoming flat-convex to nearly flat; "very dark earth-brown to olive-brown or dark vinaceous-brown"; dry, dull subtomentose, plush-like, "often appearing innately fibrillose when older, occasionally conspicuously finely areolate and rarely fibrillose-scaly", (Smith(35))
FLESH  white, turning blue when exposed, sometimes slowly, "then staining reddish brown and finally grayish brown to grayish black"^, (Bessette), 1-3cm thick at disc, soft, spongy; "white, turning to bright blue when cut and then in a few minutes reddish brown, finally dull brown", in stem whitish and not changing color readily, (Smith(35))
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "dark reddish brown to dark brown or blackish brown", turning dark greenish blue then reddish brown to dark grayish brown when bruised; tube layer 1.3-2cm thick, depressed near stem when old, turning blue when bruised^, (Bessette), small, round, dark yellow-brown (also described as coffee brown and with the Ridgway color "bister") when young, not stuffed; tube layer 1.5-2cm thick, "subdecurrent to shallowly depressed when young", becoming broadly and deeply depressed when old, pale grayish brown, occasionally grayish to pallid when older, "becoming greenish blue when bruised or exposed", (Smith(35))
STEM  4-12cm x 1-3cm, widening downward or nearly equal, solid; colored like cap, often with whitish base; dry, scurfy-punctate, with or without reticulation^, (Bessette), 4-10(12)cm x 1-3cm at top, equal to somewhat ventricose [wider in middle] to clavate [wider in lower part], solid-stuffed; colored as cap, often becoming black at base when old; dry, dull, evenly and minutely furfuraceous, "sometimes conspicuously ridged with long close lines with anastomoses, others only slightly reticulate and some not at all so", (Smith(35))
ODOR  pungent, clove-like or resembling coal tar when fresh, stem base sometimes with a strong odor of chlorine, (Bessette), sharp and rather pungent, strong, (Smith(35)), antiseptic-like (Miller)
TASTE  weakly bitter or mild^ (Bessette), mild (Smith(35)), bitter with an aftertaste (Miller)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or rarely in groups on ground "in mixed woods or conifer woods, sometimes growing on well-decayed logs and stumps"^, (Bessette), scattered to gregarious in hardwood and mixed forests along old roads, etc., (Smith(35)), summer and early fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark reddish brown^, (Bessette), distinctly reddish brown when moist, dark grayish brown when air-dried, (Smith(35))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-18 x 6-7.5 um, elliptic, smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores (12)14-18 x 6-7.5 um, (occasional giant spores up to 27 um long), narrowly elliptic, smooth, somewhat dextrinoid (tawny or more reddish) in Melzer's reagent, pale earth-brown in KOH, lacking apical pore; basidia 4-spored, 45-50 x 9-15 um, smoky brown in KOH, yellowish in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia scattered, 50-65 x 10-16 um, clavate-mucronate to fusoid-ventricose, usually with dark brown content when fresh, cheilocystidia similar to pleurocystidia or up to 30 um wide in enlarged part, brown when fresh; cap cuticle "a collapsed trichodermium, the hyphae with elliptic to subclavate end-cells with the tips tapered to an acute to subacute apex", walls thin and nearly colorless, "the cells back from the terminal cell often curved, of equal diameter, merely yellowish in Melzer's", the content of the hyphae colorless to yellowish in Melzer's, no amyloid particles present in most fruitbodies in the cuticular hyphae; clamp connections absent, (Smith(35))
NAME ORIGIN  porphyr- refers to a purple or dark red color
SIMILAR  Tylopilus olivaceobrunneus (Zeller & Bailey) A.H. Sm. (== Boletus olivaceobrunneus Zeller & Bailey, == Tylopilus porphyrosporus var. olivaceobrunneus (Zeller & F.D. Bailey) Wolfe) was considered a distinct species by Smith(8) and Smith(4), but as a synonym of Tylopilus pseudoscaber (Secretan) A. H. Smith and Thiers by Thiers(1), and Ammirati(1). In the online Species Fungorum, accessed October 23, 2018, it is listed as a synonym of Boletus edulis, but it is possible that different concepts are involved (further discussed in Both(1)). The taxon is said to grow on the ground under Douglas-fir in Oregon and Washington, with an olive-brown to blackish brown cap that stains darker when bruised, dull white flesh that does not stain blue when exposed, a brown pore surface that does not stain blue when exposed, a pale brown stem that is reticulated on the upper part, and spores that measure 13-16 x 5-6 um, (according to Bessette), with stem reticulate in upper half (as opposed to Porphyrellus porphyrosporus with non-reticulate stem) and growing under Sitka spruce (according to both Smith(4) and Smith(8)).
SOURCES  Smith(35) (as T. pseudoscaber), Bessette(3)* (as T. pseudoscaber), Thiers(1) (as T. pseudoscaber), Phillips(1)* (as T. pseudoscaber), Miller(14)* (as Tylopilus pseudoscaber), Arora(1)* (as T. pseudoscaber), Lincoff(2)* (as T. pseudoscaber), Lincoff(1)* (as Porphyrellus pseudoscaber), Ammirati(1)* (as T. pseudoscaber), Trudell(1), Trudell(4)*, Grund(11) (as T. pseudoscaber), Smith(4), Smith(8), Siegel(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Both(1) (discussing Boletus olivaceobrunneus)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Pulveroboletus ravenelii  (Berk. & M.A. Curtis) Murrill^  Mycologia 1: 9. 1909;  Boletus ravenelii  Berk. & M.A. Curtis
ENGLISH NAME(S)  powdery sulfur bolete, veiled sulfur bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a bright yellow powdery veil that covers the fruitbody at first, 2) the cap becoming orange-red to brownish red from the center outward, 3) the veil breaking to leave hanging veil remnants and an annulus, 4) the cap flesh turning blue when injured, 5) the pore surface bright yellow becoming dingy yellow to grayish brown, staining blue, then grayish brown when bruised, and 6) the stem bright yellow.^ RANGE ^Pulveroboletus ravenelii is found in OR but rare (collection at the University of Washington which also has collections from MS and TX), rare in CA (Del Norte and Mendocino Counties, Thiers), widely distributed from eastern Canada south to the Gulf of Mexico, west to TX, MI, and CA, and most abundant in the southeastern United States, (Smith). Oregon State University has collections from China and Japan.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  1-10cm, blunted rounded to convex, becoming nearly flat when old; bright sulfur yellow, becoming orange-red to brownish red from the center outward; dry and pulverulent [powdery] at first, becoming appressed-fibrillose to fibrillose-scaly, often slightly wrinkled or rimose [cracked] when old; margin typically with hanging veil remnants^, (Bessette), (1)2-6(10)cm, obtuse to convex to flat-convex to nearly flat; bright sulfur yellow overall, disc becoming orange-red to brownish red as pulverulence disappears; dry and pulverulent [powdery] or at times (when wet) somewhat tacky to viscid, when pulverulence disappears becoming felty-fibrillose, (Smith), background typically pinkish to orange brown or as pale as buff, with fibrils or streaks of reddish brown, veil elements intense yellow either unchanging or becoming paler yellow; surface viscid to subviscid but typically overlain with dry veil, tomentose to pulverulent due to veil layer, bald to appressed fibrillose when veil disappears, sometimes appearing fibrillose-scaly or eventually rimose-areolate [cracked like dried mud] when very old, margin often appendiculate with veil remnants, (Thiers)
FLESH  "white to pale yellow, slowly staining pale blue, then dingy yellow to pale brown when cut"^, (Bessette), about 1cm thick, floccose; "whitish to yellow, changing slowly to light blue and then drab-brown or dull yellowish where injured"; in stem whitish or soon pale yellowish and developing a pink tinge where bruised, (Smith), whitish to pale yellow, slowly changing to blue when exposed, sometimes becoming brown or yellow when exposed; in stem pale ochraceous to whitish except intensely yellow in base, unchanging or developing pinkish tinge when exposed, (Thiers)
PORES  1-3 per mm, angular to nearly circular; bright yellow, becoming dingy yellow to grayish brown when mature, "staining greenish blue, then grayish brown when bruised"; tube layer 0.5-0.8cm thick^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm, round or nearly so to angular; bright yellow when undamaged; tube layer 0.5-0.8cm thick, adnate, sometimes becoming slightly depressed around stem, soon primuline-yellow to lemon-yellow or more olive-tinted, changing to greenish blue then dusky brown and finally black when bruised, (Smith)
STEM  4-14.5cm x 0.6-1.6cm, equal or widening downward, solid; bright sulfur-yellow, bright yellow and smooth above the annulus; sheathed from the base upward with tiny appressed fibrils; "partial veil membranous and powdery, bright sulfur-yellow, typically leaving a prominent, but sometimes inconspicuous superior annulus"^, (Bessette), 4-9(15)cm x 0.4-1.2(1.6)cm, equal or narrowing upward toward top or sometimes variously irregular in shape, solid; "covered at least over the lower part with yellow pulverulence from a dry veil", the veil rarely leaving a faint annulus, surface brilliant yellow; dry to moist; with yellowish to white rhizomorphs around the base, (Smith), universal veil leaving yellow powder on cap and stem, partial veil yellow, leaving fragile ring on stem or fragments about cap margin, (Lincoff), sometimes appearing somewhat annulate (Thiers)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), slight (Smith), pleasant (Miller)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), acidulous (Smith), rather sour (Phillips), mild but soon unpleasant (Miller)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on ground in woods^, (Bessette), mostly single to scattered in pine woods as well as hardwoods, (Smith), on the ground, in mixed pine, hemlock, and deciduous stands, spruce woods, and laurel and rhododendron thickets, (Lincoff), in mixed forests, most commonly under oaks (Thiers for California), summer, fall, and winter, (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive gray to olive brown^, (Bessette), smoky olive (Smith from Coker & Beers), dark olive brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-10.5 x 4-5 um, elliptic to oval, smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 8-10.5 x 4-5 um, ventricose to more or less ovate, smooth, colorless outer sheath inconspicuous, in Melzer's reagent pale rusty brown, in KOH dingy ochraceous-tawny; basidia 4-spored, 24-36 x 10-14 um, pale lemon-yellow in KOH, not as bright in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia "scattered, 30-46 x 7-12 um, sometimes inconspicuous, fusoid-ventricose", colorless to lemon-yellow in KOH, smooth, thin-walled, cheilocystidia basidium-like or similar to pleurocystidia; cap cuticle a tangled mass of narrow (2.5-6 um wide) curved hyphae yellow to colorless in KOH and reddish brown in Melzer's reagent, no gelatinizing elements even when young; clamp connections absent, (Smith), spores 8-12 x 4-6.5 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth, (Arora), veil tissue composed of narrow, loosely interwoven hyphae staining yellow in KOH, no sphaerocysts apparent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  named for mycologist H.W. Ravenel (1814-1887)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Thiers(1), Lincoff(2)*, Miller(14)*, Phillips(1)*, Arora(1), Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Rubroboletus eastwoodiae  (Murrill) Vasquez, Simonini, Svetash., Miksik & Vizzini^  in Tibpromma et al., Fungal Diversity 83: 200. 2017;  Rubroboletus eastwoodiae  (Murrill) D. Arora, C. F. Schwarz & J.L. Frank  in Frank, Index Fungorum 248: 1. 2015 (invalid new name);  Boletus eastwoodiae  (Murrill) Sacc. & Trotter  Syll. fung. (Abellini) 21: 237. 1912;  Suillellus eastwoodiae  Murrill  N. Amer. Fl. (New York) 9(3): 152. 1910;  Boletus satanas  Lenz (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  Alice Eastwood's Boletus
NOTES  {See also Red-pored Boletus Table.} Rubroboletus eastwoodiae is recognized by red pores, a light gray to olive-buff cap with pink flushes, and an abruptly bulbous reticulate stem.^ |This taxon has been known as Boletus satanas Lenz in California and the Pacific Northwest. Thiers and Halling in 1976 examined the type of Boletus eastwoodiae (Murrill) Sacc. & Trotter and found it to be Boletus satanas: what had passed in California under the name Boletus eastwoodiae was renamed Boletus pulcherrimus Thiers and Halling, (Both(1)). However, European material of Boletus satanas has a different coloration of the cap and lacks the abruptly bulbous base (the separation of the two should be established by molecular methods). |To summarize the confusing issue, Boletus eastwoodiae was the name for what used to be called Boletus satanas, and Boletus pulcherrimus the name for what used to be called Boletus eastwoodiae! Boletus eastwoodiae then became Rubroboletus eastwoodiae and Boletus pulcherrimus became Rubroboletus pulcherrimus. |The proposed new name, Rubroboletus eastwoodiae (Murrill) D. Arora, C.F. Schwarz & J.L. Frank, was considered invalid because the type of the name is not indicated properly. Index Fungorum no. 248 indicated the Basionym as Boletus eastwoodiae (Murrill) Sacc. & Trotter, when it should have been Suillellus eastwoodiae Murrill. In 2017 the name was published validly by other authors. RANGE ^This taxon is found in CA (Bessette), rare in OR (vouchered at Oregon State University), and rare in WA (recorded by Michael Beug, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  7-30cm, convex to broadly convex or nearly flat when old; "pallid to gray or olive-buff", becoming suffused with pink when old, especially toward margin; "dry, smooth or sometimes cracked"^, (Arora), 10-20cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex when old, typically massive in appearance; pale gray with pink blushes, younger caps often predominantly gray with only traces of pink, but in older caps pink shades typically predominate, especially near margin, but in some very old caps, the pink is absent and the cap near olive-gray; dry, unpolished, bald to obscurely fibrillose to occasionally fibrillose-scaly, unchanging or often becoming diffracted to rimose-areolate [cracked like dried mud] on disc, or becoming obscurely scaly on disc and finely velvety to subtomentose to bald toward margin, (Thiers(1)), grayish with pink tones when young, becoming pale olive-buff to pale olive-gray and developing stronger pink tones, especially along margin when old; dry, bald, often rimose when old, (Bessette)
FLESH  white to yellow, turning blue when bruised, especially when young or near tubes^, (Arora), 2-5cm thick, yellow, turning blue readily on exposure when young, sometimes erratically when older; in stem colored the same, turning blue when exposed except in basal part, (Thiers(1))
PORES  deep red becoming red to pink or when old orange to yellowish, "turning blue to blue-black when bruised; tubes yellow to greenish"^, (Arora), very small, up to 2 per mm, when young reddish brown, when older often fading to red, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, arcuate-decurrent to subdecurrent when young, becoming deeply to shallowly depressed when old, yellow, turning blue when exposed or bruised, (Thiers(1)), about 2 per mm, reddish brown when very young, becoming red, then fading to reddish orange to orange-yellow when old, turning blue when bruised, (Bessette), pores slightly irregular (Lincoff)
STEM  6-15cm x 2-6cm at top, with massive abrupt bulb at base up to 15cm wide and especially prominent when young, solid, firm; upper part colored more or less as cap, bulb usually pink or pinkish red, but pink tones usually more evident when young and tending to disappear when old; upper part finely but distinctly reticulate (reticulation usually reddish)^, (Arora), 6-12cm x 3.5-7cm at top, abruptly bulbous, bulb 9-14cm wide, stem solid; color similar to cap, bulb often pinkish vinaceous; dry, conspicuously reticulate over upper part or entire surface, the reticulation pink to vinaceous, bald to obscurely tomentose over non-reticulate part, sometimes streaked and appearing appressed-fibrillose, (Thiers(1)), abruptly bulbous with ventricose base, pinkish when young, becoming grayish with pinkish tints when mature, covered with pinkish to pinkish vinaceous reticulation on upper part or sometimes nearly overall; partial veil absent, (Bessette)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Thiers(1)), cabbage or dung when mature (Lincoff)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Thiers(1))
EDIBILITY  poisonous, at least raw, causing vomiting, diarrhea, and severe cramps, (Arora)
HABITAT  single or in groups under oaks^, (Arora), single to gregarious under oaks, (Thiers(1)), Washington collection associated with Quercus garryana (Oregon White Oak), (Michael Beug, pers. comm.), late fall and winter (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown to olive-brown^ (Arora), brown (Thiers(1))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-15 x 4-6 um, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), typically 11-14.4 x 4-6 um, occasional giant spores up to 17.6 x 8 um or 16 x 4 um, subelliptic to subfusoid or subcylindric, smooth, bright ochraceous in Melzer's reagent, ochraceous in KOH, moderately thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 24-28 x 7-10 um, clavate, ochraceous in KOH; hymenial cystidia deeply embedded and often obscure, 28-42 x 7-11 um, clavate to fusoid to basidioid, colorless when young often becoming ochraceous when old; tube trama "obscurely divergent from central strand", colorless to yellow in KOH, "hyphal walls typically appearing incrusted or nodulose"; cap cuticle a loosely interwoven trichoderm, colorless to ochraceous in KOH; cap trama interwoven, colorless in KOH, some septa amyloid, hyphae 3-5 um wide; stem cuticle a closely packed turf of inflated to cylindric hyphal tips; clamp connections absent, (Thiers(1))
NAME ORIGIN  Rubroboletus eastwoodiae is named after the collector of the type, Miss Alice Eastwood. Incidentally the name Boletus satanas is after Satan, the ecclesiastical Latin name for whom is "Satanas". Some authors including Alexander Smith and the Millers call that species Boletus satanus but this usage is hard to defend. The ecclesiastical Latin for "Satan" is "Satanas" and Lenz presumably used this noun as the species name - there is no reason to correct it to "satanus".
SIMILAR  Rubroboletus pulcherrimus has a dark reddish brown cap, dark red (as opposed to paler and more vinaceous red) pores, and the stem is clavate or bulbous (as opposed to typically abruptly bulbous) with dark red reticulations, it is found in coniferous and mixed woods (not with oak), and microscopically the spores are larger, none of the septa of the tramal hyphae are amyloid, and the hyphae of the cap cuticle have noticeably roughened walls, (Thiers(1) comparing B. satanas to what was then generally known as B. eastwoodiae and which he and Halling later renamed B. pulcherrimus). Rubroboletus haematinus lacks the abrupt bulbous stem, has no gray color on cap or pink tones on stem, and grows with conifers. Microscopically, none of the septa of the cap trama are amyloid, and spores are larger. 
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus satanas not B. eastwoodiae), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus satanas), Arora(1)* (as Boletus satanas), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus satanas), Miller(14)* (as Boletus satanus), Thiers(9) (discussing B. pulcherrimus), Both(1), AroraPocket(1)* (as Boletus satanas), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus eastwoodiae), Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Rubroboletus haematinus  (Halling) D. Arora & J.L. Frank^ in Frank, Index Fungorum 248: 1. 2015;  Boletus haematinus  Halling  Mycologia 68: 980-983. 1976
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  {See also Red-pored Boletus Table.} Features include 1) a pale brown cap, 2) pale yellow flesh that stains blue when cut, 3) pores that are yellow when young, and soon become pale red to dark red when old, but nearly always remain yellow near the cap margin, and 4) pale yellow club-shaped stem that is finely reticulate.^ | The description is from Bessette except where indicated. RANGE ^Rubroboletus haematinus is found in ID and CA (Bessette), and WA (Castellano). It has been reported from MT (Larry Evans, pers. comm.). It has been sequenced from OR (D. Miller, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  7-20cm, cushion-shaped to convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old, margin "incurved at first, wavy, with a band of sterile tissue"; "yellow-brown to olive-brown or pale brown with reddish tints, especially on the margin", sometimes darker brown when old; dry, bald at first becoming appressed-fibrillose and rimose areolate [cracked to cracked like dried mud] when old^, (Bessette), (6)11-26(25)cm, light brown during all stages, occasionally bruising dark brown on disc when young, areoles becoming dark brown when old, frequently reddish tinges along margin and occasionally this color over most of surface, (Thiers)
FLESH  yellow, turning blue when cut^, (Bessette), 2-6cm thick, yellow, changing to blue on exposure except in stem base, eventually fading, (Thiers)
PORES  2-3 per mm, angular; "bright yellow when very young, soon becoming pale red, then dark red, and finally reddish brown in age, blueing when bruised"; tube layer 0.8-2cm thick, often depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), pores "bright yellow when young, soon becoming pale red to dark red in age but nearly always remain yellow near the pileus margin, blueing where bruised", tubes 1-1.5cm long, yellow when young, becoming green-yellow to olive when old, turning blue on exposure, (Castellano)
STEM  5-12cm x 2.5-6cm, widening downward, sometimes somewhat bulbous (but not abruptly bulbous) at first, often nearly equal when mature, solid; yellow to pale yellow; dry, covered on upper two thirds or at least upper part with red reticulation [network]^, (Bessette), reticulations pale red, occasionally breaking up and appearing granulose; base staining brown when handled, (Thiers)
ODOR  not distinctive
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  unknown but possible dangerous (Bessette)
HABITAT  associated with fir species, especially A. lasiocarpa in Washington and A. magnifica in the Sierra Nevada mountains, apparently limited to subalpine forests^, (Castellano), on ground under mountain conifers, especially red fir, (Bessette) 
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-15 x 6-8 um, elliptic to fusiform-elliptic [spindle-shaped - elliptic], smooth, pale ochraceous^, (Bessette), basidia 4-spored; hymenial cystidia 40-45 x 7.5-9 um, obclavate to ventricose-rostrate, colorless; clamp connections absent, (Castellano), spores 12-15 x 6-7.5 um, elliptic to fusiform-elliptic in face view, subfusiform and inequilateral in side view, smooth, moderately thick-walled, pale ochraceous in KOH, ochraceous in Melzer's; basidia 4-spored, 25.5-39 x 10.5-13.5 um, clavate, colorless and sometimes guttulate in KOH, inamyloid; hymenial cystidia rare to infrequent and buried, 40-45 x 7.5-9 um, obclavate to ventricose-rostrate, colorless in KOH; cap cuticle a trichodermium of tangled repent hyphae, heavily incrusted in punctate or spiral fashion; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "blood-red"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Rubroboletus pulcherrimus has a dark red pore surface that becomes orange-red, then brownish red when old, and a dull red to pale reddish brown reticulate stem, (Bessette). Rubroboletus eastwoodiae has a grayish cap with pink tones and a massive abruptly bulbous stem, (Bessette using the name Boletus satanas). Rubroboletus haematinus differs from R. pulcherrimus in the shape and color of the stem, the yellow color of the pores when young, the absence of reddish brown coloration in the cap, and the mycorrhizal associate, (Thiers). R. haematinus is distinguished from Rubroboletus eastwoodiae by the different mycorrhizal associate, the absence of an exaggerated bulbous stem, the absence of gray color on the cap or pink color tones on the stem, larger spores, and septa on hyphae of cap trama that are not amyloid, (Thiers).

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Rubroboletus pulcherrimus  (Thiers & Halling) D. Arora, N. Siegel & J.L. Frank^  in Frank, Index Fungorum 248: 1. 2015;  Boletus pulcherrimus  Thiers and Halling  Mycologia 68: 977-980. 1976
ENGLISH NAME(S)  red-pored bolete
NOTES  {See also Red-pored Boletus Table.} Features include dark reddish brown, tomentose to fibrillose cap, dark red pores, yellow flesh and tubes that turn blue, and club-shaped to somewhat bulbous stem colored pale reddish brown with dark red reticulations.^ | Thiers and Halling in 1976 examined the type of Boletus eastwoodiae (Murrill) Saccardo & Trotter and found it to be Boletus satanas (treated here as Rubroboletus eastwoodiae): what had passed in California under the name Boletus eastwoodiae was renamed Boletus pulcherrimus, (Thiers(9)). RANGE ^Rubroboletus pulcherrimus is found in WA, OR, and CA, (Thiers), and BC and NM, (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  8-20cm, convex, expanding, sometimes to flat or irregular in outline; reddish brown when young, usually with reddish tones particularly near margin, unchanging when old or darkening somewhat, moist to dry, bald to subtomentose to sometimes slightly velvety when young, unchanging or becoming obscurely appressed-fibrillose when old, often when very old somewhat areolate [cracked like dried mud] and obscurely fibrillose scaly, the scales often developing grayish tips^, (Thiers), "reddish brown at first, becoming olive-brown to grayish on the fibrils over a pinkish red to dull red ground color, distinctly pinkish red to dull red along the margin"; margin incurved at first, usually with a narrow band of sterile tissue, (Bessette)
FLESH  2-4cm thick, bright yellow, changing to blue when first exposed then often fading to pale bluish yellow^, (Thiers)
PORES  up to 1 mm, angular; dark red when young, changing to reddish brown when old, often yellow near margin, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 0.5-1.5cm thick, adnate to adnexed when young, usually depressed when old, yellow, turning blue immediately on exposure^, (Thiers), 2-3 per mm, angular to irregular, dark red at first, becoming orange-red, then brownish red, instantly turning blue when bruised, (Bessette)
STEM  7-16cm x 2-5cm, widening to 10cm thick at base, club-shaped to bulbous but not abruptly bulbous, sometimes equal especially when young, solid; pale reddish brown, base often staining brown, turning blue elsewhere when bruised; dry, dark red reticulation at least over upper 2/3^, (Thiers), partial veil and annulus absent (Bessette)
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers)
TASTE  mild^ (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  poisonous, causing severe gastrointestinal distress, at least one fatality, (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to gregarious in humus in mixed woods^, (Thiers), single or scattered on ground in conifer or mixed woods, August to January, (Bessette), summer, fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Thiers), olive-brown (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-16 x 5.5-6.5 um, subelliptic to subfusoid, smooth, not dextrinoid, ochraceous in KOH, moderately thick-walled^; basidia 1-4-spored, 35-40 x 9-12 um; hymenial cystidia scattered, 33-60 x 8-12 um, fusoid-ventricose to subclavate to basidioid, colorless to ochraceous in KOH; cap cuticle a trichodermium of tangled hyphae with noticeably roughened walls; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "very beautiful"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Rubroboletus haematinus lacks red brown color in the cap, has a differently colored and shaped stem, has yellow pores when young, and is associated particularly with mountain conifers especially Red Fir, (Thiers). Rubroboletus eastwoodiae has 1) a pale gray to olive-buff cap (rather than brown), 2) paler and more vinaceous red pores, 3) typically abruptly bulbous stem (as opposed to clavate or bulbous), 4) association with oak, and 5) spores that are smaller, and 6) septa of some of the tramal hyphae that are amyloid, (Thiers comparing Boletus "satanas" - here treated as Rubroboletus eastwoodiae - to what was then generally known as B. eastwoodiae). See also SIMILAR section of Neoboletus 'luridiformis'.
SOURCES  Thiers(9) (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Castellano(1)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Arora(1) (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Ammirati(1)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), AroraPocket(1)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus pulcherrimus), Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillellus amygdalinus (Thiers) Vizzini, Simonini & Gelardi^  in Vizzini, Index Fungorum 188: 1. 2014;  Boletus amygdalinus  (Thiers) Thiers  California Mushrooms  pp. 50-51. 1975;  Boletus puniceus  Thiers
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  {See also Red-pored Boletus Table.} Features include 1) a rose-red to ocher-red cap that bruises blue, 2) yellow flesh that turns blue when exposed, 3) red pore surface that bruises blue, and 4) yellow stem with reddish granules but no reticulation.^ RANGE ^Suillellus amygdalinus is found occasionally from coastal central CA into southern OR (Bessette). This species or one that matches its appearance occurs in Washington (M. Beug, pers. comm.)
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^application of KOH - flesh yellow to pale orange, cap cuticle red but quickly darkening; FeSO4 - flesh negative to pale gray, cap cuticle negative to pale gray, (Thiers)
CAP  6-10cm wide, convex becoming flat-convex to flat, often highly irregular or undulating, margin incurved becoming downcurved; typically dark rose to red when young, unchanging or changing to dark brownish red on the disc when older, sometimes changing to reddish brown towards margin; "dry, matted-tomentose to cottony fibrillose to appressed fibrillose, usually not appearing fibrillose scaly or rimose"^, (Thiers), 6-12cm wide, convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old, margin incurved at first, even; dark rose-red to ocher-red when young, becoming dark brownish red to pale brown with reddish tints when old, bruising dark blue; dry, appressed fibrillose, sometimes finely rimose (cracked) when old, (Bessette)
FLESH  1-2cm thick, yellow, turning blue immediately with exposure, in stem yellow, turning blue immediately with exposure^, (Thiers), "yellow, instantly blueing when exposed", (Bessette), Laessoe(3) said that Suillellus is characterized "by fleshy fruitbodies with fine orange to red pores, strong and fast blue staining on all parts, and wine-red flesh in the stem base", so it would not be a surprise if there were wine-red in the stem flesh here, although they are talking about European species
PORES  0.5-1mm wide, angular; 'red ("ox-blood red" to "dragons-blood red" or "ferruginous" to "vinaceous-rufous")', turning blue immediately on bruising; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, shallowly to deeply depressed; dark yellow, turning blue on exposure^, (Thiers), pores 1-2 per mm, angular, pore surface "cinnabar-red to pale rusty red or dull red, instantly blueing when exposed"; tube layer 0.9-1.5cm thick, (Bessette)
STEM  5-9cm x 1-3cm at top, typically bent near base, equal to ventricose (wider in middle), solid; yellow, usually masked with red granules or tomentum, turning blue when bruised; dry to moist, bald at top, typically becoming tomentose to granulose toward base, not reticulate^, (Thiers), 4-10cm x 1.3-3cm, nearly equal or sometimes ventricose (wider in middle); "yellow and often obscured by brick-red to cinnabar-red granules", bruising blue; without reticulation; partial veil and ring absent, (Bessette)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Thiers, Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Thiers, Bessette)
EDIBILITY  avoid on the basis of the general rule about avoiding red-pored boletes
HABITAT  "solitary, scattered or in groups on ground, typically under oak and manzanita, but also reported under redwoods", October to January^, (Bessette), fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive-brown^ (Thiers), olive-brown (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11.2-16 x 5.2-8 um, elliptic to subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, thick-walled, often appearing 2-celled because of 2 distinct vacuoles^, ochraceous in KOH, dark ochraceous in Melzer's; basidia 4-spored, 30-35 x 9-11 um, clavate, guttulate; hymenial cystidia scattered, 45-54 x 10-12 um, fusoid to fusoid-ventricose with tapering apices, colorless, relatively obscure, sometimes appearing incrusted; cap cuticle "a trichodermium of interwoven hyphae with numerous free hyphal tips, frequently tapering toward the free end, not appearing cystidioid, pale ochraceous in KOH, hyphae +/- 8 um wide"; cap trama interwoven, homogeneous; stem cuticle "differentiated as a mass of caulocystidia or free hyphal tips, highly irregular in shape and size, ochraceous in KOH, sometimes appearing incrusted"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 11-16 x 5-8 um, elliptic to subfusoid, smooth, thick-walled, ochraceous, (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to almond'
SIMILAR  Neoboletus 'luridiformis' has a darker cap and pore colors. The cap is dark brown and pores are dark red. It is also smaller, with different spores, smaller cystidia, laticifers in cap trama, and a different type of stem cuticle, (Thiers). N. 'luridiformis' has a dark brown to nearly blackish brown cap, yellow stem that is densely red-pruinose, narrower spores with thinner walls, and habitat under hardwoods or conifers, (Bessette).
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus) (colors in double quotation marks from Ridgway(1)), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus), Siegel(2)*, Laessoe(3)

FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus acidus  (Peck) Singer^  Farlowia 2(2): 271. 1945;  Suillus subolivaceus  A.H. Sm. and Thiers  Contribution Toward a Monograph of North American Suillus pp.68-70. 1964;  Suillus intermedius  (A.H. Sm. & Thiers) A.H. Sm. & Thiers
ENGLISH NAME(S)  slippery Jill, olive-capped Suillus
NOTES  Features include 1) an olive-brown to yellow-brown, glutinous cap that appears streaked-fibrillose below the gluten and typically has hanging veil remnants on the margin, 2) whitish to pale yellow flesh that is unchanging or stains dingy pinkish, 3) olive-grayish-buff to yellowish pores that are often beaded with droplets, 4) whitish to yellowish stem, 5) a large band-like annulus on stem, and 6) conspicuous glandular dots on stem that become blackish when old.^ | Regarding the synonymy of Suillus subolivaceus with Suillus acidus, Nguyen(3) wrote, "The /acidus clade contained sequences from the holotypes of S. lutescens A.H. Sm. & Thiers and S. subolivaceus A.H. Sm. & Thiers. We were unable to obtain a sequence for the type of S. acidus (Peck) Singer, but all specimens of what we identified as S. acidus based on morphology fit into this clade. (Smith and Thiers 1964a) thought that S. lutescens was closely related to S. acidus, and our sequence data support that relationship. To confirm the correct name for this clade, the holotype sequences for S. acidus will need to be obtained. Given the age of the holotype (before 1905), epitypification may be the best approach." When accessed on February 18, 2018, the online Species Fungorum gave the current name as Suillus acidus, and MycoBank gave Suillus subolivaceus as separate or a variety of Suillus acidus. Suillus acidus is rather common in WA and OR, (Smith(34) as S. subolivaceus). RANGE ^Suillus acidus has been found in WA and OR (Smith), the northern Rocky Mountains, south to OR and ID, (Bessette), and it has been recorded from BC (Schalkwijk-Barendsen). A collection from BC is at the University of British Columbia, and a collection from AK at the University of Washington (both as S. subolivaceus). The University of Washington has collections from WA and ID, and Oregon State University has collections from OR (all as S. subolivaceus). It is found in MT (L. Evans, pers. comm. as S. subolivaceus).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap surface stains dark brown to black with application of KOH, flesh stains lavender with application of KOH and grayish brown with FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  5-10cm, obtuse to convex at first, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat and often broadly umbonate when old, margin becoming flat to uplifted; olive-brown to brown, tan, or olive-tan; glutinous, appressed-fibrillose under gluten; margin typically with hanging veil remnants^, (Bessette), 5-10cm, broadly convex to nearly flat, "or obtuse and expanding to broadly umbonate"; dark dingy yellow-brown to olive-brown or dingy olive; glutinous and appearing streaked beneath by agglutinated fibrils, (Smith), 4-15cm, dull olive to olive-brown, grayish olive-brown, dingy yellow-brown, or dingy tan, (Arora)
FLESH  "whitish to pale yellow, unchanging or staining dingy pinkish when exposed"^, (Bessette), up to 1cm, spongy; pallid to yellowish or olivaceous gray, unchanging when bruised; in stem yellowish in upper part, pallid to brownish in lower part, (Smith)
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular; "yellowish to olive-buff and often beaded with droplets at first, becoming dingy olive, unchanging when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, adnate to subdecurrent^, (Bessette), 1.5-2 per mm when old, about 1 mm when young, colored as tubes more or less or slightly browner, unchanging when bruised; tube layer about 1cm thick when mature, adnate becoming somewhat decurrent, grayish buff to olive-buff when young and beaded with drops of a clear to cloudy exudate, dingy yellow when mature, (Smith), grayish olive to grayish buff or olive-buff when young and often beaded with clear droplets in wet weather, becoming dingy yellowish to brownish yellow when old (and droplets often drying blackish); tubes same color or yellower, (Arora)
STEM  6-10cm x 1-1.6cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; whitish at first, becoming dull yellowish; with pinkish brown glandular dots that darken to blackish when old or when handled, dry; partial veil whitish and floccose on inner layer, olive-brown and gelatinous on outer layer, forming "collapsed, median to superior, band-like, olive-brown, gelatinous sheathing annulus that is often thickened on the lower edge"^, (Bessette), 6-10cm x 0.8-1.4cm, equal, solid; yellowish in upper part and pallid in lower part; "conspicuously covered with glandular dots which are pinkish brown but soon blacken from handling and nearly always dry blackish"; annulus median to superior, membranous with an outer gelatinous layer colored like cap, often sheathing but not flaring at lower edge, inner layer white and floccose, annulus shrinking rapidly after breaking, (Smith), 6-12(17)cm x 0.8-2cm, white or yellowish above annulus, whitish to brownish below the annulus, glandular dots will stain fingers brown, (Arora), conspicuous band-like annulus "fastened to the stem at its middle, with its upper and lower edges free and covered on the outside with brown or olive slime", (Ammirati)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), slight (Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), acid (Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under mixed conifers^, (Bessette), gregarious under mixed conifers (Smith), scattered to gregarious under conifers, particularly Western White Pine, (Arora)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), dingy cinnamon (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 3-4.5 um, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale ochraceous^, (Bessette), spores (8)9-11 x 3-4(4.5) um, typically subfusoid, smooth, pale ochraceous to greenish transparent in KOH, walls slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 18-23 x 5-6(7) um, clavate, colorless in KOH; pleurocystidia 40-55 x 6-11 um, "subcylindric to narrowly clavate or subfusoid with obtuse apices", as revived in KOH either colorless or with yellowish brown content, the cystidia in fascicles with rusty brown incrusting pigment around the base as revived in KOH, cheilocystidia "scattered and in groups, like the pleurocystidia but often longer and frequently (as revived in KOH) with so much surrounding encrusting pigment that the cystidia themselves are obscured"; cap epicutis a thick layer of gelatinous hyphae 3-7 um wide "and with some bister incrusting pigment seen as revived in KOH, the hyphae as revived appearing to be appressed and rather crooked"; caulocystidia up to 70-x 12 um but mostly 40-60 x 6-10 um, "content of most of them dark yellow brown as revived in KOH", "in dense fascicles and with a copious amount of incrusting pigment"; clamp connections none, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "about the color of a fresh olive"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Suillus flavidus is smaller and has a paler cap, a slimmer stem, and less prominent glandular dots on the stem, (Bessette). S. flavidus has pale yellowish pores when young, has a gelatinous annulus, and grows with Lodgepole Pine, (Arora). See also SIMILAR section of Suillus luteus and Suillus flavidus.
SOURCES  Smith(34) (as S. subolivaceus), Bessette(3)* (as S. subolivaceus), Arora(1)* (as S. subolivaceus), Ammirati(1)* (as S. subolivaceus), Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (as S. subolivaceus), Nguyen(3), Marrone(1)*

FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus albivelatus  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and O.K. Mill.^  Lloydia 28: 121-123. 1965
ENGLISH NAME(S)  northern pine bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a viscid to glutinous cap that is orange-brown, becoming vinaceous brown or brownish red to yellow-brown when old, 2) a cap margin with scattered white scales or hanging veil remnants, 3) white flesh that may stain vinaceous, 4) round pores that are buff to yellow or orange buff, 5) dry stem that is yellowish to buff at the top and white in the lower part, the stem sometimes having a few glandular dots, and often having a fleeting ragged annular zone, and 6) growth under conifers especially pine.^ | The holotype of Suillus albivelatus is albivelatus is one substitution and 1 indel different in the ITS region than one of Smith's original 1964 collections of S. pseudobrevipes, so it may be that they are not separate species (D. Miller, pers. comm.). But the phylogenetic tree in Nguyen(3) shows a clear separation. RANGE ^Suillus albivelatus is found in the Pacific Northwest and the northern Rocky Mountains, south to ID and northwestern MT, (Bessette). There is a WA collection at the University of Washington. It has also been reported from BC (M. Beug, pers. comm.) and AB (Schalkwijk-Barendsen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH on flesh gives pink then lilac-drab reaction, on cap cuticle no reaction, FeSO4 no reaction, (Smith)
CAP  4-12cm, convex becoming nearly flat when old; orange-brown on young specimens, becoming vinaceous brown or brownish red to yellow-brown when old; bald, viscid to glutinous; margin with scattered white squamules or appendiculate^, (Bessette(3)), 4-12cm, convex to broadly convex; pallid when covered by duff, when exposed soon pale vinaceous brown then slowly changing to dull cinnamon to dull yellow-brown, finally dingy ochraceous; buttons 0.2-1cm wide with a white-fibrillose veil over the glutinous to viscid surface, the veil breaking up into fine fibrillose scales then disappearing, in caps 2.5-8cm wide veil usually visible as white floccose scales over marginal area (or sometimes over all) and the margin toothed with remnants, finally all traces removed, especially in wet weather, when old often virgate (streaked) under the gluten; at times with part of the margin intergrown with stem, (Smith)
FLESH  white, becoming buff to lemon-yellow when old, especially near tubes, "unchanging or staining vinaceous when cut"^, (Bessette(3)), white but soon lemon-yellow, staining reddish around larval tunnels; in stem the base staining vinaceous brown from larval damage; "the interior surface and to some extent the exterior becomes vinaceous-lilac to vinaceous-pink" [unclear which part(s) of fruitbody this refers to], (Smith)
PORES  1-3 per mm, round; buff at first, becoming yellow to orange-buff when mature; tube layer 0.3-0.6cm thick, often depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette(3)), round, minute at first, finally up to 1mm when old, colored as tubes (pale ocher-yellow), not changing when bruised; tube layer 0.5-1cm thick, varying from depressed around the stem to decurrent, dull pale ocher-yellow, unchanging when cut, (Smith), pores somewhat boletinoid [radially arranged], (Bessette(1))
STEM  1-4cm x 1.5-3cm, nearly equal, solid; yellowish to buff at top, white in lower part; dry, smooth or with a few glandular dots usually near top; "sometimes with a ragged annular zone that soon disappears"^, (Bessette(3)), 1-4cm x 1.5-2.5cm, more or less equal, solid; white and becoming bright yellow, base more or less clay color from handling; lacking dots when young and when old lacking dots or with a few minute dots showing near top; veil at times (about 50%) leaving a poorly formed zone of fine scales midway on the stem, the fine scales whitish or discoloring to vinaceous fawn; reference is also made to the "annulus when present", (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette(3))
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on ground under conifers, especially ponderosa pine and other pines, June to August^, (Bessette(3)), late summer and early fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  cinnamon-brown to buff^ (Bessette(3)), near cinnamon-buff after moisture has escaped (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-8.5 x 2.5-3 um, elliptic, smooth, yellowish^, (Bessette(3)), 7-8.5 x 2.8-3 um, oblong, smooth, yellowish in KOH, only slightly more yellowish brown in Melzer's reagent; basidia 4-spored, many with red content; pleurocystidia in clusters, 38-50 x 8-12 um individually, subfusoid, clavate or cylindric, content and area surrounding cluster vinaceous-red in KOH; cap epicutis of hyphae 4-9 um wide, "appressed on cap surface (possibly a collapsed trichodermium), gelatinous as revived in KOH and with numerous minute granules in the gelatinous matrix, the granules +/- yellowish in KOH and in Melzer's sol."; caulocystidia in clusters or scattered and similar to pleurocystidia; clamp connections none, (Smith), clamp connections present on all tissues (Bessette(1))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "with a white veil"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Suillus pseudobrevipes has a veil that sheaths the stem, the cut flesh does not show vinaceous lilac or vinaceous pink tone, and the pleurocystidia are rusty brown in KOH rather than vinaceous-red, (Smith). Suillus brevipes lacks a partial veil (Bessette(1)). Suillus glandulosipes does not have a complete outer veil at any stage, and does not have vinaceous colors in the stem except when old.
SOURCES  Smith(36), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4)*, Bessette(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Nguyen(3)
