FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus punctatipes  (Snell and E.A. Dick) Snell & E.A. Dick^  Mycologia 53: 235. 1961
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a viscid to glutinous, often streaked cap that is brown to dark brown when young, often becoming purplish brown or cinnamon to orange-cinnamon when old, 2) white flesh, 3) large, cream to ocher-yellow pores that are elongated and radially arranged, and 4) a white to yellow stem that has brownish vinaceous to dark brown dots and smears and often stains brownish but has no partial veil or annulus.^ RANGE ^Suillus punctatipes is found in WA (Smith(34)), ID (Smith(36)), from MI to Pacific Northwest and CA (Bessette), OR (collections at Oregon State University), and BC (collections at the University of British Columbia).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  4-15cm, hemispheric to convex becoming broadly convex when old; brown to dark brown when young, often becoming purplish brown or cinnamon to orange-cinnamon when old; bald, viscid to glutinous, often streaked; margin even or sometimes with narrow band of sterile tissue^, (Bessette), up to 10cm broad, hemispheric at first, becoming broadly convex; pinkish brown when moist, drying avellaneous at least in spots; very viscid when wet, bald except margin which is appressed-tomentose, pellicle separable, (Smith(34)), 8-16(20)cm, convex or irregular from mutual pressure, broadly convex when old or margin spreading and wavy; pinkish cinnamon to vinaceous orange-brown, or when old grayish to violaceous brown; bald and glutinous but at times virgate [streaked] below slime; margin bald when young, (Smith(35))
FLESH  white to yellowish, usually greenish yellow near tubes and tinged grayish vinaceous under cap surface, not staining when exposed^, (Bessette), 2-5cm in center, firm, compact; white, tinged grayish vinaceous under cap surface and greenish yellow next to tubes, unchanging; in stem hard and compact, white with base dull vinaceous, unchanging, (Smith(34)), thick; white except yellow near tubes and vinaceous under cap surface; in stem white at first, "staining vinaceous to vinaceous-brown where injured, at times entirely vinaceous-brown", (Smith(35))
PORES  1-3mm long, radially arranged and elongated; whitish to tan at first, becoming yellow to ochraceous when mature, unchanging or staining brownish when bruised; tube layer 0.6-1cm thick, subdecurrent at first, becoming depressed when old^, (Bessette), medium to large, irregular, generally oval, elongated radially, arranged radially, glandular dotted; tube layer 0.5-0.6cm thick, arcuate, short-decurrent, radiately arranged and more or less separated by veins, warm buff, becoming yellow ocher, unchanging, (Smith(34)), 1-3(4)mm radially when old, boletinoid [elongated and radial in arrangement] from the first, cream when young, ocher-yellow when mature, often grayish when old, staining neither brown nor blue when injured; tube layer up to 1cm thick when mature, mostly 0.4-0.7cm thick, decurrent, pale yellow becoming ocher-yellow, not staining, (Smith(35))
STEM  3-8cm x 1-2.5cm, nearly equal or widening downward, "sometimes abruptly constricted at the base", solid; white, with yellow near top or base, sometimes yellow nearly overall, frequently stained brownish; dry, "with brownish vinaceous to dark brown glandular dots and smears, at least on the upper half; partial veil and annulus absent"^, (Bessette), 5-7cm x 1-3cm, equal or widening downward; white when young, remaining white in upper part or becoming straw-yellow, pinkish brown in lower part; very obscurely reticulated at the top, "with a subviscid pellicle which becomes minutely areolate on drying, glandular-dotted on the upper half or so", dots brownish vinaceous; bald on lower part, (Smith(34)), 6-10cm x 1-3cm, solid, usually narrowed at base; white at first becoming yellow where not stained vinaceous-brown; "glandular dotted or with masses of glandular cutis about 1cm wide present, glandulae pinkish to vinaceous-brown", (Smith(35))
ODOR  pungent or not distinctive^ (Bessette), none (Smith(34), Smith(35))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), none (Smith(34), Smith(35))
EDIBILITY  edible once gluten is removed (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on ground in conifer forests^, (Bessette), type collected under Douglas-fir, hemlock and fir, (Smith(34)), single in mixed conifers (Smith for single collection in Michigan)
SPORE DEPOSIT  ochraceous brown^ (Bessette), bright ochraceous brown (Smith(34))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 3-4 um, subelliptic to oblong, smooth, nearly colorless^, (Bessette), spores 7-10 x 3-3.5 um, mostly 8 x 3 um, elliptic, colorless, a few deep olivaceous; cystidia 40-80 x 6-9 um, cylindric to somewhat clavate, densely clustered, colorless to dark-colored, (Smith(34)), spores 7.5-9 x 3-3.2 um, oblong, smooth, inamyloid, colorless to slightly yellowish or some dark red-brown in KOH; basidia 4-spored; pleurocystidia 45-68 x 6-9 um, cylindric to narrowly clavate, clustered, "much vinaceous-red to vinaceous-brown amorphous pigment around the clusters in the hymenium" but many cystidia with colorless content at least in the projecting part; cap cuticle a collapsed trichodermium of hyphae 4-10 um wide and as revived in KOH with copious incrustation of fine granules, the layer gelatinous; caulocystidia similar to pleurocystidia or smaller; clamp connections absent, (Smith(35))
NAME ORIGIN  means "punctate-stemmed"^, (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Suillus granulatus has a pinkish cinnamon cap that lacks the darker brown tones and irregular to round pores that are not boletinoid (i.e. not radially arranged and elongated), (Bessette).
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Smith(34), Smith(36), Trudell(4)*, Snell(2), Both(1)
85
FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus quiescens  T.D. Bruns & E.C. Vellinga^  Mycologia 102(2): 442. 2010
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a glutinous cap that is light brown becoming darker, 2) a cap margin that is inrolled with a narrow sterile zone, 3) white flesh that is unchanging or slightly changing, 4) radially elongate yellow pores less than 1 mm wide, 5) a short stem that is yellow at the top and white to yellow lower down, the stem decorated with fine glandular dots, 6) fruiting in small groups with pines between November and March, and 7) microscopic characters including both 2-spored and 4-spored basidia in most collections.^ | The description is derived from Bruns(1). Colors in single quotation marks are from Kelly(4). RANGE ^Suillus quiescens was described from CA (Santa Cruz Island in Santa Barbara County, also found at Point Reyes National Seashore north of San Francisco). Fruitbodies have not yet been identified in Oregon or Washington, but molecular sequences closely match those recovered from mycorrhizae of pine seedlings in Oregon. There is a photograph of S. quiescens from WA by N. Siegel on iNaturalist (observation 99945706, not sequenced).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  6-12cm across, "broad, hemispheric to broadly convex"; approaching 'strong brown' or 'deep brown' when mature, "sometimes with olivaceous patches or tones", much paler when young but still some shade of 'light brown', or between 'light brown' and 'light orange', or grayish yellow; covered with a glutinous layer, the gluten "often drying in strands to give a slightly fibrillose look to older specimens"; margin inrolled when young "with a limited sterile zone of a millimeter or less"
FLESH  white, generally unchanging, sometimes with brownish stains just under the cap surface, and pale yellow just above tubes; in stem "white and unchanging, but sometimes with orangish stains in older or insect-damaged parts"
PORES  mature pores radially elongate but generally less than 1 mm wide; tubes when young near 'pale yellow' and "with light brown or yellowish brown glandular secretions", older tubes slightly lighter than 'brilliant orange yellow', becoming near 'olivaceous yellow'
STEM  typically 2-4cm long but sometimes longer (6-8cm), equal or slightly bulbous, or tapered to base; 'pale yellow' to 'light yellow' on the top fifth, the lower part the same color as top or white or overlaid with a 'light brown' layer or streaks of gluten as on the cap, occasionally this overlay giving the impression of an annular zone; surface finely glandular, glands at first only slightly darker than surface but sometimes light brown, becoming nearly black after drying
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  fruiting in small groups with pines, November to March^, (Bruns), fall, winter, spring
SPORE DEPOSIT  not determined but probably between 'strong yellow brown' and 'strong brown' as seen on cap surface
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.1-14.7 x 2.4-3.7 um in side view, "elongate, with a slight suprahilar depression in side view, and oblong in face view", most with a single large droplet, with inconspicuous hilar appendage^; basidia 2-4-spored, 20.2-26.2 x 5.2-6.7 um, clavate, without clamp connection, sterigmata very thick when young, 2-spored and 4-spored basidia present in most collections; pleurocystidia "similar to cheilocystidia, but size and frequency of clusters decreases distal to the tube mouth", cheilocystidia similar to those in many other granulatus-like Suillus species: 21-30 x 3.1-6.2 um, either in dense clusters (14-125 um wide) embedded in brown incrusting material, or single, primarily narrowly clavate, or cylindric and then often septate, "with yellow-brown inclusions that appear to be shrunken away from the wall, thin-walled", single cystidia colorless, "without incrustations, sometimes with mucronate or capitate apices"; no clear clamp connections seen
NAME ORIGIN  "refers to the species' ability to lay quiescent in the spore bank until it encounters pine roots", (Bruns)
SIMILAR  Fruitbodies are similar to Suillus brevipes but the cap of S. quiescens is paler, the stem apex is yellowish, and with maturity there are fine glandular dots at the top of the stem, (Bruns). Suillus glandulosipes also has an inrolled margin when young, but in contrast to that species Suillus quiescens has only a small sterile zone that one might need a hand lens to see, (Bruns). Suillus pungens of California has more prominent glandular dots on stem, has a white cap when immature, and has strong olivaceous tones before becoming similar in color to older S. quiescens, (Bruns).
SOURCES  Bruns(1), Kelly(4), Nguyen(3)
86
FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus subalpinus  M.M. Moser^  Mycotaxon 65: 397. 1997
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a patchy whitish, gray-brown, reddish brown, or vinaceous brown cap that develops orange-brown tones when old, 2) white flesh that is faintly yellowish when old, 3) small pores in a whitish pore surface that matures yellow, 4) a short, white stem that is covered with white to reddish then brownish glandular dots, 5) absent partial veil, 6) growth in subalpine habitat under Whitebark Pine, and 7) microscopic characters.^ |"Although originally reported to occur with Whitebark and Limber Pine (Pinus flexilis) in Wyoming, it appears that S. subalpinus is an obligate ectomycorrhizal symbiont of Whitebark Pine." (Siegel(3) (with Latin name italicized)). |The description is derived from Siegel(3) except where noted.| The Suillus flavogranulatus description (as opposed to the holotype whose sequence matched Suillus lakei) is very much like Suillus subalpinus, and the relationship between the two should be investigated. It should also be investigated whether some reports of Suillus granulatus are really Suillus subalpinus. (D. Miller, pers. comm.) RANGE ^Suillus alpinus was described from WY and also has been sequenced from WA, OR, MT and CA, (D. Miller, pers. comm.). C. Cripps reported it from Montana and British Columbia, (Siegel(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  3-7.5cm across, "convex with an incurved margin", becoming broadly convex to flat, "occasionally depressed at disc"; color variable, "whitish near margin, gray-brown, reddish brown to vinaceous brown towards disc, often variegated in a blotchy or spotty pattern, less often unicolor", becoming more orange-brown to warm brown when old; smooth, "glutinous when young", viscid to tacky when old; margin "lacking sterile tissue when young"^, (Siegel), disc when old flat or even slightly depressed, (Moser)
FLESH  moderately thick, firm when young, soft when old; white to faintly yellowish when old^, (Siegel), "white, in young specimens in base sometimes slightly bluish but not really changing color", when old "very faintly yellowish", (Moser)
PORES  pores "small, occasionally radially arranged", white, whitish to ivory when young, "developing creamy tones, and maturing yellow"; tubes 0.3-0.5cm long^, (Siegel), pores at first very fine, when old wider, 2-3 per mm, pores and tubes "at first white, whitish to ivory, later slowly turning more or less yellow", tubes rather short, 0.3-0.5cm long, (Moser)
STEM  2-5.5cm x 1-2.5cm at top, equal, or widening or narrowing downward, "extreme base pointed"; white when young "covered with white to reddish, then brownish glandular dots"; base color developing yellowish tones when old; partial veil absent^, (Siegel), 2-5.5cm x 1.0-2.5cm at top, base occasionally up to 4.0cm wide, "equal or base tapering or enlarged but the very base pointed"; "white, over the whole length with well developed at first white, later reddish glandular dots which turn brownish to porphyraceous" when old, apex when old slightly yellowish; no trace of veil, (Moser)
ODOR  indistinct
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or scattered in soil under Pinus albicaulis (Whitebark Pine), "summer or fall in high-elevation subalpine forest"^, (Siegel), growing under five-needle pines - Pinus albicaulis (Whitebark Pine), Pinus flexilis (Limber Pine), "in subalpine forests up to timber line", (Moser)
SPORE DEPOSIT  "olive to brownish olive"
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.5-7.7(9.2) x 2.5-3(3.8) um, "slender cylindric to subfusoid, smooth, thin-walled"^; basidia 4-spored, 23-32 x 6.5-8 um, clavate; hymenial cystidia 40-50(80) x 6-12 um (base 2-3 um wide), "clavate or occasionally slightly capitate, solitary or clustered", colorless or with brown contents, "walls incrusted"; cheilocystidia 20-90 x 5-9 um. "clavate, often in large clusters"; cap cuticle an ixocutis, hyphae 2-2.5 um wide, colorless, hyphae in lower layer up to 7 um wide, pale brown, (Siegel), spores 6.5-7.7(9.2) x 2.5-2.9(3.8) um, smooth, "pale olivaceous under microscope", thin-walled; basidia 4-spored 23-32 x 6.5-8 um, clavate; hymenial cystidia 40-50(80) x 6-12 um, "clavate, sometimes slightly capitate", "single or in large fascicles or cushions, colorless or with brown contents, walls encrusted and often pigment accumulations around the base"; cheilocystidia 20-90 x 5-9 um, clavate, "with brown contents, often in large clusters"; cap cuticle an ixocutis, hyphae on surface 2-2.5 um, "colorless, granulose encrusted, irregular to subrepent", in deeper layer up to 7 um wide "and walls pale brown, encrusted and more regularly arranged", (Moser)
NAME ORIGIN  named after subalpine habitat
SIMILAR  Suillus brunnescens is similar but has a veil (Moser). Suillus albidipes "can also have white to vinaceous brown colors, has similar spores and may also occur with 5-needle pines", but the glandular dots of the stem are lacking in young stages and only slightly developed when old in lower parts. Suillus pallidiceps "changes color from white to yellowish or cinnamon buff", has no glandular stem, and "grows with 2-needle pines", (Moser)
SOURCES  Siegel(3)*, Moser(12)
87
FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus suilloides  (Thiers) Kretzer & T.D. Bruns^  Mycologia 89(4): 589. 1997;  Gastrosuillus suilloides  (Thiers) Thiers  Mem. N. Y. bot. Gdn 49: 358. 1989;  Gastroboletus suilloides  Thiers  Brittonia 21: 248. 1969
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Also listed in the Truffles category. Features of Suillus suilloides include 1) a hypogeous to partly emergent fruitbody that is convex to nearly round, the surface yellowish brown to cinnamon brown or pinkish brown, dry, and bald becoming fibrillose-scaly or tomentose, 2) unchanging buff to cream context, 3) a spore mass with distorted greenish-yellow tubes, and small irregular pores of the same color, 4) an poorly developed stem-columella colored like the cap, 5) mild odor and taste, 6) growth under conifers at higher elevations, and 7) microscopic characters including fascicled darkly staining cystidia and spore shape that both suggest Suillus.^ | The current name in the online Species Fungorum, accessed April 20, 2020, was Suillus suilloides, with Gastrosuillus suilloides and Gastroboletus suilloides as synonyms. MycoBank, accessed the same day gave all three names as obligate or homotypic synonyms, without specifying a current name. The description is derived from Thiers(7). RANGE ^Suillus suilloides was described from CA but according to Noah Siegel (pers. comm.) has been recorded also from OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  2.5-5cm across when mature, "convex to subglobose to plano-convex during all stages of development"; peridium ' "ochraceous buff" to "cinnamon brown" to "buckthorn brown" to occasionally as dark as "mikado brown" to "sayal brown" ' when older, "unchanging when bruised"; dry, bald when young, "often becoming fibrillose to tomentose to appressed fibrillose scaly with age, sometimes becoming areolate with age"
FLESH  0.3-0.7cm thick, "ochraceous buff" to pale "cream color", "unchanging when exposed"
PORES  pores "small, irregular", greenish yellow to olive-yellow; gleba distinctly "tubulose", tubes typically exposed at maturity (but "arranged so as to make a spore deposit impossible"); colored like the pore surface, "unchanging when bruised or exposed"
STEM  stem-columella typically poorly developed, 0.5-1.0cm x 0.3-0.5cm, more or less colored as the cap during all stages of development; dry, tomentose to appressed-fibrillose
ODOR  not distinctive
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single to gregarious, "hypogeous to partially emergent", under conifers including Abies magnifica (California Red Fir) and Pinus contorta ssp. murrayana (Sierra Lodgepole Pine) in higher elevations of the Sierra Nevada of California, late summer
SPORE DEPOSIT  none
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.4-12 x 3.5-4.5 um, elliptic to inequilateral, some appearing oval or "occasionally more or less constricted at the midportion", "smooth, walls slightly thickened, sterigmal appendages oblique"^; basidia 4-spored, 20-37 x 6-8 um, "narrowly clavate to subcylindric, clavate to depressed at the center", colorless in KOH; brachybasidioles 15-23 x 4-8 um, clavate; cystidia "abundant to numerous", 35-55 x 5-11 um, "clavate to cylindric to subcylindric", "typically clustered and staining dark brown in KOH and Melzer's" as in Suillus; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means 'like Suillus'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Thiers(7) (colors in double quotation marks from Ridgway(1)) (as Gastroboletus)
88
FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Suillus tomentosus  Singer, Snell, and E.A. Dick^  Mycologia 51: 570. 1960
ENGLISH NAME(S)  tomentose Suillus, woolly pine bolete, woollycap, blue-staining slippery-jack, poor man's slippery-jack, woolly-capped Suillus, bluing slippery-jack
NOTES  Features of Suillus tomentosus include 1) a dry to viscid cap that has grayish brown to ocher, olive-yellow or reddish brown fibrils or scales on yellow to orange yellow background, 2) pallid to yellow flesh that turns blue, 3) brown then yellow pores that turn blue when bruised, 4) stem the same color as the cap or more orange, with glandular dots on stem that are usually browner, and no partial veil or annulus, and 5) growth under conifers, particularly 2-needle pines. The fruiting body tends to stain the fingers brown.^ | S. tomentosus is very common in the Pacific Northwest. RANGE ^It is found in WA, OR, ID, CA, CO, MI, WY, (Smith(34)), NB, NS, PQ, and NC, (Both). It is widely distributed from eastern Canada south to NC and SC, west to the Pacific Northwest, south to CA, (Bessette). It has been reported from BC (Bandoni), and MT (L. Evans, pers. comm.). Var. discolor occurs in ID.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap surface stains pale gray with application of FeSO4, (Bessette), flesh pale gray with FeSO4, (Thiers)
CAP  5-12cm, obtuse to convex, becoming broadly convex to flat when old and margin uplifted and wavy; yellow to orange-yellow ground color, fibrils and scales grayish, yellow-ocher, dark olive-yellow, or reddish brown; conspicuously fibrillose-scaly when young, becoming nearly bald when old; margin coarsely tomentose at first^, (Bessette), 5-10(15)cm, obtuse to convex, becoming broadly convex; ground color pale yellow to pale orange-yellow, with tomentum and scales that vary 'from pallid (young buttons) to pale buff, pinkish buff, grayish (when wet), orange-cinnamon to tawny, russet, or "Bordeaux red" (dark red)' (Ridgway colors); viscid beneath tomentum and scales, at first tomentose-floccose to squamulose but gradually becoming bald; margin rather coarsely tomentose at first but gradually becoming bald, (Smith), 5-15(20)cm, "background usually pale yellow to yellow-orange, orange-buff, or dark dull orange", "fibrils or scales usually grayish brown to brown (reddish in one form), sometimes pallid or buff when young"; dry, but in wet weather or when old often viscid, at first covered with a dense coating of fibrils that break up to form scattered scales and are often sparse or even absent when old, (Arora), velvety to soft-scaly; margin extends beyond tubes (McKnight), "typically has a covering of grayish, yellowish, reddish brown, or bright red fibrils or fibrillose scales over an orange-yellow to yellowish background", (Trudell)
FLESH  "yellow, staining blue irregularly and often slowly when exposed"^, (Bessette), 0.8-1.5cm; pallid to yellow but paler than cap surface, "changing slowly to blue or greenish blue when cut or bruised"; in stem yellow but paler than cap surface, staining greenish blue slightly when cut, (Smith)
PORES  up to 1mm, angular; "brown to vinaceous brown or dingy cinnamon", becoming dingy yellow to olive-yellow when old, turning blue when bruised; "sometimes beaded with yellowish droplets at first"; tube layer 0.8-1.5cm thick, adnate to subdecurrent^, (Bessette), about 2 per mm, bister to dark dingy cinnamon or vinaceous brown when young, slowly becoming dingy yellow, staining bluish when injured at least when old; tube layer 1-2cm thick, adnate to decurrent, rarely depressed around stem, "pale dingy yellow becoming dingy olive-yellow and staining dingy greenish blue when injured", (Smith), usually turning blue at least slightly when bruised, at least when old, (Arora)
STEM  5-12cm x 1-2.5cm, widening downward or nearly equal, solid; yellow to dull orange-yellow; with darker orange to brownish glandular dots, dry to moist; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), (3)5-10(12)cm x 1-2(3)cm at top, equal or clavate, solid; colored as cap or more orange, when tomentum is reddish on cap, the base of the stem may be reddish also; "glandular-dotted to glandular-punctate over all"; no veil remnants; mycelium around base cream to cream-buff at first, soon becoming vinaceous-buff or redder, (Smith), 3-11(15)cm x 1-3cm, dots may be the same color as stem or browner and are often obvious when old; base sometimes reddish-stained, (Arora)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), none (Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), slightly acid to mild (Smith)
EDIBILITY  edible (Bessette)
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under conifers^, (Bessette), scattered to gregarious under 2-needle pines, (Smith), summer and fall (Miller), primarily under Lodgepole and Shore Pines (Trudell)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown ^ (Bessette), dark olive brown, very slowly changing to dull cinnamon, (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-12 x 3-5 um, fusoid to elongate-oval, smooth, yellowish^, (Bessette), spores 7-10(12) x 3-4(5) um, obscurely fusoid to elongate-ovoid, smooth, inamyloid, pale yellowish in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 26-34 x 5-8 um, clavate, yellowish in KOH; pleurocystidia "30-46 X 7-10 um, subcylindric to narrowly clavate or narrowed to a blunt apex, often with incrusting debris", content colorless to dingy yellow-brown, "typically in bunches and at level of hymenium the cluster surrounded by brown incrusting material, as revived in KOH", when fresh colorless to pale vinaceous brown in KOH, cheilocystidia "similar to pleurocystidia, very numerous and tube dissepiments often with copious brown amorphous pigment"; cap epicutis "a layer of gelatinous interwoven hyphae in the form of an interrupted trichodermium the elements of which become fused to form the squamules", these hyphae 8-12 um wide, with end-cells somewhat cystidioid and 10-16 um at widest part; caulocystidia abundant, similar to cheilocystidia; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "covered with hair"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Suillus fuscotomentosus does not stain blue, its cap color is darker, and its spores are larger, (Smith). |Suillus discolor (=Suillus tomentosus var. discolor) grows on the ground under white pine and has a dense appressed layer of grayish fibrils on the cap when young, a bald, glutinous to viscid, brownish to olive-brown cap that becomes dull cinnamon brown at maturity, dingy ochraceous flesh "that typically stains blue when exposed and slowly stains green with the application of FeSO4", "dingy ochraceous pore surface that slowly and slightly stains greenish blue, then brownish when bruised", stem "with coarse, ochraceous to orange-brown glandular dots that soon become dark brown to grayish brown from handling", yellow-brown spore deposit, and spores 9-13 x 4-4.5 um, (Bessette). Both(1) says var. discolor differs in its association with white pine, different color of cap, pores less brown, and different color of spore deposit. 
SOURCES  Smith(34), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Thiers(1), Lincoff(2)*, Miller(14)*, Ammirati(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Sept(1)*, Bandoni(1), Both(1), AroraPocket(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*, McAdoo(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*
89
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus amylosporus  (A.H. Sm.) J.L. Frank & N. Siegel^  Fungal Systematics and Evolution 6: 267. 2020;  == Boletus amylosporus  (A.H. Sm.) Wolfe  Nova Hedwigia 43: 518. 1986;  == Porphyrellus amylosporus  A.H. Sm.  Mycopath. et Mycol. Appl. 26: 397. 1965;  == Tylopilus amylosporus  (A.H. Sm.) A.H. Sm.;  = Gastroboletus xerocomoides  Trappe & Thiers  Brittonia 21: 247. 1969
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry, velvety cap that is dark olive-brown to grayish brown or vinaceous brown with whitish to yellow or pinkish flesh showing in the cracks, 2) flesh that may turn blue erratically and shows red in stem and around larval tunnels, 3) yellow pores that sometimes have reddish tints and that turn blue when bruised, 4) stem that is reddish or with red striations over a yellow base, some becoming brownish, olive-gray, reddish, or reddish brown, often with a red band at the top, 5) growth under conifers, alder or oak, and 6) microscopic characters that include weakly to distinctly amyloid spores, most with a truncate apex.^ |Siegel(2) say (in the California context), "There are three species that have been lumped under the incorrectly applied name X. chrysenteron, with X. diffractus being the most common and widespread of the bunch. X. amylosporus typically has a darker cap with more scattered and irregular cracks, the pores bruise dark inky blue, the stipe develops dingy brownish tones, and the spores are more reddish brown in color." X. salicicola is similar but much rarer, and "can be told apart by its more-often cracked cap and growth with willow", (Siegel(2) with Latin names italicized). |X. amylosporus is uncommon, but rather widespread, (Frank(9)). |Collections in dry habitats tend to form gastroid fruiting bodies, "and many Sierra Nevada collections are deformed", (Frank(9)). |The ITS sequence of the type of Gastroboletus xerocomoides is identical to that of the type of Xerocomellus amylosporus. Note that while about half of the spores of Gastroboletus xerocomoides were truncate as in X. amylosporus, the spores were described as inamyloid and wider, measuring 12.8-18(21) x 6.4-8 um. RANGE ^The known distribution is Pacific Northwest, north to at least Vancouver Island BC, south into CA, east to the Northern Rocky Mountains; collections were examined from WA, OR, ID (including holotype), and CA (including type of Gastroboletus xerocomoides), (Frank(9)). Xerocomellus amylosporus is found in ID (Smith), the northern Rocky Mountains and CA (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap flesh no reaction to KOH or FeSO4 (Smith)
CAP  4-10cm across, bun-shaped becoming convex to flat; "dark olive brown to grayish brown, to vinaceous brown", "in cracks dull whitish to yellow, becoming pinkish in age"; "dry, finely velvety to matted-tomentose, lacking cracks when young, becoming areolate around the margin, to extensively areolate in age", (Frank(9)), 4-12cm, convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old; dark olive-brown to grayish brown, yellow to dull whitish flesh showing in cracks; dry, velvety-subtomentose, become cracked and scurfy when old, (Bessette), 4-12cm, convex becoming broadly convex; dark olive-fuscous becoming olive-brown to olive-buff; dry, velvety, when old areolate [cracked like dried mud] or merely cracked, (Smith)
FLESH  in cap "firm to soft, light yellow, erratically bruising blue", (Frank(9)), yellow to dull whitish, turning blue erratically or sometimes not changing when exposed, (Bessette), pale yellow except red just under cap surface, staining blue when cut and slowly becoming red around larval tunnels; in stem red, (Smith)
PORES  1-2 per mm, "slightly angular or irregular", "yellow to golden yellow at first, becoming olive yellow, bruising inky blue to dark blue somewhat quickly"; tube layer slightly sunken around stem, (Frank(9)), 1-2 per mm, angular; "yellow, becoming golden yellow to dull yellow or olive-yellow", sometimes with reddish tints, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 0.8-2cm thick, depressed near stem when old, (Bessette), large and irregular in outline when mature, greenish yellow, readily staining blue; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, ventricose, dull yellow to greenish yellow, turning blue where cut, (Smith), pores bruise dark inky blue (Siegel)
STEM  4-10cm x 1-2cm, cylindric to club-shaped, "reddish or with red longitudinal striations over a yellowish base when young, soon becoming brownish to reddish brown over much of the stipe to extensively brown with a red band at apex", (Frank(9)), 4.5-10cm x 1-2cm, nearly equal, solid; "with a pale tan to olive-buff or whitish ground color that is typically punctate with reddish dots and points"; dry, not reticulate; yellow basal mycelium, (Bessette), 4-9cm x 1-1.5(2)cm, equal; reddish in a few places but usually entirely olive gray to the pallid apex; faintly pruinose and longitudinally striate at apex, (Smith), develops dingy brown tones (Siegel)
ODOR  indistinct, (Frank(9)), none (Smith)
TASTE  mild, (Frank(9)), mild (Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single or scattered in troops under conifers, especially Picea sitchensis (Sitka Spruce); type collection was under Alnus rubra (Red Alder) but Alnus has not been present at all sites, (Frank(9)), single or scattered on ground under hardwoods or in mixed woods, frequently associated with oak or red alder, (Bessette), type gregarious under Alnus rubra in Idaho, (Smith)
SPORE DEPOSIT  "olive brown to dark reddish olive" (Frank(9)), dark reddish brown (Bessette), dark "wood brown" on white paper, (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (11.4)13-16.2(18.1) x 5.2-6.5(7.1) um, "fusoid to subcylindrical, inequilateral, most with truncate apex, smooth, weakly to distinctly amyloid", amyloid reaction of the spores "may fade over time or may be weak in some collections"^; basidia (2)4-spored, 27.3-35.7 x 9.9-12.9 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia 28.9-73.5 x 2.9-8.1 um, "narrowly ventricose with elongated apices"; cap cuticle "a trichoderm made up of elongated cells" 8-10 um wide, "with brown incrustations and pigment"; terminal cells conic; clamp connections absent, (Frank(9)), spores 13-18 x 4-7 um, fusoid to subcylindric [spindle-shaped to somewhat cylindric], truncate, smooth, weakly to distinctly amyloid, ochraceous, (Bessette), spores 12-17 x 4.4-6 um, "boletoid" in shape, smooth, "with a circular apical thickening depressed in the center and from here a discontinuity in the wall extends to the interior" (as in Boletus truncatus), on spores crushed out from the dried fruiting body weakly but distinctly amyloid (dull violaceous) and more violaceous in pore region than elsewhere, "spores from a deposit on clean white paper dextrinoid in outer half or one third (apical regions) and merely yellowish toward the apiculus"; basidia 4-spored, 32-38 x 8-10 um, clavate, inamyloid; pleurocystidia scattered, 40-60 x 8-12 um, narrowly fusoid to slightly ventricose to a subacute apex, not incrusted, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium of hyphae 8-15 um wide, with plate-like incrustations of pale bister (in KOH) pigment along the walls, end-cell somewhat cystidioid; clamp connections rare, seen at base of occasional basidium in some fruitbodies, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with amyloid spores'
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus diffractus has reddish flesh showing in cracks, olive-brown spore deposit, and spores that are inamyloid and not truncate, (Bessette). X. diffractus does not stain blue as quickly (N. Siegel, pers. comm.). Note that although the original description does not note pink color in the cracks, there actually can be pink in the cracks (D. Miller, pers. comm.)
SOURCES  Frank(9)*, Bessette(3) (as Tylopilus amylosporus), Smith(31) (as Porphyrellus amylosporus), Siegel(2), Thiers(7) (description of Gastroboletus xerocomoides)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus atropurpureus  J.L. Frank, N. Siegel & C.F. Schwarz^  Fungal Systematics and Evolution 6: 276. 2020
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a cap that is dark blackish purple to dark wine red or olivaceous black, and lacks a prominent paler margin, 2) a cap surface that is usually bare or with a slight bloom, often bumpy-wrinkled, 3) yellow flesh that is unchanging or turns blue slightly when older, 4) a dull yellow to greenish yellow pore surface that can be blushed reddish, only rarely bruising blue, the pores moderately small, round to slightly angular, 5) a stem with a yellowish background that is red-punctate or extensively red, often with slightly darker streaks, and 6) growth under conifers and under hardwoods.^ |Gastroid morphological variation occurs in high elevation arid habitats in the southern Cascade Range and the Sierra Nevada (Frank(9)). The description is derived from Siegel(2). RANGE ^Xerocomellus atropurpureus ranges from BC south at least to Monterey County, CA. Collections were examined from WA, OR (including holotype), and CA, (Frank(9)). X. atropurpureus is more common in CA than Xerocomus zelleri (Siegel).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  4-10(12)cm across, "rounded, bun shaped to broadly convex at first, becoming flat occasionally slightly wavy in age"; dark blackish purple, "blackish, deep reddish purple to dark wine red", more rarely "with extensive olivaceous-black tones"; "dry to moist, usually bare or with a faint pale bloom, but not truly velvety, often extensively bumpy-wrinkled, occasionally smoother"^, (Siegel), (3)5-10(14)cm across, "rounded, bun-shaped to broadly convex at first", becoming flat, occasionally slightly wavy when old; "dark blackish purple, deep reddish purple to dark wine-red or reddish brown, sometimes paler red, rarely with extensive olivaceous tones", "dry to moist", usually bald "or with a faint pale bloom, sometimes finely pruinose, rugulose to extensively bumpy-wrinkled, occasionally smoother", irregular cracking can occur when old or under dry conditions, (Frank)
FLESH  firm, fleshy; "marbled yellow, unchanging or bluing slightly in older specimens"^, (Siegel), in cap firm, "pale yellow to creamy white, typically not staining, sometimes erratically staining bluish"; in stem "fibrous, light yellow to creamy whitish, occasionally bluing, more often in base", (Frank)
PORES  moderately small, "round to slightly angular"; pale dull yellow to dingy greenish yellow when old, "occasionally reddish blushed, or rarely extensively red", rarely bruising blue, "and only in older and/or waterlogged specimens"; tubes 0.5-1.5cm long, sunken around stem^, (Siegel), 2-4 per mm, circular when young to slightly angular and wider when mature, pale dull yellow to dingy greenish-yellow when old, "occasionally bruising blue, occasionally reddish-blushed, or rarely extensively reddish"; tube layer pale yellow to yellow, tubes usually colored as pores, (Frank)
STEM  4-9cm x 0.8-3.0cm, "equal or club shaped with an enlarged base"; "densely covered with fine red punctations with a yellowish background, soon extensively red, often with slightly darker streaks"^, (Siegel), 4-12cm x 0.8-3cm, "equal or with enlarged base", "extensively red over a yellowish background, often with red punctations aggregated into blurry chevrons and streaks, rarely staining blue", "base often white", (Frank)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Siegel, Frank)
TASTE  mild or lemony^ (Siegel, Frank)
EDIBILITY  edible and good (Siegel)
HABITAT  single "or scattered in troops", "on well-decayed, moss-covered logs and stumps or duff", both with hardwoods and with conifers, in California mostly fruiting in fall and winter, with scattered spring fruitings^, (Siegel), single or scattered in small groups, sometimes cespitose, in mixed forests, typically with conifers, sometimes with oak and other hardwoods, "occasionally emerging from well-rotted wood", (Frank)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dull olive brown (Siegel, Frank)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-15 x 4-5.5 um, elongate to elliptic, smooth^, (Siegel), spores (11)12.2-15.9(16.6) x (4.6)4.8-5.8(6.2) um, subfusoid to subcylindric, inequilateral, "smooth, inamyloid"; basidia 4-spored, 19.6-29.9 x 7.4-11.8(13) um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia rare, 40-60 x 8-12 um, ventricose, colorless; cap cuticle "a trichoderm with cylindrical to elongated cells with brown walls", "some terminal cells clavate, slightly wider than underlying cells, some narrow with dark incrustations", clamp connections absent, (Frank)
NAME ORIGIN  atro- (from ater, Latin for black or dark), -purpureus (from purpureus, Latin for purple), referring to the color of the cap, (Frank)
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus zelleri has these provisional differentiating morphological features: 1) X. atropurpureus "more often has a reddish purple to wine-purple cap (usually lacking a distinct pale margin)", whereas X. zelleri caps "tend to be darker" - "blackish purple to olivaceous black with a distinctly paler margin", 2) the young caps of X. zelleri "are often slightly velvety" (as opposed to glabrous on X. atropurpureus.), (Siegel(2)). X. zelleri 1) is smaller and more slender, 2) has a dark vinaceous black, dark olivaceous black or brownish black or olive brown to gray cap (as opposed to the more often reddish purple to wine purple cap of X. atropurpureus), 3) has a slightly velvety cap surface (rather than bald to finely pruinose), 4) almost always shows a narrow, pale band around the cap margin (more variable in X. atropurpureus), 5) is less likely to show blue staining (although X. atropurpureus is variable in its staining reactions), and 6) has a cap cuticle with a trichoderm "of subglobose to ellipsoid cells, with narrow awl-shaped terminal cells" (as opposed to a trichoderm of cylindric cells with the terminal cells of the same width or slightly wider than underlying cells), (Frank(9)). |Xerocomellus mendocinensis, when dark, has been mistaken for X. atropurpureus or X. zelleri, but it stains rapidly blue, shows more distinct punctuations on the stem, and is typically associated with hardwoods, (Frank(9)).
SOURCES  Siegel(2)*, Frank(9)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus diffractus  N. Siegel, C.F. Schwarz, & J.L. Frank  J.L. Frank, N. Siegel & C.F. Schwarz  Fungal Systematics and Evolution 6: 278. 2020^;  Boletus chrysenteron  (Bull.) Fr. (misapplied name);  Xerocomus chrysenteron  (Bull.) Quel.  (misapplied name);  Xerocomellus chrysenteron  (Bull.) Sutara  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  red-cracked bolete, red-cracking bolete, cracked-cap bolete, yellow-fleshed Boletus
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark to light brown to olive or olive gray cap that is tomentose to velvety and when mature has whitish to pale yellow to reddish cracks, 2) whitish to yellow flesh that turns blue slowly to moderately or erratically, 3) yellow to greenish pores that become large and irregular and typically stain blue slowly to moderately where bruised, 4) a yellowish to golden stem streaked with red punctations especially on lower stem, 5) fruiting under both conifers and hardwoods, and 6) non-truncate spores.^ | Xerocomellus diffractus is fairly common in the Pacific Northwest. It has been identified in the past as Boletus chrysenteron which is a European species. A sister species to Xerocomellus diffractus is indicated by molecular data from a Xerocomellus chrysenteron-like taxon in eastern North America but is not yet named (Frank(9)). RANGE ^Distribution is from central CA through the Pacific Northwest into BC, east to the Rocky Mountains of WY, and south into AZ; collections were examined from WA, OR, AZ, CA, and WY (Frank(9)). A. Parker, pers. comm., identified what is likely to be this species from ID (under the misapplied name Boletus chrysenteron before Xerocomellus diffractus was published).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  3-10(14)cm across, "rounded, bun-shaped to broadly convex at first, becoming flat", occasionally slightly wavy when old; "olive, olive gray to leather brown or tan", becoming paler when old, "more rarely reddish to pinkish brown"; in cracks whitish to pale yellow, becoming pinkish when old; "dry, finely velvety, becoming cracked"^, (Frank), 6-8cm, convex to broadly convex to flat, margin downcurved; dark brown or dark gray, pallid in cracks but usually with reddish tints in cracks toward margin when older; dry, dull, velvety to tomentose, usually shallowly and conspicuously areolate [cracked like dried mud] when old, (Thiers), 3-11cm, dark olive-brown to dark grayish olive, grayish brown, or brown, often tan to olive buff when old, (Arora), grayish brown to blackish brown (Trudell)
FLESH  "firm when young, becoming soft"; "whitish to pale yellow in pileus, yellow in stipe, bluing slowly to moderately or erratically"^, (Frank), 1-1.5cm thick, yellow, unchanging or becoming blue in irregular areas when exposed; in stem pallid to yellowish at top, "typically changing to reddish toward base, unchanging where exposed", (Thiers)
PORES  1-3(4) per mm, "small and round at first, enlarging and becoming slightly angular", pale yellow to dingy greenish when old, "staining blue to bluish gray, slowly to moderately when bruised"; tube layer sunken around stem, 0.5-1.5cm long^, (Frank), 1-1.5 mm, typically irregular, yellow, typically turning blue where bruised; tubes arched decurrent to depressed, similar in color, (Thiers)
STEM  4-10cm x 0.8-2cm, "equal or with an enlarged base", "yellow to golden, streaked with fine red punctations with red pigment more concentrated toward base, often sparse or absent entirely towards apex", when old "becoming more red upward"^, (Frank), 7-10cm x 1-1.7cm, equal or narrowly slightly downward, solid; usually yellow at the top, becoming reddish toward base, "sometimes pallid toward base and reddish only in midportion, or entirely pallid with only the striations or ridges colored red"; dry, bald, often longitudinally ridged or striate, mycelium at base whitish to yellow, (Thiers), 4-13cm x 0.5-1.5cm, (Arora)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Frank)
TASTE  mild^ (Frank)
EDIBILITY  yes but not choice and bear in mind the risk of confusing with untested species, (Thiers)
HABITAT  single "or scattered in troops", "under both conifers and hardwoods", "fall and early winter, or occasionally in spring on the California and Oregon coast, and summer in the Southwest, and at higher elevations"^, (Frank), single to gregarious under hardwoods and conifers, (Thiers)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dull olive brown^ (Frank), olive-brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (12.5)12.9-16.2 x (4.7)5.1-6.2 um, subfusoid to subcylindric, inequilateral to cylindric in side view, inamyloid^; basidia 4-spored, 18.5-27.2 x 7.5-11.5 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia infrequent, 38.9-49.5 x 11.6-11.9 um, "ventricose to subclavate"; cap cuticle a trichoderm of cylindric to elongated cells, terminal cells 5-7 um wide, "often narrower than those lower down", "pigment brown, parietal and incrusting, but light to absent in many terminal cells"; clamp connections absent, (Frank), spores 12-13.5 x 5-6 um, elliptic to subventricose [wider in middle] to subcylindric [almost cylindric], smooth, not truncate, ochraceous in KOH and Melzer's; basidia 4-spored, 33-36 x 7-9 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, common only on pores, 56-75 x 10-13 um, fusoid to fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium with slightly differentiated hyphal tips, hyphae +/- 10 um wide, often heavily and spirally incrusted; stem cuticle interwoven, incrusted; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  from diffringere in Latin, meaning "to break", "referring to the extensively cracking pileus surface"
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus amylosporus has a darker cap "with more scattered and irregular cracks", a stem that develops brownish tones, "pores that bruise darker (inky) blue, a spore deposit that is more reddish brown in color, and larger, distinctly truncate spores", (Frank). X. amylosporus stains blue more quickly than X. diffractus, (N. Siegel, pers. comm.) |Xerocomellus rainisiae can be distinguished by its thick velvety cap cuticle, "the deep greenish black to bluish black staining" on the lower stem, and "the lack of extensive red pigmentation or red punctations" on the stem, (Frank). |Xerocomellus salicicola has a light brown to reddish cap "developing extensive cracking", an obscurely reticulate stem that is extensively red or retains a red band near the top, and grows with willow and birch, (Frank). |Xerocomellus mendocinensis "has quickly blue-staining pores" and a coarsely punctate stem "that is often extensively red or with a distinct red belt near the apex, and smaller truncate spores", (Frank). |Xerocomellus dryophilus has a more rosy red to pinkish brown cap, "stains blue more quickly and deeply", and grows with Quercus, but in dry conditions, fruitbodies can be hard to distinguish from X. diffractus, (Frank). |Xerocomus subtomentosus group is usually more olive to olive-brown on cap with no red in cracks, (Thiers). |Boletus 'spadiceus' [here treated as a synonym of Boletus 'ferrugineus'] characteristically shows blue to blue-green discoloration when ammonium hydroxide is applied to the cap, (Thiers). |See also SIMILAR section of Hortiboletus rubellus.
SOURCES  Frank(9)*, Thiers(1) (as Boletus chrysenteron), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Smith(35) (as Boletus chrysenteron), Arora(1)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Lincoff(2)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Ammirati(1)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Miller(14)* (as Boletus chrysenteron), Redhead(5) (as Boletus chrysenteron), Both(1) (as Boletus chrysenteron), Desjardin(6)* (as Xerocomellus chrysenteron), Siegel(2)*, Marrone(1)*, MacKinnon(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus dryophilus  (Thiers) N. Siegel, C.F. Schwarz & J.L. Frank^  Index Fungorum 179: 1. 2014;  Boletus dryophilus  Thiers  California Mushrooms (New York): 82. 1975
ENGLISH NAME(S)  red cracked-cap bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a velvety cap that is ruby red, vinaceous red or brick red when fresh, becoming more pinkish brown, the cap surface usually becoming extensively cracked revealing whitish to pinkish flesh, 2) yellow flesh that becomes blue when cut, 3) moderately large, angular, yellow pores that readily bruise blue, 4) a club-shaped stem that is yellow at the top and red to wine red on its lower part, the red pigment sometimes appearing punctate, and 5) growth under live oak.^ RANGE ^Distribution includes CA (Siegel(2)) and it has been found in the Columbia River gorge area (N. Siegel, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  3-11cm across, "rounded, bun shaped to broadly convex at first", to nearly flat when old; ruby red to vinaceous red or brick red when fresh, "becoming dull tan-brown with pinkish or reddish tinges in dry weather", forms can occur that are dark brown to olive-brown; "dry, velvety-tomentose, and usually extensively cracked into tomentose plaques or scales", flesh in cracks "pallid yellowish white at first, often becoming pinkish tinged"^, (Siegel), 3-8(11)cm across, "hemispheric or bun-shaped to broadly convex" and then flat when old; "rosy red to vinaceous red, rosy red or brick-red when fresh, becoming dull tan-brown with pinkish or reddish tones in dry weather, occasionally with olive tones to extensively olive", in cracks showing "pallid yellowish white, often with some pink tones"; "dry, velvety-floccose and usually extensively cracked into plaques or scales", (Frank), 4-10cm across, convex to cushion-shaped when young, unchanging or becoming broadly convex to flat "to highly irregular and undulating" when old, margin incurved becoming decurved [downcurved]; when young near reddish brown to red, sometimes overlain by an olive brown tomentum, with age, in some the red color darkens to near dark red, in others the red fades and the olive brown predominates, often the combination of a red background and pallid tomentum gives the cap a pinkish brown appearance; "dry to slightly moist, conspicuously tomentose when young, unchanging or appearing subglabrous with age, sometimes becoming rimose to split and often developing into large cracks or conspicuous fissures", margin entire becoming eroded, (Thiers)
FLESH  firm, fleshy; "yellow, bruising blue rather quickly when cut"^, (Siegel), "pale to bright yellow, bruising blue rapidly", (Frank), 1-2cm thick, yellow with a slightly reddening under the cap surface, "slowly and erratically becoming blue when exposed", in stem colored as the surface, "becoming blue when exposed except at the base", (Thiers)
PORES  moderately large, "angular or irregularly shaped"; "pale yellow to dingy yellow or lemon yellow, bruising blue readily"; tubes sunken around stem^, (Siegel), "large, slightly angular or irregularly shaped, pale yellow to dingy yellow or lemon-yellow, bruising blue quickly when damaged", (Frank), 0.5-1 mm broad, angular, uneven, near olive yellow, bluing when bruised; tubes up to 1cm long, "broadly and deeply to shallowly depressed, sometimes appearing decurrent when very old", near olive yellow, bluing when exposed, (Thiers) 
STEM  3-10cm x 1-2cm thick, cylindric-straight or slightly curved, "sometimes slightly sinuous", "enlarged or club shaped at base, often tapered and sometimes slightly rooting at extreme base"; lemon yellow or dull yellow, "downward becoming orange-red and then red to wine red near base", reddish pigment "sometimes appearing very finely punctate", "basal tomentum yellowish"^, (Siegel), 3-12cm x 1-4cm, cylindric, "straight or slightly curved (sometimes slightly sinuous), enlarged or clavate at base", often narrowing downward "and sometimes slightly rooting at extreme base"; "lemon yellow or dull yellow over upper half, red to wine red near base", "reddish pigment sometimes appearing finely punctate over surface", "basal tomentum yellowish", (Frank), 5-8cm x 1-2cm at top, solid, equal or enlarging slightly toward the top, "often pinched and narrowed at the base"; yellow at the top, changing toward the base to reddish, the red colors particularly evident when old, darkening when bruised; dry to moist, glabrous (bald), (Thiers)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Siegel, Frank), not distinctive (Thiers)
TASTE  mild^ (Siegel, Frank), slightly acid near the cap cuticle, otherwise mild, (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  edible, consumed where better boletes unavailable, but mature specimens soft, and even young ones commonly parasitized by Hypomyces, (Siegel)
HABITAT  single or scattered, "often in drier forests under live oak", late fall into spring^, (Siegel), single to gregarious "in humus in open oak woods", in California associated with coastal live oak, (Thiers)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dull olive-brown^, (Siegel, Frank), brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11.5-16 x 5-6.5 um, elongate to elliptic^, (Siegel), spores (11.1)12-15.7(16.1) x (5.3)5.8-6.9(7.9) um, subelliptic to subfusoid, "smooth, inamyloid"; hymenial cystidia of two types: a) 23-29.8 x 6.5-8.6 um, "scattered to abundant, often fasciculate, narrowly clavate", and b) 30.2-42.9 x 10.1-12.6 um, "scattered, clavate"; cap cuticle "a trichoderm made up of elongated cells" 8-10 um wide, "terminal cells not differentiated", "pigment brown, coarsely encrusting in lower elements, finely encrusting in terminal cells"; clamp connections absent, (Frank), spores 12-16.5 x 5.5-8 um, subelliptic to subfusoid, not truncate, smooth, moderately thick-walled, brown in KOH, dark cinnamon brown in Melzer's reagent; basidia 4-spored, 20-26 x 7-9 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia 25-40 x 7-12, "scattered, inconspicuous, and sometimes apparently absent", clavate, embedded in the hymenium, colorless; cap cuticle "differentiated as a tangled trichodermium of heavily incrusted hyphae that stain pale ochraceous in KOH, the incrustations frequently spirally arranged"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'tree-loving' or 'oak-loving', in this case probably the latter
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus diffractus "has an olive-gray cap, and a slow bluing reaction on the pores", (Siegel(2)). "Xerocomellus" mendocinensis comb. prov. "has a much darker cap and a more evenly red stipe", (Siegel(2)).

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus mendocinensis  (Thiers) N. Siegel, C.F. Schwartz, & J.L. Frank^  Fungal Systematics and Evolution 6: 282. 2020;  Boletus truncatus  (Singer, Snell & E.A. Dick) Pouzar (misapplied name);  Xerocomus truncatus  Singer, Snell & E.A. Dick  (misapplied name);  Xerocomellus truncatus  (Singer, Snell & E.A. Dick) Klofac  (misapplied name);  Boletus mendocinensis Thiers, California Mushrooms (New York): 84. 1975
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry, finely velvety cap that is dark olive-brown when young lightening to olive-tan to medium brown, the cap surface cracking in its outer part to reveal flesh that is usually not pinkish, 2) yellowish flesh (reddish to tan near stem base), that bruises blue slowly to quickly, 3) a pore surface that is yellow (sometimes orange-red) when young, quickly bluing where bruised, the pores irregularly round to angular, 4) an equal stem that is yellow when young but soon covered with dense reddish dots and often washed red almost to the top, often with a contrasting red girdle just below the yellow at the top, 5) growth with conifers or hardwoods, and 6) elongate spores, many with a strongly truncate (squared-off) end.^ |'Xerocomellus' mendocinensis has been known by the misapplied name Boletus truncatus (an eastern North American species). It is almost the same as Xerocomellus chrysenteron but with truncate spores. The ITS DNA sequence of the type of Boletus mendocinensis Thiers "is identical to the bulk of western North American collections" labeled as 'truncatus', (Frank). Anomalously, the type description of Boletus mendocinensis describes long spores that are not truncate: "15-21(27) X 4-5(6) um, narrowly cylindric in face view, slightly inequilateral in profile, giant spores and pleomorphism rather common"..."The absence of any amyloid reaction or truncate apex in the spores readily distinguishes it from B. truncatus and related species" (Thiers(1) using a mu character rather than u in the unit of measurement). |Thiers(1) distinguishes Boletus truncatus (described separately from Boletus mendocinensis) from Boletus chrysenteron primarily on the character that at least a majority of the spores are truncate, but also says that Boletus truncatus does not usually show the characteristic red or reddish pigment in the cracks that Boletus chrysenteron shows, (Thiers(1)). |Siegel(2) say "Reliable identification of the cracked-cap boletes in our area [Redwood Coast of California] is difficult and is more reliably done after experience with all of the species involved and the range variation shown by each one. In comparison to the other Xerocomellus in our area, the relatively dark, moderately cracked cap, quick blue staining, and extensively red, punctate stipe are good clues." |The description is derived from Siegel(2) except where otherwise noted. RANGE ^Xerocomellus mendocinensis is known from CA, OR, and WA, "likely also occurring in northwestern Mexico, and expected into southern British Columbia". It is thought to be the correct name for what has been called Boletus truncatus on the west coast of North America. Boletus truncatus has also been reported in Idaho (Both), and occurs in eastern North America (Siegel).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH blackish on cap, orange on flesh
CAP  3-10cm across, "domed or bun shaped when young, expanding to broadly convex, eventually nearly flat, occasionally wavy"; usually "dark olive-brown when young (to almost blackish in one uncommon variant), soon olive-tan to medium brown, sometimes lighter gray in age"; "dry, finely velvety, cracking near the margin, often only cracking on outer quarter of cap, but occasionally extensively cracked across entire cap", flesh in cracks "dull tan-brown or light olive to pallid creamy (rarely pinkish)"^, (Siegel), 3-10cm across, hemispheric to bun-shaped becoming broadly convex, nearly flat, "sometimes wavy"; "most often dark olive brown when young, sometimes cool gray or nearly blackish, soon olive tan or medium brown", sometimes medium gray when old, color in cracks "dull tan brown to strongly pinkish or dull red"; dry, "finely velvety", usually cracking over outer quarter of cap radius, "sometimes becoming extensively cracked over most of surface" (usually when old or in dry weather), "rarely not cracking at all", (Frank)
FLESH  yellow, "marbled with creamy white in some areas", reddish to tan near stem base, "slowly to quickly bruising blue" [the illustration in Siegel(2) shows the most intense blue staining in the bottom part of the stem flesh]^, (Siegel), in cap "pale whitish yellow, moderately to strongly bluing when exposed"; in stem "fibrous, pale whitish yellow, with darker yellow to red in base, staining blue when cut", (Frank)
PORES  small and irregularly round at first, "soon moderately large and distinctly angular"; bright yellow to dull yellow, "soon straw colored, often with ocher or reddish brown mouths in age, sometimes distinctly orange-red from start", quickly turning "dark teal to navy blue where bruised"^, (Siegel), "round and more densely packed when young", somewhat angular when old, "pallid yellow at first, soon bright yellow, then dull yellow with orange-brown tones, occasionally orange-red to red, quickly and intensely bruising strongly blue when damaged"; tube layer sunken around stem, (Frank)
STEM  4-10cm x 1-2.5cm, cylindric, "usually straight or slightly curved near base (sometimes squat and irregular)"; yellow when very young, "but soon extensively covered with a dense layer of reddish dots and often evenly washed red almost to top", often with a strong, contrasting band or girdle of red pigment "just below yellow area at extreme apex"^, (Siegel), 4-10cm x 1-2.5cm, cylindric, "equal to clavate"; "extensively red to rose red with red punctation over a yellow ground color", uppermost part of stem often with strongest red hues, "sometimes appearing as a red band near apex", "staining dark blue when handled", (Frank)
ODOR  indistinct^ (Siegel, Frank)
TASTE  mild^ (Siegel, Frank)
EDIBILITY  edible - texture is a bit soft, with an unremarkable flavor, (Siegel)
HABITAT  single, scattered in troops or in small clumps, "most common under live oak in the southern part of our range" [Redwood Coast of California], but found in mixed forest of Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) and Notholithocarpus densiflorus (Tanoak) northward, fruiting in fall and early winter^, (Siegel), single, in small groups or scattered in troops, "typically not clustered", found "in a wide variety of forest types": in the Pacific Northwest with conifers, commonly in California under Quercus agrifolia (California Live Oak) and Notholithocarpus densiflorus, sometimes with Chrysolepis chrysophylla (Golden Chinquapin), (Frank)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Siegel, Frank)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-15 x 4.5-6 um, "elongate, smooth, many with a strongly truncate (squared-off) end"^, (Siegel(2)), spores (9.7)11.7-15.1(16.7) x 4.3-5.4(5.8) um, "subfusoid to subcylindrical, inequilateral", "smooth, inamyloid". "In the majority of collections examined, the spore apex shape varies from truncate to convex, with typically about half the spores truncate". Frank(9). Spores are longer in the holotype: 15-21(27) x 4-5(6) um as measured by Thiers (and Frank(9) confirmed that the holotype has anomalously large spores up to 27 um long and a convex, non-truncate apex). Hymenial cystidia infrequent, 31.6-54.1 x 9.9-13.6 um, cylindric or ventricose with elongated apex; cap cuticle "a trichoderm with elongated cells and erect irregular to clavate to conical or hardly differentiated terminal cells", "pigment brown, coarsely encrusting in lower elements, light brown and parietal in terminal cells"; clamp connections absent.(Frank(9)).
NAME ORIGIN  means 'from Mendocino'
SIMILAR  Other similar species do not have frequent truncate, inamyloid spores. Xerocomellus diffractus (previously known by the misapplied name Boletus chrysenteron) has a less extensively red stem, an often paler cap, and slower blue-staining, (Siegel(2)). Xerocomellus atropurpureus "can appear similar, but has a dark blackish purple to wine red, wrinkled to smooth cap that doesn't become cracked and pores that rarely stain blue", (Siegel). Xerocomellus rainisiae "has a thick velvety-tomentose cap skin that only cracks in age", has a golden yellow stem with a reddish blush at the base, "and stains intensely dark blue-green when handled" (especially on the stem), (Siegel(2)). Xerocomellus zelleri is somewhat similar but it is usually darker in color with a pale marginal band, and pores rarely stain blue. Xerocomellus amylosporus has spores with a truncate apex, but they are at least weakly amyloid (Frank), its stem often develops brown or dingy colors, and its blue-staining is not as fast or intense as in X. mendocinensis (N. Siegel, pers. comm.). See also SIMILAR section of Xerocomellus amylosporus and Hortiboletus rubellus.
SOURCES  Siegel(2)*, Frank(9)*, Smith(35) (as Boletus truncatus), Both(1) (as Boletus truncatus), Thiers(1) (as Boletus truncatus), Desjardin(6)*
94
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus rainisiae  (Bessette & O.K. Mill.) N. Siegel, C.F. Schwarz & J.L. Frank^ [as 'rainisii']  Index Fungorum 179: 1. 2014;  Boletus rainisiae  Bessette & O.K. Mill.  [as 'rainisii'], in Bessette, Roody & Bessette, North American Boletes, A Color Guide to the Fleshy Pored Mushrooms (Syracuse): 145. 2000;  Cyanoboletus rainisiae  (Bessette & O.K. Mill.) Gelardi, Vizzini & Simonini [as 'rainisii'], in Vizzini  Index Fungorum 176: 1. 2014 (proposed new name);  Boletus pulverulentus  Opat.  Wiegm. Arch. Nat. 2: 27. 1836 (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark olive or olive-brown to blackish brown, velvety-tomentose cap that becomes paler olive-brown to yellowish brown at maturity and develops conspicuous cracks with yellow flesh showing, 2) yellow flesh, base flesh with dull red tints, 3) small, pale yellow to yellow-olive pores, often developing a reddish blush, 4) a stem that is club-shaped to equal, bald, smooth, and yellow, often with reddish longitudinal streaks or reddish blotches, 5) growth under conifers, 6) microscopic characters, and 7) green to blue staining reactions of all parts.^ | The staining reactions are described this way: for cap, instantly staining greenish black (or more blue) when handled or bruised; for flesh and tubes, instantly turning blue when exposed or cut; for pores, instantly staining dark green or dark blue-green when bruised, then slowly brownish; for stem, instantly staining dark green or dark blue-green to dark teal-blue, then greenish black when handled or bruised. The greenish tint described for the staining on cap and stem can in fact appear more blue - the exact color can be affected by moisture and age. Alan Bessette subsequently noted that the observations used for the descriptions were made on rather wet specimens (Alan Bessette, pers. comm.). RANGE ^Distribution is from Vancouver Island in BC south into OR; collections were examined from WA and OR, (Frank(9)). Boletus pulverulentus was reported from at least Oregon prior to the description of X. rainisiae but the reports may represent X. rainisiae.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  4-12cm across, "rounded to convex when young, becoming broadly convex" to nearly flat, "margin downturned, becoming even", occasionally uplifted when old; "dark olive, olive-brown to blackish brown when young, becoming paler olive-brown, olive gray to yellowish brown" with yellowish showing in cracks when old, "instantly staining greenish black when damaged"; "dry, velvety to velvety-tomentose when young, more appressed-tomentose and conspicuously areolate" when old^, (Frank), 4-11.5cm, convex to nearly flat, margin incurved at first, with narrow band of sterile tissue; "dark olive-brown to blackish brown when young, becoming paler olive-brown to yellowish brown at maturity, instantly staining greenish black when handled or bruised"; "dry, velvety-tomentose to appressed-tomentose", becoming conspicuously cracked, with yellow flesh showing between cracks when old, (Bessette(3))
FLESH  "in pileus moderately thick, firm, yellow with red in stipe base and around larva tunnels, staining blue when cut"^, (Frank), yellow, instantly turning blue when exposed, stem flesh yellow with dull red tints at base, also instantly turning blue when exposed, (Bessette(3))
PORES  2-3 per mm, "round to angular, yellow to golden yellow when young becoming dark golden yellow to yellow-olive, and often developing a reddish blush" when old, "quickly staining dark blue-green when bruised, then slowly brownish"; tube layer "broadly attached or with a narrow notch" at stem, 0.5-1.5cm long^, (Frank), 1-3 per mm, angular to irregular; "pale yellow, becoming darker dull golden yellow with brownish to reddish tints in age, instantly staining dark green, then slowly brownish when bruised"; tube layer 0.5-1.2cm thick, colored as pore surface, instantly turning blue when exposed, (Bessette(3))
STEM  3-9cm x 1-3.5cm, widened in lower part when young, becoming more equal when old, "with a pinched base"; "bright yellow to golden yellow", often tinged with reddish longitudinal streaks, "and red blotches near base", "staining dark blue-green to dark teal-blue to greenish black when handled", white mycelium at base; stem dry, smooth^, (Frank), 3-9cm x 1.5-3.5cm, club-shaped at first, widening downward or sometimes nearly equal when mature, with pinched base, solid; "bright yellow with rusty red tinges near the base, instantly staining dark green, then greenish black when handled or bruised"; dry, bald without reticulum or longitudinal ridges; partial veil and ring absent; basal mycelium white, (Bessette(3))
ODOR  indistinct^, (Frank), not distinctive (Bessette(3))
TASTE  indistinct^, (Frank), not distinctive (Bessette(3))
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette(3))
HABITAT  single, "scattered in troops or small clusters, in conifer forests, both along the coast and in the foothills and drier eastern slopes of the Cascade Range"; typical sites forested with mixed conifers including Picea sitchensis (Sitka spruce), Abies grandis (Grand Fir), Abies concolor (White Fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), "along coast from mid to late fall into winter, typically later than other Xerocomellus, and at higher elevations in summer and fall"^, (Frank with Latin names italicized), scattered or in groups under mixed conifers, including Douglas-fir and Western Hemlock, October to December, (Bessette(3))
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown (Frank, Bessette(3))
MICROSCOPIC  spores (11.4)13.3-16.4(18.5) x 5-6.5(7.1) um, subelliptic to subcylindric, "inequilateral in side view, smooth, inamyloid"^, basidia 4-spored, 24.6-38.3 x 9.5-13.6 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered, 39.9-68.1 x 9-11.1 um, with 2.8-4.7 um wide necks, "ventricose rostrate to fusoid ventricose"; cap cuticle "a trichoderm with narrowly clavate to fusiform terminal cells, wider than underlying cells", "pigment brown, coarsely encrusting in lower elements, hardly present in terminal cells"; clamp connections absent, (Frank), spores 10-17 x 4.2-7 um, ventricose to elliptic in face view, inequilateral and narrowly elliptic in side view, smooth, ochraceous in KOH or Melzer's; basidia 4-spored, 37-55 x 9-14 um, narrowly clavate, thin-walled, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered, 35-58 x 8-12 um, ventricose-rostrate to fusoid-ventricose, smooth, thin-walled, colorless with ochraceous content in KOH; cap cuticle a trichodermium with narrowly clavate to fusiform end cells; clamp connections absent, (Bessette(3))
NAME ORIGIN  in honor of Arleen Rainis Bessette who first collected it
SIMILAR  X. rainisiae can be distinguished from Xerocomellus diffractus and Xerocomellus mendocinensis by its thick velvety cap cuticle, "the deep greenish black to bluish black staining" on the lower stem, and "the lack of extensive red pigmentation or red punctations" on the stem, (Frank(9)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus rainisii), Frank(9)*, Bessette(1)* (photograph as Boletus pulverulentus)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus salicicola  C.F. Schwarz, N. Siegel & J.L. Frank^  in J.L. Frank, N. Siegel, C.F. Schwarz, B. Araki, E.C. Vellinga, Fungal Systematics and Evolution 6: 283. 2020
ENGLISH NAME(S)  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dull red, tan to brown cap that becomes extensively cracked-areolate, with pinkish yellow tones beneath, 2) light yellow flesh that bruises blue, 3) relatively large yellow pores that bruise dark blue fairly quickly, 4) a stem that is reddish overall, or yellowish with red streaks and a reddish band near the top, the upper part reticulate, 5) growth usually under willow, but also cottonwood and birch, and 6) microscopic characters including relatively small spores and clavate to ventricose hymenial cystidia.^ | The description is derived from Frank(9). RANGE ^Xerocomellus salicola is known to occur from San Diego County, CA north into OR; collections were examined from CA including the holotype, (Frank(9)). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^"KOH turning the tissues yellow"
CAP  3-10cm across, bun-shaped, becoming convex to flat; "dull red, tan to brown"; "dry, slightly tomentose or suede-like when young", "surface soon extensively cracked, in age this areolate pattern extending over entire surface with many brown plaques revealing pinkish yellow tones between"
FLESH  in cap "fleshy, light yellow"; in stem fibrous, "light creamy yellow, bruising blue throughout"
PORES  "pores relatively large at maturity, slightly angular or irregular", "pale yellow to dingy yellow or lemon yellow, bruising dark dingy blue fairly quickly and strongly"; tube layer slightly sunken around stem
STEM  3-5cm x 1-4cm, solid, cylindric, "or with a pinched and tapered base"; "reddish overall or yellowish with red streaks and a reddish band near the apex"; upper part "coarsely reticulate, sometimes becoming diffuse and obscure"
ODOR  not distinctive
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  in small groups of 1-4 fruitbodies, "sometimes clustered, often from wet, muddy or hard-packed soil in riparian areas", typically associated with willow species, and in one case with Populus trichocarpa (Black Cottonwood), occasionally in landscaped areas under Betula pendula (Silver Birch); "in spring, summer, and fall"
SPORE DEPOSIT  "dull olive brown"
MICROSCOPIC  spores (10.5)12-14.2(14.5) x 4.6-6 um, "subellipsoid to subfusoid"^; basidia 4-spored, 27.3-37.1 x 10.2-12.3 um, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered, 32.8-46.1 x 11.8-14.5 um, "clavate or ventricose with acute to obtuse apex"; cap cuticle a trichoderm made up of cylindric to ellipsoid cells up to 15 um wide, with conic or cylindric terminal cells, "pigment brown, incrusting, but terminal cell walls often colorless"
NAME ORIGIN  from genus Salix (willow) and -cola, Latin for "inhabitant of", "referring to the dominant tree in the habitat where it is most commonly encountered"
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus diffractus has slightly larger spores, and a different habitat preference (conifers and hardwoods), (Frank(9)). Xerocomellus dryophilus could be confused if faded, but usually has a more evenly pinkish red cap, a two-toned stem, with a yellow upper half and red base, and slightly larger spores, (Frank(9)). Additionally, the occasionally coarsely reticulate, and reddish upper stipe of X. salicicola helps to distinguish it from X. dryophilus and X. diffractus, (Frank(9) with Latin names italicized).
SOURCES  Frank(9)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAME(S)  Xerocomellus zelleri  (Murrill) Klofac^  Ost. Z. Pilzk. 20: 39. 2011;  Boletus zelleri  (Murrill) Murrill  Mycologia 4: 217. 1912;  Xerocomus zelleri  (Murrill) Snell
ENGLISH NAME(S)  Zeller's bolete
NOTES  Features of Xerocomellus zelleri include 1) a dark vinaceous black, occasionally dark olivaceous black or dark brownish black cap with a paler band around the extreme margin, the surface finely velvety when young, 2) light creamy yellow flesh that sometimes turns slightly blue, 3) moderately sized, round to irregular yellowish pores that occasionally turn blue, 4) a stem with a yellow background that has red punctations at first and becomes more evenly red in its upper part or throughout, 5) growth on or around moss-covered logs in conifer forest, and 6) microscopic characters.^ | Xerocomellus atropurpureus was been included with the species concept of X. zelleri before the description of X. atropurpureus in 2020. The included images specifically represent Xerocomellus zelleri as opposed to X. atropurpureus. Differentiating features between the two are given in the SIMILAR section of Xerocomellus atropurpureus. X. atropurpureus is more common than X. zelleri. Frank(9) say (with Latin names italicized), "Locally common in mature forests on the Olympic Peninsula in Washington, rare across the rest of its range. Despite extensive collecting over a six-year period in [sic], we encountered this species only five times. We studied all observations of X. zelleri on Mushroom Observer (mushroomobserver.org) and concluded that all but one observation from California represent X. atropurpureus." RANGE ^Xerocomellus zelleri ranges from southern BC to the northern California coast (Frank). Collections were examined from WA, OR, and CA, with additional GenBank data from BC, (Frank).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^
CAP  2-6(9)cm across, convex to flat, occasionally uplifted and wavy when old; dark vinaceous black, "occasionally dark olivaceous black to brownish black, rarely deep reddish black", "at all times with a paler, whitish beige to yellowish tan band around the margin", "smooth or finely roughened, wrinkled or pitted, finely, densely velvety when young, becoming more matted in age"^, (Siegel), 2-6(9)cm across, convex to flat, occasionally uplifted and wavy when old; "dark vinaceous black, occasionally dark olivaceous black to brownish black or olive brown to gray, rarely deep reddish black, at all times with a paler, whitish beige to yellowish tan band around the extreme margin"; "smooth or finely roughened, wrinkled or pitted, finely, densely velvety when young", becoming matted to bald when old, (Frank)

PORES  small, "round to slightly angular or irregular"; pale creamy yellow to dingy yellow "or pale yellowish olive when young, becoming dingy yellow to dingy yellow olive, occasionally developing reddish blushes in age", not bruising blue, or occasionally doing so in older, waterlogged specimens; tubes "short to moderately long", sunken around stem to slightly decurrent^, (Siegel), 2-4 per mm, "round to slightly angular or irregular", "pale creamy yellow to dingy yellow, or pale yellowish olive when young, becoming dingy yellow to dingy yellow olive, occasionally developing reddish blushes" when old, "not bruising blue, or occasionally erratically so in older, waterlogged specimens"; tube layer sunken around stem to slightly decurrent, (Frank)

ODOR  indistinct^ (Siegel, Frank)
TASTE  mild to lemony^ (Siegel, Frank)
